THE RED RAGE.
THE YEAR OF THE SOUL.
The hollow crystal of my winter dream
and silences, where thought for worship, white,
shimmer'd within the icy mirror-gleam,
vanishes down the flood of broader light.
The royal weft of arduous device
and starv’d with strangest gems, my shadowy pride
and ritual of illusive artifice,
is shed away, leaving the naked side.
No more is set within the secret shrine
a wonder wherein day nor night has part ;
my passing makes the days of earth divine
with the wild splendours of a mortal heart.
A whisper thrills the living fringe of green
on my retreat : tiptoe the silence stands :
the breathless morn waits that her step be seen,
my summer bride, new life from distant lands.
The hidden places of her beauty hold
the savours shed o'er wastes of island air :
and her crown'd body’s wealth of torrid gold
burns dusky in her summer-storm of hair.
Her breasts, in baffling curves, an upward hope,
strain towards the lips pain'd with too eager life :
and the rich noons faint on each lustrous slope
where thunder-hush in the ardent brake is rife.
I cannot tell what god is in her gaze,
such depths of slumbrous magic drown my breath .
but where the charmed shadow clings and stays .
fate cowers before that high disdain of death.
O take me to thy bosoih’s sultry beat,
steep , all my sense in thy long breath of flame,
oppress me with thy summer's heavy heat,
consume all me that wears an uncrown'd name!
burn this my flesh to a clear web of light,
send thy keen airy spirit to search each vein,
that the hard pulse may throb with strong detijjf
o'ermastcring life, and life's divinest pain l
Then, then we twain will seek each farthest tear,
mingled in radiance over cloud and lea,
our joy shall swell the exulting heart of day,
. our love shall tinge the rose of sky and sea :
and we shall know the steep pride of the hills
and the sombre meditation of the wood,
or quench our rage where the red wine-god ■spill,,
o'er glowing rocks the madness of his blood ;
our link'd approach shall flush the water-maid
who dreams her limpid realm with tender eyes :
our noon-tide sleep shall haunt her memory's sha.'.
vexing her dim breast with unwonted sighs :
and where our fiercer joys have thrill'd the earth
shall burst hard, stalks and cruel cups that keep
strong soul of seasons dead to fill the dearth
in lesser lives whose dream is dead with sleep :
and gloriously our summer’s reign shall end ;
in some dark pass that leads into the west,
burnt incense-wise, ov/r blood shall sweetly blend,
exhaled in music from each love-slaih breast ;
some eve whose dragon-dying hides the sky
and holds the hour on its empurpled wings,
while pallid seers proclaim the doom-day nigh
and shuddering nations watch the deaths of kings.
See now the time (O eve of smoky brown t)
the morbid season of my close content ;
drown'd flame, broad swathes of vapour clos '..,
down
round the clear gaze that pierces, vainly pent,
and knows how vain the hero-death that flung
far flame against the craven face of dark—
poor hero-heart the minstrel summer sung,
O brooding hidden over a bitter earkl —
how vain ! did not the hot-strength of the earth
exude in drifts of colour, dwindling
unto dim odour-wafts, a hearted ivorth
the long-defeated tribes to altar bring.
.
The unslaked caravans of vast desire
seeking in furnace-sands some fierier rose
with deadly heart; the red crusades of ire
following some dusky king of mighty woes
unto a nameless fall in distant fight-
such only freedom from the daily mesh
woven by the crafty lord of wrong and right ;
the pride and splendour of rebellious flesh,
full-sated with wild honey of summer's heart,
the golden lot of ignominy that cdst
and craved the honour of a menial part,
to follow on bleeding feet, nor fell the last
how high their pyre blazed and insensate will
that the last word of their red tale, be told !
and o'er their darkening blood, a moment, stilt,
hung, on horizon wings, the spirit’s gold,
the ghost of flame, in the vast crucible
transmuted of some viewless Trismegist—
haply the same whose touch, inaudible,
dissolves the lingering leaf to evening mist.
flow with the lucid flowers in their hands
that gem the pale fields of Thulean spring,
and silver from the moon-made table-lands
of snoiv, the glimmering distance vanishing ;
with, opals that engeal the boreal gleam
and diamond-drip of ether's crystal thrill
miraculous, the corteges of dream,
over the hills of legend gathering, fill
the imaginary a venues of gloom
up to the ivatching ivindoivs that betray
the House of Contemplation, vaulted room
soaring, with shade that broods above pale day ;
pale day that wastes even since morning, drain'd
by ambush’d mystery of its wanton breath . .
see now the time that rises, pale, imstain’d,
the fixed light that charms the fields of death f
A little yet, a little—wait, O files
obedient to my dumb command—the brow
may waive its frigid loneliness, the wiles
of the spent heart becloud it — wait: and thou,
dark Presence, large above the passing world,
biding the full hour of the fated stroke,
ere in the sudden gust of truth be whirl?d
the veils of kindly Maya, leaf or smoke,
let thei r suspense of smouldering glory be
yet mirror''cl in this mind's untroubled pool
or e’er beneath the implacable certainty
of icy light and thought's untarnish'd rude
the vacant world stand rigid ! let me yet
this vesper dream I am not all alone
and ponder with luxurious ref ret
over the singing, golden morning flown :
soon, soon enough the spirit, v/nreproved,
shall on its proud predestin'd circle range
in dread indifferent solitude removed
above the poignant pageantry of change,
and the large brows whose curves are centuries
arise of Isis’ carven front supreme
that bids the lucid soul in stillness freeze :
the glittering crystal of my winter dream.
N.S.W.
C.B.
Education: A Re-Statement.
I~T is my love of, and sympathy with, children
I that compels me to write. Their happiness
is my concern. In the early part of my
ministry I taught them to place their hopes
beyond this world ; now, I teach them to live here
and in the present. Duty done here is the fitting
preparation for there. Tradition says that the
way thither is one of sorrow: Nature, with her
bountiful provision for pure enjoyment, says
otherwise. The future world was for the poor
and afflicted-: this, for the prosperous afflietar.
We do not think so now. This world is a com-
monwealth, entered by birth. There are those
who bitterly deny that mere birth into the world
confers rights ; but why argue the matter ? The
compassion of the perfected man would grant
those rights were they non-existent. Thus, the
child has a right to happiness. Many things
which mar the joy of life, such as transmitted
taints, are outside the scope of this article; here
we have to deal only with that arch-destroyer of
youthful happiness—the present system of edu-
cation.
My views are summed up in the sentence :
Education should be limited by time, and be
exquisite in emotion. That was in my mind when
1 quoted the old grandmother spinning and chant-
ing songs of love and death ; and it is at that
point that I did not make my meaning clear. Let
me now say that I prefer as an educational instru-
ment the grandmother’s story with its environ-
ment of blazing hearth and wide, desolate moor to
the mere statement of “the length of the Amazon.”
The Amazon rejoicing down .its vast, sunlit valley,
with all its import to man, is what I am pleading
for. I want to linger there, to float in its broad
sunshine, to pierce its waving forests, to know its
peoples—in a word, to fall under the spell of’its
romance. I desire with a great yearning to gaze
at the fact until the “Something” behind burns
with light and colour like an opening star, and
I see it and my children see it. But the
examiner practically forbids me to do this ; hence
my cry for the deliverance *of the children out of
the land of Egypt. We are touching on the
subject of Geography, and my meaning will be
made clear by the quoting of four questions from
a recent examination paper (University of Tas-
mania, Junior Geography, 1900) —
1. Draw a map of South America, marking on it the
Amazon, Orinoco and De la Plata Rivers ; Aconcagua,
Cotopaxi, Chimborazo, and Lake Titicaca ; the Straits of
Magellan, and the Caribbean Sea ; and Rio de Janeiro,
Monte Video, Valparaiso, Lima, Pernambuco, George-
town, Quito and La Paz.
2. (a) When it is noon in London it is 6 p.m. in Gal-
utta and 7 a.m. in New York. State approximately the
longitude of Calcutta and of New York.
(of Explain what is meant by climate. What are the
various causes producing the climate of Tasmania ?
(c) On which two days in the year are day and night
equal all over the globe ? Explain this equality.
5. Write a short description of (a) Italy, (b) New Zea-
land, (c) the Transvaal.
6. Write a description of the Pacific Ocean, naming its
coasts, chief inland seas, islands and other characteristics.
There are four more questions. And the time ?
Two hours. You see now what I mean when I
say that the examiner will not allow us to educate
our children. It is true that some examiners state
in private that they do not expect the child to
answer all the questions. Such a defence of these
impossible papers is as wicked as the practice of
setting them. Think of the agony of a sensitive
child during the examination ; the disappointment
after careful preparation ; the anxious waiting for
the result; the grief of failure ; and the cramming
of the following year to reach the standard set by
the questions. “ Curse ye, Meroz, said the angel
of the Lord ; curse ye bitterly.” For the Outlines
of Geography prescribed by the Regulations we are
preparing a text-book of 188 pages, every line of
which is cunningly packed with one or more
facts. How is it possible in the limited time to
make each fact shine? I think the last straw is
the getting-up of the counties of England, Wales,
Scotland, and Ireland. Yet Tasmanian children
must do it, for in the last examination-paper there
is a question on counties. How can we produce a
White ora Jefferies when our children are tor-
tured with such old-world divisions ? Passing on
to the neighbour-subject of History, we find pre-
scribed :
(a) Outlines of English History.
(b) Outlines of Sacred History.
Now, consider the glorious opportunities of
English history-teaching; what living descrip-
tions of places! what tracing the growth of
events ! what noting of character! And then
ponder over the years of English history—you
dare not pause to make the ideas shine. And the
Outlines of Sacred History. How simple the
words, but how vast the task ! There is no text-
book mentioned, so you must face the whole of
the Bible. This use of the Bible has to my know-
ledge inspired life-long hatred of that sublime
book, and, as a minister of religion, I here enter
my protest. The following are some questions set
for juniors :
(7) Name, and give the geographical position of, the
districts ruled over by Og, Sihon, and Balak; and relate
the action of these kings towards the Israelites (1899).
(8) Write a short history of Joshua, Elijah, Athaliah,
Cornelius, and the origin of the feast of Purim (1900).
(9) Give particulars of the rival kingdom set up at
Saul’s death at Mahanaim (1900).
(10) What incidents are described in the New Testa-
ment as having happened at Capernaum and Athens
(1900.)
They are set, you see, to cover the whole range.
Can you answer them ? The children answer
them : but the cost! And they learn as history
parts of the Bible which we know are fabulous
or inaccurate. But the worst feature about the
two papers (Geography and History, 1900) is—
“ With the exception of the question about
climate, Tasmania is not mentioned.” When
shall we learn to be Australians?
These are facts to ponder over.
(1) The examination is open to all over 12
years of age op the first day of December
of the year in which the examination is
held (Regulations).
(2) Nine subjects may be taken —Latin, Greek,
French or German compulsory.
The rivalry of schools and the ambition of parents
compel masters to enter their pupils as young as
possible, and for as many subjects as possible.
The actual working of this examination is a crime
against youth.
(In parenthesis: “Business and professional
men declared that to boys when they entered an
office an examination was of little or no value.
They had to be taught from the beginning.
Parents wanted their children to pass examina-
tions'. . . . For the purposes of daily life, the
examinations were more harmful than useful.”—
Hermann! Ritz, M.A. Lecture at Government
Technical School, Hobart. Hobart Mercury,
August 20, 1901. And read by me just at this
point of my paper.)
A healthy and happy childhood is one of man’s
most precious possessions. In those early days
we live on the mount of vision ; our senses deal
with things with a fresh wonder that never comes
again; and we store up then the most vital
impressions of life. Wander with A. France on
the quays of old Paris; be with Pater the Ohild
in the House ; experience again with R.L.S. the
shy, vague, intuitions of childhood; talk with
Dickens with the queer boy ; shout in the great
Ode—but why quote further ? We who are in the
autumn of life know the value, the wistful beauty
of those years of dawn. That part of time seems
another life lit by the
. gleam,
The light that never was on sea or land.
For me: no bells have the charm of that Pen-
wortham chime which, floating across the river,
disturbed me at my play; no twilight rain has
ever slanted down the world with the mystery of
those passages in the evenings of long ago. God’s
pity be on the man whose life begins in misery !
Youth is a sacred time of preparation for the life
of the soul—not fqr the office-bench. A man’s
true business in life is with the development of
the soul. The labour of gaining a living is sent to
prevent a too great leaning to mysticism—beyond
that it is a danger and a harm. The fatigue of
play, and a something of the animal in the child,
make it safe to lead him into the royalties to
which he is born.
Does the present system of education guard,
open, stimulate, and satisfy all that is best in the
child ? No, again ! Children leave school after
years of cramming, unfit and uneducated in the
true sense. They are crammed with a vast
number of unassimilated facts, but they are not
educated. The health suffers even under the best
conditions: the scholars come back from the holi-
days bright and rosy, and gradually grow pale
and dull, especially the girls. Any system which
pales the cheek, dims the eye, and starves the
imagination is self-condemned. Even as the
plant’s food must be soluble in water before
absorption, so the food of the mind and the heart
must be rendered soluble by curiosity and imagi-
nation on the part of teacher and taught. The
plant would starve in the midst of food in the
dormant condition : the child starves in a plenty
of food which is an intrusion, not a living part
of his organism. No farmer would thrust the
dormant reserve into his plants, yet he allow*
others to work a greater mischief with the delicate,
mechanism of his child’s brain. My dream of
youth is such that grey-haired men should be able,
softly and tenderly, to live again the beautiful
time in gentle recalling of its happy days. As .we
grow older, and approach nearer to the inevitable
grave, we prize more and more the memories of
those years spent under so wild and romantic a
light.
A new system of education that shall be accept-
able and universal is beyond the powers of any
man : he can only put before bis constituents, with
an engaging air, the bit of truth he has won with
his life-blood. He must not hope for more than
the official smile. The official smile—the in-
credulous eye and the curving lip ! Who that is
in earnest does not know it? And the more
ignorant the owner, the broader the grin. I won-
der if anyone has shouted “pom-pom” into the
British War Office. No, we must not hope for
much in this world ; rather let us pray for help to
receive another man’s “ truth ” with sympathy.
There is against the reformer of education the
weight of the academical world. Professors and
say, in effect: “ The system made me ;
therefore it must be right.” Reform shuts out all
clumsy methods of examination ; the tests of the
new education are delicate. Deep must call unto
deep. Many will laugh at this—not those who
have spent their days with children. Attempt to
utter that which does not ring true and they will
detect it at once. They watch you with keen un-
preoccupied eyes, and read your soul.
I can fully understand Le Gay Brereton’s
revolt. It has been my-attitude for years. Some
day I shall announce to my little flock my inten-
tion to share no more in the tyranny that blights
their happiness. (I hope to put forth m.y system
of education in another paper.) It will wreck my
school, but save my (teaching) soul alive. As it
is, I have lost boys by refusing parents’ requests
to fill in every minute of their children’s lives
between school and bed with home-work. They
all plead the certificate —their children will be
nothing in life without the certificate. When I
think of the certificate and its ruinous effect on
youth, I envy the ancient prophets the vigour and
resource of their language.
And now to the desired end. The fqrmation of
a high, almost impossible, educational ideal, to
be realised in the years, will help to bring the
golden time about. The Sermon on the Mount
may be impraoticable in individual and in State
life ; but, nevertheless, it has, by its divine love,
helped Civilisation along her thorny path.
Tasmania.
JAMES HEBBLETHWATTE.
Ethel Turner’s The Story of a Baby (1895) and
The Little Duchess and Other Stories (1896) are
newly reprinted in one volume, with the addition
of three sketches not previously seen in book-
form. The publishers (Ward, Lock, and Co.,
Ltd.) follow a detestable practice in omitting the
date of publication; and there is nothing to show
that the matter is reprinted;—theideabeing, appar-
ently, to leave unwary purchasers in ignorance that,
in one form or another, the work has been before
the public for several years. The passage in The
Story of a Baby which paraphrases Swinburne, in
a fashion here complained of when the book was
originally published, is still perpetuated—thus:
IN A GARDEN.
(Poems and Ballads, Hi,
83-U.)
Baby, see the flowers i
—Baby sees
Fairer things than these,
Fairer though they be than
dreams of ours. . . .
Baby, see the moon !
—Baby’s eyes
Laugh to watch it rise,
Answering light with love
and night with mOon. . .
Baby, see the star!
—Baby’s hand
Opens, warm and bland,
Oalm in claim of all things
fair that are..
Baby, hear the bells !
—Baby’s head
Bows, as ripe for bed.
Now the flowers curl round
and close their ce115....
Baby, flower of light,
Sleep, and see
Brighter dreams than we,
Till good day shall smile
away good night.
THE STORY OF A
BABY.
(New edition, SIS.)
Such wonderful, wonder-
ing eyes it had, intensely
blue, intensely earnest. . . .
Did you show it a beauti-
ful flower or a low-hanging
silver moon, a picture,
something bright with
colour, it seemed to be
looking away far beyond
them and smiling in a faint
sweet way, because it saw
fairer things than ever you
dreamed of.
The hands . . . were like
the inside of a flushed,
curled rose-leaf, and when
they closed round your
finger, you felt how
strangely sweet and soft
and warm they were.
From the long open
window came the: Sound of
Dot’s voice, singing. The
baby was listening as it lay
in the hammock
Seeing that Ethel Turner’s readers are certainly
not, as a class, readers of Swinburne, this persistent
reproduction in prose of Swinburne’s poem, as
“By Ethel Turner,” is both discreditable and
surprising.-

WOMEN AND
WARNER’S
SAFE CURE.
The health of women is of supreme im-
portance. The daughter of to-day becomes
the wife of to-morrow, and it is abso-
lutely essential to the welfare of the race
that good health should be hers; good
health which brings happiness and en-
sures vigour in posterity. A woman’s
nervous system is very highly strung.
Her mechanism is far more delicate and
complex than that of a man, and there
is little to wonder at when it breaks down
under the continuous high pressure of
modern life.
Most women require some aid to enable
them to support the daily stress and
strain, and this aid is found in WARNER’S
SAFE CORE —a medicine wonderfully
adapted to their delicate organisation-
which has for twenty years secured the
grateful endorsement of women in all
walks of life in all parts of the civilised
world.

cough of influenza; and for whooping
cough and croupy coughs of children. To

it is an especial boon. The cough and
breathing are made easier, the irritation in
throat and bronchial tubes is soothed, the
character of the expectoration is altered,
and a general improvement effected in all
the symptoms. We have had hundreds of
reports from all parts of the English-
speaking World attesting its remarkable
power to relieve and cure troublesome
coughs.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Under the Magnolias.
He (a Poet) :
Better this scented darkness than the glare
Within the ball-room.
She :
It is better air.
He :
This soft magnificence and splendour deep
Of midnight walking on the shores of sleep,
These flowers that blush upon the dark green trees
And keep the moonlight in their chalices—
Ay me, when Cleopatra reigned of old
There were such midnights !
She :
It is getting cold.
He:
Cold ! There is no such word as cold for me—
My burning heart would thaw the Polar Sea.
Had I the universe at my command
I’d give it gladly
She (softly) :
James, you have my hand.
He:
Your forehead is as white as virgin snow ;
Your skin is pink and white like Marvell’s doe ;
Your fair translucent skin,
Lilies without, roses within.
Beneath this moon you are a dream divine,
A nymph of Greece—a Lorely of the Rhine.
Your eyes are set to music—two such eyes
I saw beside the gates of Paradise
When Eve went weeping after Adam fell, —
The bright and tender eyes of Israfel.
Your lips are—
She (with a yawn) :
Yes, dear —
He :
Like the scarlet flower
Of green hibiscus in its passionate hour,
When, crowned and wreathed with it in Southern
Isles,
The sleek brown Circes lure men with their smiles.
(Musingly):
From Yap to Islands far below the Line,
The whole brown world of women it was mine.
O fair and lovely isles! —
She :
And did you dare ? . . .
He:
The night of Araby is in your hair,
You are the harvest that the Heroes reap
She :
Oh, dear ! poor James is talking in his sleep.
Mother!
The Mother :
Yes, Ada.
She :
Sit beside my lad ;
I think he’s sober, but I’m sure he’s mad.
The Mother :
And you were saying, Mr. Dooley, then
He (not noticing the difference and talking with
closed eyes) :
Yes, I was saying One sweet kiss again !
( Chanting):
Sweeter by far you are to me
Than nightingales in Italy :
The blackbird singing in the sedge,
The brown thrush in the hawthorn "hedge,
The linnet in the greenwood glooms,
The goldfinch in the apple blooms,
The bird of Joy, the seraph lark
Singing at daum above the dark ;
They were . . .
(trailing off)
The Mother (nestling closer and beginning to
enjoy the situation)
They were, dear!
He (kindling again)
Singing prophets they
Of what should come to me in my good day.
Ada, the night is golden ; we shall go
The Mother :
But I am Ada’s mother : don’t you know ?
He (magnificently):
Mother or Daughter matters not to me,
You are the Keeper of the Rainbow Key.
We go into the distance darkly blue
To find Romance. Arise !
(Both arise. The Mother kisses her hand towards
the house.)
“ Ada, adieu ! ”
V.J.D.
One Awake in the Grave.
Sleep—might I sleep ! but I must wake and listen
Oh, weary waiting, icy-cold and dumb :
In the long grass where rain-drops cling and
glisten,
When morning breaks, at last her step may come.
Does she live on, my Love I love for ever,
Has she a thought to send beneath the grave ?
Would she bring back the life we lived together,
Heal with her tears the mortal wound she gave ?
She does not come. For I would hear her weeping,
Feel her light footstep tremble through the earth.
While here I wake, a hopeless vigil keeping,
What does she count my broken slumber worth ?
Sleep—l must sleep—but still I wake and listen
Oh, weary waiting, icy-cold and dumb. . .
Through the long grass where rain-drops cling
and glisten,
When evening glows, at last her step may come.

expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 7, 1901.
Peacock the Apostate.
As one of the inevitable results of Federa-
tion, State Premier Peacock, of Victoria,
sees a deficit ahead. All the States are in
the same situation, and it is nothing to the
discredit of the Federal principle that they
should be so. The Commonwealth tariff is
nearly due, and, when it begins to operate,
border customs will be abolished, and a
large revenue will disappear. Nobody will
lose anything by its disappearance. The
money will be in the pockets of the people
instead of in the Treasury ; it only remains
for the authorities to find some new way of
taking the same amount of money out of the
pockets of the people, and all will be well.
The general idea among Protectionists is
that the seller pays the duty, and among
Freetraders that it is paid by the buyer.
In the present case, it matters nothing
which party pays it. As regards inter-
state commerce, the seller and buyer are
both Australians, and inter-State duties are
paid by Australians either way. Therefore, by
the abolition of these duties the Australians
of every State cease to pay a large amount
of customary taxation, and the natural con-
sequence is that the Treasurers find them-
selves on the rocks, just as they would if any
other large tax was abolished. They couldn't
fairly expect to be in any other place.
* * * *
And here the good old Australian prin-
ciple of Shirk comes out strongly. In every
State the people will, this year, be relieved
of a heavy tax, and the natural course is to
put on an equal tax of some other kind and
make things balance. But not one Treasurer
lias yet ventured to definitely suggest such a
thing. In every State there are pathetic
remarks about the cruelty of “laying extra
burdens on the people,” and the fact
that they wouldn’t be extra is unanim-
ously suppressed. The various ways of deal-
ing with the case that have been proposed
up to date are to borrow more money and
pay the shortage out of that ; or to rob
some poor person, or cut dov\ n some badly-
paid person’s salary ; or else to do nothing
at all and just let things happen till further
notice. All the States except one seem in-
clined just to let things happen, or else to
borrow some more money. Premier Pea-
cock, of Victoria, is the only political leader
up to now who has had, even in a small
degree, the courage of his own meanness, or
of his own convictions, or of his own
cowardice, and who has come forward with a
definite scheme for robbing the poorest class
of the community in order that the richer
portion of it may pay less taxation than
heretofore.
* * * 9
Peacock’s brilliant idea is to cut down
old age pensions from the present maximum
of 10s. to a maximum of 7s. per week. He
also proposes to have a rigid overhaul of all
pensioners, and to take away the pension in
every case where any semblance of an excuse
can be found for so doing. By this unutter-
able economy Peacock hopes to save £44,000
towards an anticipated deficit of £229,000.
This is the only measure he has yet laid
before Parliament ; the other tT85,000 of
the deficit is just to happen until further
notice. The poorest and most helpless class
of the community—also the class about
whose sufferings, and whose deserving charac-
ter, and whose bygone services in building up
the State Peacock and co. have wept most
piteously—is the first one, and, so far, the
only one, which is required to contribute a
copper. Whether the buyer or the seller
pays the customs tax—whether Victoria pays
on its border exports or its border imports—
the Victorian people will save, and the
Victorian revenue will lose, probably some-
thing like £250,000 per annum. It is
impossible to say accurately what the
reduction will be, but it is likely that
the Federal tariff will yield just about
as much as the present Victorian tariff
does on oversea imports, and the abolition
of border duties will be so much saved.
Victoria is a lightly-taxed State, even with-
out this saving, and has no need to resort to
any specially mean devices in order to reduce
its burdens. The average direct and in-
direct taxation all over the Commonwealth
is £2 14s. per inhabitant annually ; the
average for the Commonwealth and Maori-
land is £2 17s. 9d. ; the Victorian rate is
only £2 10s. 7d. Out of the seven States of
Australasia there are only two where taxa-
tion is lower than in Victoria, and both
these States keep their taxation lower for
the time being principally by charging a
huge amount of their ordinary expenditure
to loans. It it only necessary to retain the
taxation at its present level and (providing
there are no more Boer wars, and Boer wars
don’t happen every day) Victoria can pay
old age pensions on the present basis, and
meet all its other expenditure, and still have
a good amount over with which to reduce
the fine old deficit.
* * * *
Whether it is common courage or common
humanity that Peacock lacks, it is difficult
to say. It would be necessary to fight the
House of 48 Rich Landlords on the question
of imposing a new tax in place of the one that
is being abolished, and the House of Land-
lords is a body that would rather sacrifice
anybody, however poor and aged and deserv-
ing, than lose a chance to reduce its own
very moderate burdens. It consists largely
of fat and Tory Freetraders, and though these
persons are shortly going to be blessed with
the largest measure of Freetrade to be found
in any part of the world, save, perhaps,
Canada and Russia—the total abolition of all
customs duties over an area of 2,972,906
square miles—they are not the kind of men
who will take that blessing gratis if they can
so arrange things as to be paid for taking it.
Still, it is impossible to say for certain, until
the experiment is tried, that the House of
Landlords would definitely refuse to vote an
extra land-tax to make good the loss. Premier
Peacock might at least have had the credit
of making the experiment before he began
to oppress the poor and take the crust out of
the mouth of the aged—ail for £44,000 a
year.
• * * *
Victoria’s old age pensions, on the present
scale, seem likely to absorb £300,000 a year.
No sane individual, considering the experi-
ence of Maoriland with two-thirds the popu-
lation, expected the amount to be any less,
and if Peacock expected it to be less he was a
fool. The State also pays £340,000 a year
in Civil Service pensions. It can’t econo-
mise on the latter item without breaking
faith with its pensioners. It can’t economise
on the former without at least indirectly
breaking faith, and without cruelly and
heartlessly taking back a portion of the very
scanty measure of justice it recently gave
with such a fanfare of trumpets to its old
and deserving citizens. It granted the pre-
sent pensions well knowing that the loss of
its border customs revenue was close at
hand, therefore the aged recipients had every
right to suppose that it saw its way to pay
them. As for the proposed 7s. a week, that
sum, as rents and prices go in Australia, is
the most abject mockery of a means of sup-
port that could well be conceived. To the
old inhabitant, who depends on it
as his entire means of support, it
isn’t so much a living as a prolongation of
the misery of dying. It is slow starvation—
and some people would almost prefer to have
their starvation done promptly. It isn’t
enough to keep the aged recipient from
being sent to gaol by Peacock’s awful magis-
trates and J’s.P. for “ insufficient lawful
menus of support.” Some of these strange and
hog-like characters have already been on the
verge of sending to gaol under the Vagrancy
Act pensioners with 10s. a week, on the
ground that the sum which Peacock says is
too much is really not enough. It is less than
a year since the Government of which Pea-
cock was a member granted this scanty
boon, and said proudly that in handing out
10s. a week to the respectable aged inhabit-
ant who had no other means whatever, it
merely rendered bare justice to a class
which had deserved well of the country. By
its own showing it now proposes to do gross
injustice—for the s&ke of £44,000 a year.
It isn’t much as the price of Peacock’s
honor, and the honor of Victoria. No
Government ever arranged to do a
shamefully mean action for a smaller
inducement. The amount won’t even
cover the anticipated deficit ; it won’t cover
quite one-fifth of it. The necessity for hunt-
ing for new sources of revenue won’t be
much less after this miserable breach of
faith than it was before, and the amount of
suffering it will inflict is appalling. The
world has a certain regard for the man
who does bad things, or even mean things,
on a large scale ; for the man who does mean
things on a petty scale it has nothing but
contempt. If all Australia knew the whole
record of all its present political leaders, and
a poll was taken to-day to elect the meanest
boss politician in Australia, Alexander
Peacock would have a good show of coming
out on top.
* * * *
If Victoria’s House of Landlords has any
gift of foresight—a thing of which it has not
hitherto been suspected, by the police or any
other authority—it will interfere with Pea-
cock’s foolish and incompetent device. The
Commonwealth Parliament is already half
inclined to force the Barton Government to
take up the Old Age Pensions question as a
Federal matter. If Peacock’s backward
movement is carried out it will probably be
a great deal more than half inclined. If it
does take the matter up, it will probably fix
the maximum pension at something more
than 10s. a week instead of less. It will
certainly grant far more pensions ; an all-
Australian system would apply to any person
who had lived a certain number of years in
the Commonwealth territory (not in any one
State), and a large number of nomads who
are now disqualified because they spent their
lives roving from State to State would come
under its provisions. And the Federal
Government would almost certainly have to
impose a land-tax to raise the needful funds
—a very different kind of land-tax from the
small, easy-going apology for an impost that
the Victorian now pays. So long as the
land-tax is regarded as a purely State impost
the House of Landlords can veto any attempt
to increase or extend it. When the Com-
monwealth Parliament takes to imposing
such a tax things will be very different. The
Commonwealth land-tax will come just as
soon as the Commonwealth Old Age Pension
does, and Peacock is helping that along
gaily. If the Upper House doesn’t realise
this, and doesn’t know when to climb down
in self-defence, it will have a long way to
fall one of these days.
The Man on the Land.
Within the last six months the cultivated area of
New South Wales has again begun to diminish,
and lavish loan expenditure is one of the probable
causes. In 1899, when the loan expenditure was
under lg millions, the area of land in cultivation
increased largely. In ’9O and ’9l loan expenditure
more than doubled, and the cultivated area
decreased. Dibbs cut the loan business down to
a very great extent, and a huge agricultural
expansion—the biggest N.S.W. ever saw-set in.
But after a while loans grew larger again, and the
expansion of agriculture became slower, till with
the great See-O’Sullivan loan-boom it shows the
first signs of being once more on the down grade.
The explanation is simple enough. The agri-
culturist is mostly fitted for hard labor at
Government works, and very few of him
would go on the land at the miserable pittance
which can be earned there while a job at
railway or bridge-building, or sewerage, or road-
making, or other such work is procurable. Also,
many who are on the land are ready, for good and
sufficient cash reasons, to get off it with all pos-
sible speed when such a chance presents itself.
The reasons in question are numerous enough.
The Bulletin offers a few to go on with :
* • * «
(1) The Borrowing Curse. Nearly half the freights
charged on all produce sent anywhere by rail goes to pay
interest on the debt with which the railways are bur-
dened. If the railways had been built out of the land
revenue, produce could be carried at a much cheaper
rate, and with less of his earnings absorbed in freight
there would be more left for the Man on the Land.
(2) The want of a system of Resumption of Land for
Closer Settlement. The agriculturist is drifting out into
the howling Wilderness, where crops are scanty, and
where the expense of getting them to market per the
mortgaged railway line, so eats up the profits that there
isn’t half as much left for the grower as he could earn
making railways for O’Sullivan. If such institutions
as the Australian Agricultural Co. were moved out of
the way, the agriculturist might get land comparatively
near a market, where freights wouldn’t devour so much
of the produce, and the grower and his employees would
consequently be left a better income.
(3) Centralisation of everything at Sydney. A man
who wants to export may have a port near to him, but all
railways lead to Sydney or Newcastle and nowhere else.
Therefore these are the only places where exporting can
be done. And the freight over a needlessly long railway,
with a huge mortgage on it, eats up the profit.
(4) Freetrade. When the produce gets to Sydney by a
long, long journey (because the Australian Agricultural
Co. and other loafing monopolies prevent the settler being
nearer at hand) at a high freight (because the interest on
the railway mortgage must be paid), it has to compete
against produce imported from cheap labor countries per
a sailing vessel which travels a highway that isn’t mort-
gaged. Consequently the ship hasn’t to charge exorbi-
tant rates to cover interest bills.
(5) Freetrade again. There is very little manufac-
turing population to consume the produce locally, because
the bulk of N.S.W. manufactured goods are imported
under the blessed Freetrade policy. And exporting to
the only available market (England), to feed the people
who make our tweeds and calico goods there, when these
people should be making them here, is a game at which
the agriculturist can only average a few shillings a week.
With poor land, a long haulage over a railway which must
charge high rates to pay the interest on the mortgage,
and then a longer sea journey and more costly sea freight
than almost any of our competitors are burdened with,
how much is left for the Man on the Land?
(6) Lastly, the Borrowing Curse again, for, in addition
to paying needlessly high freights to cover the interest on
the railway debt, there is an immense debt —getting on
for £30,000,0 o—incurred for other purposes, and the
taxation required to pay the interest also falls, more or
less, directly or indirectly, on the producer.
(7) And, by way of postscript, there is the 7 or 8 or 9
per cent, mortgage on top of all.
* * * *
There is absolutely no complete immediate
remedy for t/iese evils. It has taken 30 or 40
years of misgovernment, of cowardly shirking of
responsibility, of letting things slide, of booming
and borrowing and passing on the responsibility,
to_ bring about the present miserable state of
things. If 20 or 30 years of good strong govern-
ment could wholly undo the evil, it would be a
marvellous performance. The Man on the Land
is in a bad way. He only goes on the land by the
compulsion of poverty, in a large proportion of
cases; he remains poor, in most cases, while he
is on the land; and his readiness to get off it again
is pathetic. And, even if O’Sullivan borrowed
.£6,000,000 a year instead of about £3,000,000, and
paid 10s. a day on Government works instead pf
75., it would only make the lot of the Man on the
Land worse in the end The railway that is built
at 7s. a day costs more in borrowed money than
the one built at ss. a day, so there is a bigger mort-
gage on it, and higher rates have to be charged to
make it pay, and the higher the rate the less
remains out of his crop to support the Man on the
Land.
» m ♦ •
But by way of a beginning on the road to a
better state of things here are a few suggestions :
(1) Let the N.S.W. land tax be graduated till on the
biggest estates it runs to 3d. in the ,£. That will pro-
duce more revenue. It will also compel many big mono-
polists to cut up and sell their properties, and give 3ome
men a chance to go on the land nearer to a market.
(2) Establish a vigorous system of resumption of big
estates for closer settlement. That will have the same
result. But don’t resume the big estates with borrowed
money.
(3) Stop borrowing, no matter what it costs in present
taxation and in hard economy. Even if the Governor’s
salary has to be reduced, and the publication of the
“History ofN.S.W. from the Records” has to be suspended
let this be done. Let all new puhlic works be constructed
out of revenue, and make a beginning, however small, at
wiping off the existing debt. Then there will come a
time when the produce of the Man on the Land can be-
carried at half the present rate. If this isn’t done a time
may come when he will have to pay twice the present
rate.
(4) Let the Man on the Land have money on mortgage
at 4£ per cent, per medium of a State Land Bank, instead
of the rate of interest he now pays. But don’t borrow
for this purpose either.
(5) Foster manufacturing industries in every possible
way by a Protective system. Then in time there will be
a large producing local city population to buy the local
agriculturist’s produce—not an unproductive city popula-
tion living largely on loans, and on finance, and on
charging commissions, and on cutting each other’s hair.
The Man on the Land has to exchange his produce for
clothes and the like. If his produce has to pay freight to
London before it can be finally swapped for clothes
because the man who makes the clothes is there, then by
reason of the freight there is less left to buy the clothes
with than there would be if the man who made them was
close at hand.
(6) And let us have a weighty, not to say enormous,
absentee-tax as a strong inducement to the man who has
money invested in Australia to live here, and spend his
money locally. If he doesn’t live here the proceeds of
the tax will help just a little to make borrowing unneces-
sary and to ease the burden of the Man on the Land.
(7) Also, let us hang O’Sullivan as the great borrowing
enthusiast of the N.S.W. Ministry, and the man whose
accumulated interest bill will shortly make the lot of the
Man on the Land even worse than it is now.
There are other things—irrigation, road-making,
locking of rivers, &c. —which want to be done for
the Man on the Land, but any Government which
adopts the above ideas will at least have made a
good beginning at alleviating his hard lot.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
IThe Minimum Wage v. The Maximum
Dividend.
William M‘Millan, of N.S.W., the represen-
tative of an absentee firm of importers which has
just declared an 11 per cent, dividend, made to a
great extent out of the Australian people, con-
tributes a bitterly indignant article to the Review
op Reviews about the evils of the Victorian Mini-
mum Wage law. The M‘Billion belongs to a
class which mostly talks as if it was in business for
the sake of a great moral principle, and the fact
that it declares 11 per cent, dividends for its
absentee shareholders is kept very much in the
background. No one who has heard the brassy
M‘Billion dilate on the crushing monopoly of
protected manufacturers (whose dividends, in Vic-
toria, average about 8 per cent.), would
imagine for a moment that his crowd of
absentees paid 11 per cent, (some people
in the same importing business pay as much
as 17 per cent.), and that he was really howling
against local trade rivals who cut into the profits
of the great M ‘Arthur and Co., of Loudon,
absentee softgoodsmen. M ‘Billion calls the
abolition of sweating by legal enactment “The
Defeat of Liberty.” So it is. It abolishes the
liberty of great softgoods firms to have clothing
made at ll|d. a day by sewing-women of the
kind that Hood described when he wrote :
With fingers weary and worn,
With eyelids heavy and red,
A woman sat in unwomanly rags
Plying needle and thread.
• • » • '» ;
Stitch ! stitch ! stitch !
In poverty, hunger and dirt.
And still with a voice of dolorous pitch
(O, would that its tones could reach the rich !)
She sang this Song of the Shirt.
That is how things were, and how they still are in
London, where M ‘Arthur and Co. hang out and
pay 11 per cent. It is also, judging by the
awful revelations made before a commission of
inquiry a few week ago about pants made at
4d. a pair, and similar horrors, how they are in
Sydney—where M ‘Arthur and Co. also hang out.-
The Minimum Wage Law abolished, so far as Vic-
toria was concerned, the liberty of any softgoods
firm or person to run things on such principles.
It also abolished the liberty of any woman to be
ground down to these awful depths of degrada-
tion. So it was, as M‘Millan says, the “ Defeat
of Liberty ” beyond a doubt. It was just as much
a defeat of liberty as when, in England many
years ago, the Protestant was deprived of the
right to be burnt, and his liberty to be a martyr
was taken from him. Of course it is impossible to
pay Is. for making a pair of pants here—a wage
at which it is possible to live—if M‘Millan’s, or
any other, firm is permitted to import an English
article made at 4d. ; the only result would be to
destroy the local industry altogether, and leave
the local worker with—instead of a miserably
poor wage—no wage at all. And to shut out the
imported article would be bad for the great
absentee firm of M‘Arthur and Co., which lias
just paid 11 per cent, dividend to its English
shareholders. M‘Millan writes scornfully about


the “ infantile cry of Victoria ” that all the States
shouM enforce the payment of a living wage, so that
it won’t be driven, by the competition of States
where the blessed liberty to sweat and be sweated
is still rampant, to abandon its living wage in self-
defence. Also he says, “ the dream is being
rudely dispelled by the fresh air of the whole con-
tinent of Australia,” which is now blowing on the
industries which pay a living wage—by which he
doubtless means that the fetid breath of the fever-
dens where haggard, bony women live a death-in-
life-at making pants at 4d. a pair will blow on
these industries when border tariffs are removed.
And if it isn’t allowed to blow Liberty will be
outraged and great absentee softgoods firms may
have to be content with 5 per cent, instead of 11.
And M‘Mill an demands scornfully
Is this restrictive system strong enough to withstand
:the ordinary and inevitable laws of political economy ?
To which the reply is certainly “No!” No
•system can stand against any inevitable law. But
the law it is going to stand against is the artificial
and diseased and bogus law that an absentee firm
must make 11 per cent. The law of the Minimum
Wage is as good as the law of the Maximum
Dividend any day.

More Adelaide Tramway Literature.
Adelaide Observer barracks once more with
great violence for handing over the Adelaide
tramways to a private syndicate
All the available borrowing power of the State, which
is relatively small and will become less and less, is needed
for current and prospective undertakings of a truly
national and developmental character ; and to appropriate
any of this margin for a city luxury, when the money
might be better spent in opening new avenues of settle-
ment in the interior, would be to adopt means of retard-
ing general progress and prosperity. No legislator, and
particularly no representative of a country electorate,
would be acting in the best interests of his constituency
by sanctioning the diversion of a limited national credit
for a purely local purpose. And even the possibility that
a work will pay a handsome profit does not alone entitle
the State to take it over* especially when the temptation
to do so raises moral as well as economic questions.
What a truly brilliant idea ! The State’s borrow-
ing powers are becoming less and less because the
borrowed money is spent on works which don’t
pay interest. When the State borrows £1,000,000
(annual interest, £35,000) and invests it in some
work which only yields £15,000, the result is that
£20,000 a year more has to be raised by taxation.
South Australia has been doing this sort of thing
for 47 years, till the balance of interest that has to
be raised by taxation through spending the loan
money on unpayable works is £240,000 a year.
This is more, in proportion to population, than the
whole interest bill in Great Britain, and naturally
the borrowing power of a State in that situation is
growing limited. If S.A. raised another million
(interest £35,000 a year), and invested it in a good-
paying undertaking like the Adelaide trams, and
made £65,000 a year out of it, there would be
£30,000 a year profit, and the burden which has to
be raised by taxation would drop to £210,000.
Then the country, being less embarrassed, would
have more borrowing powers instead of less. A
rich man may borrow £2,000,000 much more easily
than a poor one can borrow £IOO. Unfortunately,
it has become a kind of fixed idea that unprofitable
public works should be done by the State, and
profitable ones should be left for syndicates and
iboodlers and individual sharks. Even now, bad
-as things are, the S.A. Government could borrow
the money to build Adelaide’s electric trams
at 3| per cent, and the foreign syndicate, it
;is dead certain, won’t pay less than 4 per
.cent, for its debenture borrowings probably
it will pay five. The money will most likely
be raised in England either way, but the
biggest drain on the country for interest will
ibe under the syndicate system. Then the syndi-
cate’s shareholders will want 10 per cent, if they
can get it—the Melbourne tramway shareholders
took 24 per cent, last year—on the portion of the
money they put up themselves, and a lot of that
will also go out of the country. A country must
be darned poor when it is so poor that it can’t
lafford to make a profit of 10 per cent.—or 24 per
cent. ; it is like being so naked that one can’t wear
clothes. And the State can do the work quite as
fast as any syndicate if Premier Jenkins only
moves. So far, however, the purchased press of
Adelaide persistently warns Jenkins not to move,
and that rather uncertain gentleman from the
Wooden Ham country is so much afraid to oppose
the press that he obeys. Then the same press
points out that the failure of Jenkins to move
shows that State ownership is impossible.

Seventy per Shent.
“ Pyalla ” to The Bulletin :
Two money-lending bills were recently introduced into
N.S.W. Assembly—one by John Hurley, the other by
James Ashton on behalf of Hushes, M.L.C. Both have
the same object, yet are totally dissimilar in principle.
Hurley’s bill fixes a legal maximum rate of interest,
Hughes’ mentions no rate at all. The former is actually
a resurrection of the British laws, which were
repealed in ’sl. Hughes’ bill is a copy of the
measure drafted by Lord James of Hereford, which
became law in England last year. The principle of
fixing a rate of interest by Act of Parliament was first
adopted in England in 1571, and was maintained until the
17th year of Victoria’s reign. From that time until last
year Britain was without usury laws, and the word
“ usury ” had no legal significance. Many attempts, how-
ever, were made in Great Britain and the colonies to re-
enact the sumptuary laws, but all failed, in face of the
argument that Parliament should not attempt to perform
the functions of an open-market by fixing the price of a
commodity-— i.e., money. Nevertheless, the alarming
rampancy of the evils of usury was fully recognised,
and a profound sensation was caused by the reports of two
• select committees of the House of Commons, in ’97 and ’9B.
A proposal by the ’9B committee was to give the Courts
absolute discretion in dealing with money-lending trans-
actions, all of which should be open to judicial review.
The suggestion was embodied in the British Bill, which
is now law, and is adopted in Hughes’ Bill which it is to be
hoped will soon become law in N.S.W. In all money-
lending cases that come before the Courts power is given
(to the said Courts to review and go behind any contract,
and to relieve the borrower where the burden is grossly
disproportionate to the amount of benefit he receives.
Also, application can be made to the Courts by the debtor
for review and relief, and the Courts can determine the
amount considered as a fair and reasonable rate.
Though Hughes’ bill does not fix a rate of interest, it
will be more drastic in its effects upon the usurer than
would Hurley’s. Its initial clauses are calculated to
at once shut the doors of many a Shylock. First, Shy-
lock will have to register himself as a money-lender, and
he must trade in his registered name only, and at a regis-
tered address. Thus he will have to find his own prey
without the aid.of such jackals as “Christian gentle-
man’’and “wealthy widow.” Further, where a security
given by the borrower is evidenced by any document, a
copy of such document will have to be furnished to the
borrower and to the surety, if any, so that he or they
shall know positively the nature of the bargain entered
into. Heavy penalties are imposed for any infringement
of these provisions.
Hurley’s Bill has been withdrawn owing to a technical
error in the manner of introducing it ; and writer knows
that many a bloodsucker simply grinned when the
measure was introduced because he knew it would never
become law. But, with regard to Hughes’ Bill, Shylock
is shaking in his shoes because such a measure would
“ get at ” him properly. It is rumored that the worst of
the money-lenders (the large majority) are taking con-
certed action, and intend to spare no expense in blocking
the measure! This, of itself, should put the proper label
on them, because the provisions of the Bill affect only
“ cronk ” and rapacious transactions. The “ fixing of the
price of a commodity ” dodge won’t work now, in the face
of the simple and obvious course of enabling the judiciary
to act upon its discrimination between reasonable fair-
dealing and voracious scoundrelism.

A Tasmanian Legislator.
Among some Australian politicians almost every-
thing is a good excuse for floating a loan, just as
almost every circumstance is held by the intensely
red-nosed citizen as a good and sufficient reason
for whisky. John M‘Call, M.H.A., of Tas-
mania, is the newest gentleman to put up a record
in this way, and it is a fine large record, too. The
situation is this. Tasmania, like all the States,
will lose heavily under the Federal tariff by the
abolition of inter-State duties. That is to say its
Treasury will lose—-its people will lose nothing.
Whether duties are paid by the buyer, as Free-
traders believe, or by the seller, as Protectionists
believe, inter-State duties are paid by the Austra-
lian people because in that kind of commerce
they both buy and sell. The Tasmanian people
will save, either as buyers or sellers, what their
Government loses, and it is only necessary that
they should pay in extra direct taxes what they
cease to pay in indirect taxes, and the people and
the State Treasury will be as solvent as ever.
Even that wouldn’t make the direct taxes
burdensome, for Tasmania now pays less of them
than does any other State. Here are the figures :
Direct Taxation ter Inhabitant Annually.
Maoriland .. £0 18 8 Victoria .. .. £0 11 3
Queensland .. 0 13 3 Westralia .. 0 11 1
South Australia 0 12 10 Tasmania .. 0 7 8
New South Wales C 11 3
And this is just where M‘Call, M.H.A., comes
in. He can’t bear the idea of paving enough new
taxes to make up for the old ones that are
abolished, and he proposes that for five years
Tasmania should borrow enough (about £500,000,
he thinks, would be sufficient) to put things right,
and posterity should thereafter shoulder the
burden as usual. And this (ye gods!) is his
reason :
To induce New South Wales to come in, we had to agree
to provide a book-keeping period of five years. This
means a distribution of the surplus customs and excise
revenue, according to the amount consumed or used in
each State, instead of per head of the population ; though,
strange to say, the cost of government is taken on this
basis. Now it has been shown, over and over again, we
will be much better off when a change is made in favor
of the distribution according to population, and I am one
of those who believe, when this can be considered by the
Federal Parliament on its merits, without the fear of
threats from any State opposed to it, the good sense of
the Federal Parliament will, with a proper federal spirit,
agree to the method of distribution that will be advan-
tageous to the smaller States. I mean, of course, those
with the smaller population. I take this view that the
Treasurer’s loss will possibly be £500,000 for the five
years we are under the “ book-keeping” system. What-
ever it is, I contend it is the cost of union—a union that
is not only to benefit the people of this State for five
years (the book-keeping period), but for all time. If we
admit this, I contend the people who inhabit this State
for the particular period when this bill is run up for the
permanent benefit of the State should not be called upon
to pay the lot.
For those first five years a strict .account is to be
kept, and each State is to get back the exact
amount due to it over and above its share of the
Federal expenses. That is to say, every inhabitant
is to pay an equal share of the cost of the Federal
Government, and each State is to get back from
the Commonwealth exactly what it contributed in
taxes, less its people’s share of the cost of the
Federal Government. After five years the Common-
wealth Parliament can fix things as it pleases, and in
a rough and ready division of the surplus Tasmania
may get more or it may get less —one is as likely
as the other. But dear old John wants to borrow
£500,000 to pay ordinary expenses with in the
meantime, and leave the bill to posterity, on the
supposition that Tasmania may get more than its
share after the book-keeping period is over. The
present generation is to cease paying border duties,
and put on no new taxes to make up for the
old tax taken off. Truly posterity is a useful
animal to carry the burdens of the lazy, selfish
old man of to-day.
“Tied” Hotels.
The Parliamentary Committee which is sitting in
Sydney to consider the questions of “tied ” hotels
has unearthed an extraordinary and almost in-
credible state of affairs. If the sacred name of
Liberty is to amount to anything, the enslaved
publican is a good subject to practise on, and the
slave-owning brewer is almost as much in need of
suppression as any planter who ever walloped his
nigger on an Alabama plantation. Here are some
samples of evidence:
The system connected with a publican going into a tied
house was as follows : The brokers advertise houses at
given prices. The applicant then obtains particulars re-
garding a house he fancies, as to lease, the amount of
money required, and the loan permitted. The books
showing the trade done are then produced, and if the
results are satisfactory the solicitors for the broker draw
up a bill of sale on the furniture and fittings, and a
bond is then drawn up for double the amount of the lien,
which specifies that unless the licensee deals with the
brewery in question for all beers, wines, spirits, and
aerated waters, the bond can be enforced. With regard
to the lien on the hotel, in 99 cases out of 100 it had never
been incurred by the signer of the bond.
The bond, let it be observed, is for twice the
real amount of the brewer’s claim on the hotel, so
as to bind the luckless devil of a publican all the
more surely. Some more evidence :
Brewers charged to the licensees of their tied houses
considerably more for beers and spirits than they could pur-
chase them for in the open market. The brewers allowed
no discount to tied houses, but they did to free houses.
The licensee of a tied house was bound to accept what-
ever liquor was supplied to him, whether it was good or
bad, or suited the customers or not, and if sent back no
allowance was, as a rule, made for any such liquor.
A very strong agreement now existed between the
different brewers not to interfere with each other’s houses.
With the abolition of the border duties, the combination
would work their utmost to prevent Victorian, South
Australian and Tasmanian beer from coming into the
State. In Victoria, a 54-gallon hogshead was supplied to
free houses for £2, and to tied houses for £2 10s. ; the
Adelaide 00-operative Brewery supplied the 54-gallon
hogshead at either £2 or £2 25., and a rebate was
allowed on this amount. Anyone who once drank
Victorian beer would never want again to drink the
Sydney beer. In Sydney the SU-gallon hogshead cost
£3 12s. In Victoria there was a heavy duty on malt and
hops, which were, however, free in Sydney. In the case
of these hogsheads, although the brewers charged their
customers for 54-gallons, they were only charged on 50-
gallons by the Excise Department. When a cask of bad
beer was returned to the brewery the Excise allowed, a
rebate, but nothing of this was returned to the publican,
who had to suffer a loss. The frequent sale of illicit
liquor was attributed to the excessive charges made by
the brewers to their “ tied ” customers. If a publican in
one of these “ tied houses ” refused to accept any goods
supplied by the brewers, the result of his bond would be
that a bailiff would be put in the next morning.
No wonder Sydney brewers are prosperous; £3
12s. for a hogshead of beer which is pretty often
bad —there is no particular reason for troubling to
make it good when the publican has to sell it, or
at least pay for it, in any case—against Adelaide’s
£2 2s. and Melbourne’s £2 10s. for a superior
article. And here is something more about the
brewer’s way of doing business:
Whichever firm (of brewers) secures the lease, the
amount of the bonus paid by the brewers to the landlord
becomes a first charge against the ingoing tenant, and
this is the way the liens are usually incurred. If, through
neglect or other cause, a tenant is unable to pay his ac-
counts, and the brewery enters into possession, the
amount of this tenant’s debts goes on to the lien, and the
next licensee has to sign a bond for the original amount
with the sum in question added, as well as interest at the
rate of 6 per cent. One brewery, to the witness’s know-
ledge, had direct control over four hotels, the licenses being
taken out in the names of the firm’s clerks and travellers.
In many cases, just before 12 o’clock on licensing day,
the applicant was rushed into the office of the brewers’
solicitors, and the bill of sale, lease, promissory note for
the amount of the lien (repayable on demand), a blank
transfer of the licence, and all the other documents were
put before him. He had no time to read any of them.
He had to sign them beforehe could go before the licensing
bench. The documents bound the signer body and soul
to the brewer. There was seldom anything done till the
day when the transfer was to be applied for. Say the
transfer was on a Monday, the brewers, however, wanted
the money lodged on the Saturday. Applications for the
documents prior to day of the transfer were always met
with the reply that they were not ready. In every case,
too, the applicant had to pay all costs, both those of the
lawyers and for stamps.
As to why the publican stands this sort of thing
well, the average publican knows no other way of
making a living. He must get a public-house, and
as 98 per cent, of the public-houses belong to
brewers he has to take the only kind of public-
house he can get. If the brewer’s demands
amount to more than he can earn honestly by
selling the brewer’s bad and exorbitant beer, he
goes in for Sunday trading, and sublets his private
bar at a hideously-exorbitant rent to ladies with
tight waists and loose characters, and makes the
money to satisfy his beer-cormorant by any means
he can. After these and other revelations, the
Bill for the abolition of “ tied ’’-house agreements
should pass by a deafening majority —still it is
hard to say. Truth is mighty and will prevail.
Beer is also mighty, and it prevails quite as often
as truth does.

A Tale of the Sea-
“ Shellback ” tells a tale of the sea :
Re Tagliaferro-Alala salvage case, recently heard in
Melbourne. Evidence was given by masters of both
vessels to the effect that their ships were thoroughly sea-
worthy, and one of them had only undergone its annual
survey three months previously. Yet, when the Taglia-
ferro ranged up alongside the Alala, in response to latter s
signals for assistance, it was found that neither vessel had
a 'suitable towline on board. Mooring wires and sundries
were used, and, bv good luck, assisted by a fresh gale on
her quarter, the Tagliaferro ultimately brought her tow
to a safe anchorage in Portland Bay. Now, when under-
going survey for Marine Board, Board of Trade, or any
other qualified authority’s sea-going certificate, sundry
items of equipment must be produced for surveyor’s
inspection and approval. Amongst them is a suitable
towline, either of wire or rope. As both these ships had
recently passed their survey, the question arises—What
had become of their towing-gear ? It’s not an article that
the cook might, by accident, heave overboard to wind-
’ard, nor is a 120-fathom 11-inch hawser a thing that can
get/' itself lost in the chart-room locker, or hide its
modest head in a corner of the bo’sun’s store. The
natural inference remains that these two vessels passed
at least one survey minus this part of their necessary
equipment. If one item can be successfully dodged, then
why not others, as, for instance, boat-accommodation,
life-belts (not that the latter are of much use, still, like
the Scripture, they afford some consolation to nervous
passengers), or unworkable hand bilge pumps ? These
latter, as a rule, are only shipped for surveyors’ inspec-
tion, and rarely, if ever, put to the purpose for which
they are intended, and through time and want of use
frequently get out of order. As a matter of fact, the
official survey of deck-gear is conducted in a most per-
functory manner. The time at the official’s disposal, as a
rule, is too limited to allow him to do much more than
just glance at the various items as they are brought
under his notice. The operation too, as often as not,
takes place in the graving-dock, so boats, as a rule, are
just lifted from their chocks and swung outboard. Occa-
sionally they are floated, with oftentimes disastrous
results as regards leaking. As an instance, I was once in
a South Sea Island steamer loading in Sydney. Surveyor
stopped ship from proceeding to sea on account of heavy
list to port. The impulsive little Welsh skipper, anxious
to get away (before all the split peas on his manifest
were detected) yelled for the hose and ordered a star-
board lifeboat to be filled with water, just to put
the ship on an even keel till we got outside.
We poured a good part of the harbour into that boat, and
then the skipper grew madder than ever, and yelled to
us to leave off, for the so-called lifeboat leaked worse than
a basket, and he was afraid the surveyor would stop him
for that as well. Eventually the packet was trimmed,
and she got away from the wharf. Outside the Heads
the mate commenced to get the mudhook on board.
Coming up one fluke touched the side with a
pretty smart tap, which brought from the skipper an in-
junction to “be careful with that anchor, mister, as she
is mighty thin forrard.” One voyage in that greyhound
was enough for me. On the whole, it seems that the
system of yearly inspection in local ports leaves consider-
able room for improvement.

More about Private Railways.
A faint attempt to resurrect the old dead agita-
tion for private, instead of State, railway owner-
ship is noticeable, now and then, in Australia.
This country’s great rival is Argentina, just as
the great rival of Britain is the United States.
And Argentina threatens to leave Australia very
badly—it may be only a temporary result of the
drought, or it may be a permanent proceeding.
So we begin to meet interesting little items like
this in the dailies (Sydney Telegraph sample):
Captain A. W. Pearse, in his pamphlet entitled “ Our
Great Rival : the Argentine Republic,” presents a glowing
picture of the progress and prosperity of that South
American country. His aim is to show how Australia is
being distanced by these rival producers at the River
Piate, and to point out how legislation is injuring this
country. Well, we are inclined to think that in the
matter of legislation Argentina has not much to boast
about; that country has progressed in spite of it, as N.Z.
has latterly done, and, as we hope, Australia will pro-
gress. But it is quite to be admitted that in recent years
Argentina has gone ahead the faster. Captain Pearse
says that Buenos Ayres has a much larger trade than
Sydney, which is true, because Sydney is not the only
port in Australia. He says their railways, though in the
hands of companies, carry live stock, &c., on lower terms
than our Government railways do ; and that, while here
there is a dearth of employment, our rival is going ahead
by leaps and bounds.
Certainly, three-fourths of the Argentine rail-
ways—not all, by any means—are private rail-
ways, and these do carry goods pretty cheaply.
But behind this there is the same old explanation.
Every private line in Argentina was propped up
with G-overnment subsidies and guarantees. As
far back as 1891 the Government’s liability for
pauper aid to these awful private speculations was
£920,000 per annum, with a prospect of the bill
rising to £1,500,000 per annum. Finally, the
matter was compromised by giving them immense
lump sums in Govt, securities. One way and
another, the private railways of Argentina have
saddled the Govt, with a permanent liability
of between £600,000 and £1,000,000 a year,
to say nothing of land-grants; and even
with this assistance they don’t pay as well
as the Australian railway systems. • The Gov-
ernment, tottering under this load, went broke
a few years ago, though it has since recovered
a little, and is now able to get about and
pay its debts, partly in very bad paper-money,
worth less than half its face value. If Australia
wants cheaper railway freights on the Argentine
principle, there is no need to sell its lines to pri-
vate companies. All that is wanted is to save
about half the interest bill by compromising with
the public creditor, and then all rates could be cut
down 25 per cent, in one act.

Touching the Vic. Mount Morgan Co. a share-
holder writes :
A little more than a year ago, I attended a meeting o!
shareholders, whereat the chairman announced that as
there were a large number of forfeited shares of no use to
the co. it was proposed to give 10,000 of them for a valu-
able block of land adjoining the mine. On the table
was lying a long document, with the co.’s solicitor
in attendance. A shareholder next me asked that
it might be read, when it appeared that under this
agreement 10,001 shares were to be given to the chairman,
E. M. Cairns, for this lease—a lease that had been taken
up long ago and abandoned without any work being done
on it, though from its situation it might be of value. In
reply to protesting shareholders, the chairman, Mr.
Cairns, stated that it had been determined to acquire the
ground, which was necessary for the mine, and he held
proxies enough at his command in the room to settle the
question. Of course during this heated discussion nobody
thought to examine the agreement, and the resolution
was put to the meeting and carried by a majority of
one, Mr. MacDonald, a shareholder, calling out loudly
“ this is a fraud on the shareholders.” The whole thing
was an utter surprise. In addition, there was a further
agreement, that the directors were at liberty to acquire
any other leases they thought fit on any terms they
pleased. Immediately this was carried the minutes of
the meeting were confirmed, and in an incredibly short
time 10,000 of the co.’s shares had passed into the chair-
man’s possession without cost to him, for, by the terms
of the agreement, the shareholders were made liable for
the calls. With regard to the second part of the agree-
ment, we heard nothing for months, but recently we
were told that 10,000 other shares had been given to the
Chairman (Cairns) and one of the directors (Nicholas' for
two other leases pegged by them, at the rate of 5000
shares each- costing them merely the trouble of pegging
but the ground. So that in all 20,000 shares have gone
to the chairman and directors, the present value of the
shares being about £1 each. This is c oifinned by the
chairman’s circular of 28th May last informing us that the
mine had been sold in London for 40,000 shares in a new
co., of which they, the shareholders, were to have 20,000,
leaving 20,000 to the parties who held the agreement I
have referred to. A further call of Id. per share on these
shares was the subject of a recent indignation meeting in
Melbourne.

cook Government in favor of . an elected Conven-

third reading unless some impossible amendments
which they have outlined are accepted in com-
mittee. Fink, the chanipion wobbler and time-
server, is leading this section of the Govt, sup-
porters. Fink was one of the renegade Liberals
who assisted to oust Turner, and was rewarded
by M ‘Lean and Shiels with an empty portfolio.
At the time Shiels said they took him into the
Ministry because he (Shiels) wanted the assist-
ance of “an intelligent young man.” By some
clever political high-tumbling, Fink is again an
alleged supporter of the old Turner party. When
the session opened he was ready to become “ the
intelligent young man ” to Knight Gili.ott the
helpless, hopeless Attorney-General. But Pea-
cock knew Fink too well. All good Victorians

Melbourne ship-owners and agents, representing
much foreign capital, lately deputationised
Premier Barton to express their righteous horror
of the Immigration Restriction Act —that step-
ping-stone to a White Australia. Captain Currie,
their chief spokesman, declared the proposed Act
to be “un-British” and unjust, but plainly
showed where the shoe really pinched when he
pointed out that under the Act his co. would lose
a large income gained in carrying Asiatic passen-
gers to these States. Capt. Currie evidently
holds that anything which takes the bawbees out
of his pockets is un-British and unjust. Further-
more, Currie, after asserting that he would be
compelled to raise the price of freight to the detri-
ment of Australian producers in the event of
losing the Asiatic passenger traffic to Australia,
proceeded to declare that, whereas in 1890 there
were 19,000 aliens in Victoria and N.S.W.,
in 1900 there were only 10,500 —a decrease of
8500 in 10 years. Therefore he held that the
proposed Restriction Act wasn’t needed because
there are practically no niggers coming here ;
also, that it is unjust, because it will deprive his
co. of the large profits it makes in bringing that
absent and non-existent person. Further, it is an
outrage on the liberty of the numerous scarce local
nigger who doesn’t exist, because it deprives him
of his right to come here, and it is superfluous
because he isn’t coming in any case.
