It is truly wonderful to consider how a politician
can thrive with no stock-in-trade save the coarse,
ignorant assurance of the blatant pill-vendor or
book - canvasser. Last week George Reid,
the most bowled-out politician in Australia,
was still able to raise a cheer in a Sydney
suburb by an allusion to that deficit of his
“which nobody had been able to discover.”
The deficits whose existence that famous
political thimble-rigger denies in so off-hand a
fashion were shown up by the N.S.W. Govern-
ment Statistician, by the Auditor-General, and
by the committee of three (two of them nominated
by Reid himself) which reported on the accounts
at the end of his Premiership. The same howling
dervish of finance, in the early days of his Pre-
miership, appointed a committee of three Sydney
bankers to overhaul his accounts and guarantee
the absolute genuineness of his one great surplus,
and after going through the figures the committee
refused to do it. Whereupon Reid had the
astounding cheek to lay their refusal on the table
of the Assembly and say it was a triumphant
vindication of his character. And some years
later, after his ejection from office, he appointed
another committee, and paid it himself, to go into
his accounts and again prove his complete re-
liability and truthfulness, and even his own hired
committee wouldn’t do it. And George Reid
again said he was triumphantly vindicated.
Reid wasn’t the only man in N.S.W. who made
deficits, but in the way of denying their existence,
and cooking the accounts to conceal them, and
swearing that his own shortages were the work of
his predecessors, he never had an equal. There
was a streak of unutterable meanness in him
which dragged N.S.W. politics down to a level
which even they had never reached before. The
fact that this amazing political bounder is still
politically afloat and flourishing is almost the
worst thing that can be said of Australian Par-
liamentary life.
Attorney-General Gillott, in the Vic. Assem-
bly, has come to the conclusion that if specula-
tion were removed from the Stock Exchange we
would have it in a greater degree in other lines.
Speculation in mining is a preferable evil to
gambling in wheat, oil and other commodities.
The same truth would be admitted in the Federal
Parliament if members were expressly asked for a
Yea or Nay to it, and yet P.M.G. Drake is
allowed to hang on to his anti-“ Tattersall ”
legislation, whilst the Church and Ikey Mo Guild
fleefully rubs its ill-matched paws. Practically
)rake’s scheme is to feed a gang of dishonest


“ Men pay taxes all their lives, and such
ought to be the premium for Old Age Pensions.”
There is one place in Sydney where the
business of money-lending is carried on in an
equitable manner and where those in need of
financial accommodation can rely on being
decently treated. The sensible man who wishes
to get rid of his monetary worries at a minimum
of cost and trouble to himself goes to the offices of
the Australian Equitable Investment Co., Ltd.
The co. has a large capital for investment at low
interest, and is always open to advance money on
risks which the banks would decline; and acts
generally as a medium between the banks and
ordinary money-lenders. Men in business can
obtain assistance for their venture at a figure
which will leave them a fair profit for themselves.
The co. advances money to any extent on deeds of
land, bond warrants, merchandise, stock in trade,
scrip, interests under wills, furniture and all classes
of security. Press notices which have been pub-
lished from time to time express the favorable
opinion which generally exists regarding the co.
Mr. J. E. O’Brien is the Managing Director, and
the co.’s offices are at 45 Elizabeth-street, between
King and Hunter-streets.*

stock-jobbers with the money which he will refuse
to transmit to “Tatt.” by post. The square
racing gamble is to be suppressed that
cronk Stock - exchange business may in-
crease. All the talk about the “morality”
of Drake’s proposal is the merest piffle. Man is
a gambling animal to the same extent that he is
an alcoholist, and the suppression of sweeps will
not suppress the mental craving for which they
cater. As for the cant-snufflesome objection that
sweeps encourage gambling among women and
boys, the first answer is that 999 women out of
1000 only gamble with the consent and knowledge
of the men they get the money from. And the
youth, inasmuch as he is father to the man, can’t
be too soon initiated into the foolishness of build-
ing false hopes on the chance of a ss. ticket in
“ Tatts.” In fact, youthful experience of blank
disappointment is a healthful tonic rather than a
moral poison.

It must have been in a moment of con-
scientiousness bordering on frenzy that Donizetti
acknowledged a fancied debt to Walter Scott
in the title of “Lucia di Lammermoor.” No
single character is common to the novel and to the
opera, and only the one incident a trifling
matter in those days, the stabbing of the bride-
groom. The impecunious master of Ravenswood,
in the opera, is described as enjoying a state of
“ haughty prosperity ” ; the Lord Keeper and his
awesome wife presumably sleep in the cemetery
the Ashtons appear to keep conveniently in the
back garden; and the faithful Caleb, the one
creation which makes it possible to read Scott’s
dismal book, seems to have been discharged
from the service of his too affluent master.
In the first act, it appears that that
master is sneaking about the Ashtons’ grounds
on the look - out for Lucy ; while Lucy’s
brother and his Lowland followers, disguised in
kilts, and carrying modesty to a point no High-
lander has yet thought of by hiding their blushing
knees behind silk continuations, are looking for
Ravenswood. The hero, however, warned, no
doubt, by the melodious din with which the search
is accompanied, has got Luoy (Signora Sapio) and
a rejuvenated Alice (Signorina Marchi) to himself
in another part of the grounds. While, there-
fore, Lucy’s brother Henry (Signor Bozzoli) is
declaring his unalterable determination that Lucy
shall marry Bucklaw (Signor Cannonieri), Lucy
and Ravenswood are making quite other arrange-
ments, which include the transfer of a ring and a
very pretty love duet.
* * • *
In the second act it appears that Henry
has got into Opposition, and expects a visit from
the police. Bucklaw alone can work the Ministry,
and Lucy is Bucklaw’s price. Resort is had to
the dear old expedient of suppressing and forging
Ravenswood’s correspondence ; and for the sake
of her mother, who it now appears is really dead,
Lucy signs a marriage-contract with Bucklaw.
This, of course, is the cue for which Ravenswood
has been waiting in the wings, and he enters in
time to take a leading part in a great sestet and
chorus. After this peaceful demonstration, swords
are drawn, and there might have been the deuce
to pay but for the intervention of a douce
person of the name of Raimondo de Bitebend
(Signor Travaglini), who, it appears, is tutor
in the establishment. He adopts the simple
expedient of showing the marriage-certificate
to Ravenswood, who thereupon curses the
lady and discreetly withdraws. In the third
act it appears that the marriage has been
solemnised ; but a disturbance takes place, and
Bitebend informs the interested chorus that Lucy
has stabbed her husband in the bridal-chamber.
This intelligence is confirmed by the entrance of
Lucy, arrayed in very late evening-dress, and
carrying in her hand a bloodless weapon.
She closes her career by a very fine piece of
vocalisation, accompanied by a single flute.
Ravenswood meanwhile is meditating among the
tombs of his ancestors in the Ashtons’ cemetery,
at the back. Here a crowd of people naturally
come to look for him, anxious to impart the news ;
but any hopes which he may have founded upon
Lucy’s early widowhood are very quickly dis-
pelled by the careful Bitebend, who comes to tell
him that Lucy is dead. Then, after the usual
Eolite resistance and protest by his companions,
e stabs himself.
• * • *
The artists concerned and the manage-
ment alike are to be heartily congratulated on
the manner in which this famous opera was
placed before a tremendous house. The only
lady who has any opportunity is the heroine ;
and Signora De Vere Sapio made the most' of it.
Lucia is one of those parts which give no oppor-
tunity for a moderate success. It is all or noth-
ing. You may sing it well or sing it badly,
but never moderately well; because with this
sort of music moderately well means intolerably.
Signora Sapio was entirely successful alike in
“singing sorrow,” in giving the tears in the
voice, and in dealing with those awful vocal
tangles in the first act and in the mad scene.
Moreover, in her madness she looked so sweet
that one longed for an asylum where one
might hope to meet with one distraught like
this. Dani is more pleasing every time the pub-
lic makes his acquaintance. Unlike the average
tenor, he can act, and act well. He
is modest, unassuming, and never rushes to the
footlights to unburden himself of his breath.
Signor Bozzoli, as Henry, should break himself of
that habit of clawing at the air with the right
hand. There is nothing else of which he requires
breaking. Signor Travaglini should wash some
of the distemper off that too capacious forehead.
He can do nothing else to improve upon his
performance of Raimondo de Bitebend. As
Normanno, Signor Marcello does all that is
necessary ; and Signor Cannonieri bears up with
heroism against the white satin jacket and short
white trousers in which Bucklaw presents him-
self for the marriage ceremony.
** * *
“ Struck Oil ” was revived at Sydney
Lyceum last Saturday, and, judging by the house,
it is capable of a longer run than the few nights
for which it is billed. It is a common opinion
that without Maggie Moore’s unique personality,
“Struck Oil” would die out, but it is more
probable that it will keep on its legs so
long as the company that holds it up is
of a fair average merit. The chief motive of
“Struck Oil,” as everyone knows, is the affection
between father and daughter, and persons who
hear dry-eyed the wailings of the average stage
heroine when she loses her child (and goes about
hunting for it in a desultory sort of fashion and
finding it by inconsequent pieces of blue ribbon)
and persons who yawn fiercely when the bereaved
wife faints on going to see her about-to-be-
executed husband, —these persons had to cough
loudly when Lizzie Stofel, in a ridiculous pinafore
and sunbonnet, took her Saturday farewell of her
amiable but brainless German pa.
* * * •
Lizzie Stofel is the type of child who is
much more amusing on the stage than off, and
when she cleans a window by giving two dabs at
it, the hearts of gallery mothers are touched, for
they remember how their own Marys and Janes
clean the windows just like that at home. Lizzie’s
pa goes to the war with the honorable intention of
keeping well behind the other fellows. All one
sees of the war is a number of very small tents
which suddenly light up, also a vision of Pa
Stofel triumphantly dragging his own officer by a
rope from one scene where it is night into another
where it has suddenly become broad day. It is in
this drama, also, that it takes about six men to
steal one keg of whisky. Also, Stofel’s household
clock sticks to quarter past one in the first act,
and is as faithful to four o’clock or thereabouts in
the last. The engine in the Oilsburg scene rushes
into its part before the curtain ascends, and Pa
Stofel regains his memory and gets his oil just as
he has done many thousands of times before.
* * * *
Maggie Moore rises gloriously beyond her
avoirdupois, and as a large, healthy German
“ kind” manages to make the pathetic touches of
the character as forcible as the humorous ones.
Maggie’s magnetic personality and emotional
voice carry her triumphantly through many diffi-
culties. In the grown-up scene she is less
effective, but Lizzie Stofel, when she arrives at
long dress period, is a person of no great impor-
tance. Nevertheless, the long dress made a little
longer would suit Maggie better. E. B, Russell
makes a good impression as the amiable Deutsch
Pa, and the other characters, if sometimes a little
stiff, are, on the whole, harmless enough.
** * *
“Lady Hunt worth’s Experiment,” a
comedy as yet unseen by Australia, is to be
staged at Sydney Royal on Saturday. It is the
work of R. C. Carton, and is said to have made a
good impression in London. Under these cir-
cumstances Galfred, Earl of Tweenwayes, will
wear the brown suit and blackened eye of an’effete
aristocracy until Friday only. Though the
“ Amazons ” has been well supported, there has
not been, as there should have been, a concerted
rush of the democratic section of the community
to gloat on the degradation of Galfred, Earl of
Tweenwayes. The gallery, where democrats
mostly go, has shown no special accession of
business. Yet the Earl of Tweenwayes is
about the hardest thing, in a brown suit
and a dirt-colored whisker, that was ever
said about the British aristocracy. Coming, as
he did, after the visit of sundry brief and
anxious-looking real aristocrats, he suggests that
a long pedigree is more disastrous than long beers,
and that a family tree is apt, in the long run, to
get into the head of its possessor. There are
other members of the nobility in Pinero’s comedy,
but the bent and decayed earl is the only one who
is specifically described as being the remnant of
an ancient race, whose hollow cough had disturbed
the sleep of centuries. The others look like a
recently created and healthy peerage which lately
made its money in wild-cat.

Nothing large enough to shock this paper
out of its usual calm has happened at either the
Sydney Tivoli or Empire this week, so these two
shows may consider themselves released on their
own recognisances, if they have any.
** * *
Sydney Palace Theatre has issued a type-
written manifesto re the numerous changes that
are to be shown on its stage next week. Among
the new performers is some mention of a cul-
tured pig who is to do something really brainy—
writer forgets exactly what at the moment. Pour
weeks without one change in the programme is a
food record, but the Sydneyites seem to love their
’alace under any circumstances, and the number
of persons who are cast into outer darkness by
the doorkeeper because there is no rest for the
sole of their evening shoe remains as great as
ever.
At Sydney Criterion the last few nights
are announced of the “ Death or Glory Boys,’ the
drama which leaves the impression of haying been
written by a clever lunatic disguised in drink.
The author’s curious ideas as to the state of
anarchy in the British Army will accordingly
cease to trouble ; and it is rumoured that some-
thing called “ The World Against Her ” will take
its place. This sounds like the good old Past,
and the wife that is forgiven after many writhings
on the floor!
** * *
“Riding to Win,” the new drama at
Melb. Royal, seems to have been born in England
and reconstructed here by the eminent Australian
tactician, Bland Holt. It is now a Melbourne
narrative of thrilling local interest and the latest
topical allusions. George Darrell, years ago, used
to compile works of a similar kind with the hero’s
top-boots measured to fit himself, but in his worst
moments he never did anything quite so unoriginal
as “Riding to Win.” All the things which have
happened oftenest in the world of melodrama are
just now occurring for the severalth time at Melb.
Royal. The bad nephew (Albert Norman) and
his minion, the bill-discounter, come along and
conspire to ruin Walter Baker in order that Albert
may marry Miss Ross and acquire the pro
perty of Uncle. The same old stolen notes are
forced into Walter’s possession, and everybody
save Miss Ross believes him guilty, after which he
toils at a boatshed on the Yarra. Also Albert the
Bad, when suddenly confronted by one whom he
had led astray, curses her, and pushes her into a
tank that professes to be a pond in Melbourne
Botanical Gardens. An over-fed Newfoundland
dog is expected to rescue Miss Seymour from her
tepid bath, but on Saturday night the bloated
quadruped forgot its part, consequently the
drowning lady had to wade about in the tank
whilst sundry able-bodied men were sooling the
dawg on to its duty. The rescue sensation will
never be a certainty unless Miss Seymour is pro-
vided with a sheep’s pluck, or a biscuit, to tempt
the animal towards her. She must appeal to its
appetite; whistling will not bring it. On Satur-
day she appeared to stand up in the tank, after
sinking for the third time, and whistle with her
last breath ; but the dog merely wagged his tail to
signify that he knew he wasn’t really necessary
for her salvation.
• • » *
The charm of Bland Holt’s new drama lies
in a splendid disregard for consequences. The
tank incident passes like a dream, and no unplea-
sant results come of it. Arthur Styan, as the
father of the intended victim on that occasion,
subsequently catches Norman in the act of murder-
ing Uncle, whereupon he demands that his
daughter shall be married by the man of blood, so
as to make an honest woman of her. All this
time Walter Baker is one of the unemployed. He
is married to Miss Ross, and is rich in her affec-
tion, but, owing to cousin Albert having obtained
Uncle’s property by murder, poor Walter can’t
buy a medioal practice in Collins-street. He has
to go looking for jobs. And then it dawns upon
hungry Walter that he is a foolish medical gentle-
man to go looking for stray jobs when he can win
a £2OO bicycle-race at the Melb. Exhibition Oval.
This inspiration seizes him after Bland and Mrs.
Holt have kindly brought some bottled beer and a
goose to his domicile. The beer and sage-and-
onions put new life into Walter; he borrows a
bike and wins the £2OO. Moreover, Norman is
biffed off to prison on the unsupported word of
somebody, and, although this illegal proceeding
can make no material difference to the other
people, they all agree that the dark cloud has
rolled from off their lives.
* * * *
Bland Holt’s co. appears to as little disad-
vantage as possible in this lamentable drama,
whilst John Brunton contributes bright views of
Queenscliff and the Melbourne district. The
person who most deserves sympathy is Miss
Harrie Ireland. She plays the part of an irrele-
vant blind girl. No interest attaches to Miss
Harrie. When she strolls blindly into Baker’s
kitchen, they just dump her into a chair on the
prompt side of the stage, far away from the rest
of the company, and leave her to bear her afflic-
tion silently until somebody chances to remember
that she isn’t getting any bottled beer.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.), Wilson Barrett
is reviving “The Silver King,” with the same
Jaikes, Elijah Coombe, Susie and Cripps who
supported him the last time he faced the fire of
Australian comparisons in Titheradge’s character.
Otherwise, the present co. is perhaps a little less
satisfactory on tne whole than Barrett’s previous
crowd. There is nothing seriously the matter
with the performance, but “Silver King ” happens
to have great traditions attaching to it in this
country, and at one time or other old playgoers
have seen every character of the drama better
played than now. Which is not to say that Mel-
bourne doesn’t want this show. Inferior revivals
have flourished for more than a week or
two, and this one ought to do its little
bit of time “on its head. Wilson Barrett, the
original head, never looked or acted Wilfrid Den-
ver with particular success in the first two acts of
the play. His touch is too heavy. Reckless dis-
sipation doesn’t come natural to him. He makes
a conscientious pretence of being young and
foolish, but he doesn’t quite get there. Later on,
when the character becomes pretty much of a
missionary, white-haired and sententious, Barrett
is himself again. The repentant Denver is a
preacher, and it may be said of Wilson Barrett
that he seems an actor who sincerely loves to hear
himself preach. If the demand for more “Silver
King” should slacken this week, “Quo Vadis”
will be produced at Her Majesty’s next Saturday,
but the chances are in favor of Jaikes getting
more than six successive shillings out of Bodgers,
the baker.
** * *
Two performances of “ The Professor’s
Love Story ” were given at Melb. Princess’s last
Saturday, by way of dispelling the notion that
Charles Arnold and Co. are never happy unless
they are in a violent hurry. There is no indecent
haste in the Prof.’s Love Story. Arnold, Miss
Knights, Miss Frederics, George Willoughby,
Denton, Fred Sharpe, and Miss Ada Lee play the
slow, sentimental comedy for all it is worth. The
Scotch accent of two out of the three last named
would probably get them a month without the
option, in a Glasgow police court, but their tri-
angular courtship is excellent humor all the same.
As for Professor Arnold and his sister Miss Agnes
Knights, they strike sparks of true pathos from
their lines, and look thoroughly genuine under
conditions which put a big strain on their art. To
night (Thursday) Arnold makes his last appear-
ance in Melbourne as Jones of the Happenings,
and to-morrow the season ends with the departure
of Smith from his home.
** * #
The Crane-Power co. takes the Princess’s
stage next Saturday (7th) on behalf of a large
spectacular tragedy, entitled “Nadjezda ” —a par-
ticularly bad title from the commercial point of
view. “Nadjezda,” however, is said to be as
good a play as “Fedora,” or fifteen times better,
and the scenery in Tyrone Power’s production
will spread itself regardless of expense. Further-
more, the cast of the tragedy, from Miss Edith
Crane downwards, promises to give it a fair
chance, and if The Bulletin’s encouraging smile
can assist the enterprise the new management
may consider it done.
** * *
“ A Message from Mars ” appears to have
caught on to the male conscience at Melbourne
Bijou, also it is much admired by the average
d.c. female who wears her frock cut short at the
shoulders. But whereas the men are struck for
the moment by the wild unselfishness of Horace
Parker when he recovers from his dream, the
women like him best before he falls asleep.
Horace’s masterly indifference to the billing and
cooing of his “intended” tickles the fancy of
those bare-necked realists in the undress circle.
Their giggle had a note of admiration in it. On
the other hand, they can scarcely conceal their
contempt for the reformed Parker, because they
know that his mania for turning his house into a
hospital can’t possibly last—after marriage.
* ♦ * *
“A Message from Mars” is a pretty
little fiction, calculated to beguile an impression-
able johnnie into a brief, unnatural benevolence,
but it doesn’t delude the johnnie’s sister, or his
cousin, or his aunt. They know what will happen
to Horace Parker when Miss Ruby Ray has
married him. There will be no selling of his
fifty-guinea overcoat to give to the poor. That
isn’t the sort of unselfishness she was trying to
bounce him into when she took his engagement-
ring off her finger and gammoned that the gold
was burning it —as though gold could ever be too
hot for the angel sex ! Parker, in his matrimonial
state, is going to be furtively kind to the
poor when he gets a chance, and weakly
submissive to his Ruby all the time. His charity
will begin at home with the payment of enormous
millinery bills, and finish with a splendid fortune
for his widow when she has worried him to death.
The case of Parker chanced to clash with “The
Professor’s Love Story ”in Melbourne. Now the
Prof, is an unsuspecting bachelor scientist, who
remarks, in the course of his ignorance, that girls
don’t marry for money. And when the learned
infant of 40 babbles his simple confession of faith,
there comes a merry peal of sweet soprano laugh-
ter from all over the house. The Australian
woman takes herself candidly.
** * #
At Rickards, where the juggling shadow-
graphist and the young man who sings ‘ ‘ Ta-ra-ra ”
upside-down are assisting Coleman and other
people to keep the house warm, Marie Lloyd is
the forthcoming attraction. Marie has forthcome
on two previous occasions, but this will be her
positively last farewell fortnight. Likewise, it
will bring the melodious and somewhat melan-
choly Hurley to a close.

From information to hand, the Great Southern
Consols Gold Mining 00. has taken a step in the
right direction towards reducing its working expenses.
Having decided to introduce electric traction in its
mine at Rutherglen, tenders were called from all electri-
cal firms represented in Australia, and after close investi-
gation of the different offers, and having satisfied itself
as to the superiority of the plant offered by the
Austral Otis Eng. Co. Ltd.—as agents for Messrs. Ganz
and Co.—the Board decided to accept their tender. The
contract, which is to be carried out in four and a half
months, comprises steam dynamo with two electro loco-
motives with a capacity for hauling 60 tons per hour over
a distance of 8000 feet at a speed of five miles per hour,
and the plant is to be erected under the personal direc-
tion of Messrs. Ganz and Co.’s electrical expert attached
to their Australian Agency.*

If further proof is needed that Luhrig Vannera
are the best and most economical machines for dressing
fines and slimes, it will be found in the fact that the Tas-
mania gold mine, Beaconsfield, Tasmania, has decided to
increase the number of these machines in the extension
of the present mill and reduction works. When com-
pleted it will have over 100 head of stampers at work, and
60 Luhrig Vanners, including the additional 24 of the
large type. Before finally adopting plans the Tasmania
board sent its manager to inspect the chief reduction
works in other States. Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, are the Sole Agents for the Luhrig appli-
ances.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,

Lake View Consols (W. A.): Represented in Eng-
land that the total expenses of mining and milling work out
at £2 14s. per ton, and that includes everything ; also,
that when the plant is brought up to a capacity of 12,000
tons per month the mine should be earning a monthly
profit of close upon £50,000 —“an achievement of which
any mine in the world may be proud.” The good things
that are going to happen—’twas ever thus ! When the
Lake View was turning out over 30,0000 z. per month all
the Australian newspapers regarded it as the mine’s
settled production. It suddenly fell to 10,0000 z., and
then to 5,0000 z. Shares fell from £2B to £l4, and then to
£7. They are now £8 155., which means nearly 2J
millions. Preparations are being made to largely increase
the gold output, but it is doubtful if that is warranted by
mine developments, or if it can be maintained for any
length of time.

financial statement, this is how things stand (subject tx>

financial year in the five mainland States ended on 30th
June, 1901; in Tasmania on 31st December, 1900 :

The result is a total deficit for the six Commonwealth

Eight deficits, £7,114,600: three surpluses, £1,089,343 ;

are Coghlan’s figures, from the latest issue of the “ Seven
Colonies,” pages 770 and 771.) Also about £13,500,000 of
ordinary expenditure has been charged to loans, making
the real deficit about £19,500,000. In the last 21 financial
years (starting from 1880) Maoriland has had six deficits ;
Victoria and Tasmania, nine each ; South Australia and
Westralia, 10 each ; Queensland, 11; New South Wales, 14.
•• • «
In the days when honest George Turner was
Premier of Victoria it was enacted that the whole surplus,
when there was one, should go in reduction of the old
accumulated deficit—now stauding at about £2,356,000.
Shiels and Peacock have altered all that. A year ago
Shiels had a large surplus, but he only set aside a small
amount in reduction of the deficit, and carried £llB,OOO
forward. By reckoning that £llB,OOO as revenue all over
again, Peacock is able to profess that his present surplus
is £136,000, when the real amount is £lB,OOO. He pro-
poses to pay £50,000 off the old deficit and to carry £86,000
forward, and reckon that as revenue oyer again ; so if
next year has a real surplus of £14,000, it will appear as
£lOO,OOO. This good old G. Reid device enables one real
surplus of £lOO,OOO to appear year after year, till it looks
like £2,000,000. Still, Peacock hasn’t sunk to Reid’s level
and invented a Cash Basis. If he had adopted Reid’s idea
of not charging the year’s accounts with interest on the
public debt accrued but not yet paid, the Victorian sur-
plus this year would be £708,588—0n paper, like Reid’s
famous surplus of £340,000. But such a fake would be
hardly possible, except in Sydney. It can only be done
when the big dailies are prepared, for party reasons, to
defend it, or rather to deny that there is any fake at all,
and swear to the genuineness of the surplus, as the de-
graded Telegraph and Herald did in Reid’s case some
years ago.
* • • *
Treasurer Cribb, of Queensland, proposes to meet
his big deficit by land sales and by a general faith in Provi-
dence. Treas. Bird,of Tas.,anticipates adeficitof £123,943
next year as the result of the abolition of inter-State
duties and the imposition of something lighter than the
stupendous revenue tariff now existing in his isle,
and he proposes just to let the deficit slide and do nothing.
Premier Peacock expects a deficit of £229,113 for the new
financial year through loss of inter-State customs and
other causes. And that awful man says that he has been
advised to extend and graduate the land tax, but he
isn’t going to do anything of the kind (cheers). He
isn’t going to propose any new taxation (cheers). He
proposes to cut down old age pensions to a maximum of
7s. a week instead of 10s. ; to have all the cases re-heard
with a view to seeing if a lot of the pensions can’t be con-
fiscated altogether ; and having saved £44,000 a year this
way, and also pared down the allowance for the mainten-
ance of orphans and neglected children, he proposes to
let the rest of the shortage take care of itself. If this
grossly inhuman proposal doesn’t bring Premier Pecksniff
down from his high estate there is very little public spirit
left in Victoria.

* » * *
Melbourne is not an up-to-date municipality,
but it is so far ahead of most that it owns its own electric
light and supply plant, and makes a very decent profit
on the undertaking. If Greater Melbourne was one city,
like Glasgow or any other British centre that is run on
sane principles, instead of about 13 different munici-
palities, and if the undertaking covered the whole metro-
politan area instead of the city only, the results would
probably be much better. Taking things in their very
worst aspect, and debiting the concern, not only with
expenses and interest but with the discounts and charges
on loan flotations, results are as under :
Loss on Loss on
Undertaking. Loan Flotations. Deficit.
February, 1895 .. £8 £6250 £6258
Profit.
„ 1896 .. 1983 4275
„ 1897 .. 3913 362
Accumulated
Profit.
„ 1898 .. 2591 2229
„ 1899 .. 5149 7378
„ 1900 .. 7535 14,913
„ 1901 .. 6086 1906 19,093
Interest on Investments.
1690 20,783
The municipality has put away, by annual instalments,
£20,139 to a sinking fund for the repayment of the money
borrowed for the undertaking, and during seven years
the investment of this fund has produced £1690 in
interest, making the total sinking fund £21,829. Against
this, if the undertaking is debited with the loss on loan
flotations, there is a debit at the general account of
£1046, reducing the total accumulated profits to £20,783.
That is to say, the loss on loan flotations has already been
paid off ; the concern now pays interest and expenses and
a sufficient amount annually towards sinking fund to
clear off the whole debt in about 50 years ; in addition it
already yields the city a net profit of over £2OOO a year,
which will probably grow much larger; and it supplies
electric-light at a very cheap rate. Why nob consolidate
all the municipalities into a Greater Melbourne, and let
the Council supply all the metropolitan area ? The success
of its preliminary experiment justifies such a course.

The reserves consist of reserve fund, £850,000 (separately
invested in Government securities); contingency fund,
£150,000; and undivided profit, £23,818. The Union
Bank announced several years ago that it was necessary
to make provision to the extent of £250,000 for losses,
and transferred that sum from reserve to a special con-
tingency account, reducing the general reserve to
£750,000. But the amount is only now being actually
written off. The management transfers £25,000 of the
contingency fund back to the general reserve, whence it
originally came. Then it transfers £25,000 of profits to
contingency fund ; and then it writes off the £25,000.
This complicated way of doing things possibly helps to
amuse some person or persons unknown.
* * * *
The Union Bank’s profits for the half-year to
February, 1901, are equal to £6 17s. 4d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds as at the beginning of
the half-year. The increase of dividend to 8 per cent,
makes it equal to the figure of 1893, and, but for the
£25,000 written-off, the profits would make it possible to
pay a dividend of 11) per cent. The bank holds
£1,466,070 in State and municipal securities and £3,200,478
in cash, making a total of £4,666,548 in liquid assets,
equal to over 26 per cent, on all liabilities to the public.
Altogether, things are going reasonably well with the
Union.
* * » •
Dr. Montagu, the exceedingly stout gentleman
from Canada who lectures in this country on behalf of
that truly lamentable life assurance concern, the In-
dependent Order of Foresters (Canada) has been holding
forth lately in Queensland. Among other statements he is
reported as informing the public that “ Having no stock-
holders, and therefore no dividends to pay, the society
(the 1.0. F.) could protect families and homes at a much
lower rate than had ever been considered possible.” Con-
sidering that not one of the big Australian life offices has
any stockholders either, or any dividends to pay, it re-
quires a good deal of brassy assurance to put this forward
as a perfectly new benefit conferred by the 1.0. F. But
the point that can’t be too strongly emphasised is that, so
far as concerns the small £lOO insurer, who forms an
immense proportion of the insuring public, there is no
truth —not a vestige of truth—in all the 1.0.F.’s brazen
assertions about its remarkable cheapness. The 1.0.F.’s
premiums are low, but it tags on other charges, with this
result: —
Policy for £lOO, without Profits —Age 20.
1.0. F. A.M.P.
Quarterly Premium ..£0 5 0 £076
Court Dues 0 3 1) Nil
Total Cost £0 8 1) £0 7 6
Of which the A.M.P. uses for
expenses, and the 1.0. F. for
expenses and court dues
about £0 3 4) £0 11
Leaving to accumulate towards
paying the policy .. .. 0 4 9 0 6 5
If both were new concerns starting abreast, the £lOO
insurer in the A.M.P. would pay less, and yet
have about 40 per cent, better a chance of his
policy being paid when he dies. But they don’t
start fair. The difference is that, if the customary
actuarial test is applied to the A.M.P., it has
money enough to cover all liabilities and a good deal
over, while when the same test was applied to the 1.0. F.
in 1897, the liabilities came out at £7,159,342, and the
assets at £518,724, leaving the appalling deficit of
£6,640,618. And the man who insures now acquires a
share in this deficit. All the lecturing, the music, the
bunkum about the cheapness of the office, the huge
advertisements about the Red Indian with the long name
who runs the affair in Canada, don’t alter this result by a
single copper.

Pitt, Son and Badgery, Limited (Sydney), a
wool and produce concern which, up to ’92 or ’93, used to
pay regular 10 jier cent, dividends, but which has latterly
fallen on bad times, publishes another of its curiously
reticent balance-sheets. Figures for the last few years
have been: —
Profit. How disposed of.
June, 1897 .. £1,841 .. Written off £1,841
„ 1898 .. 2,731 .. „ 2,731
„ 1899 .. 4,043 .. „ 4,043
„ 1900 . 6,555 .. „ 6,555
„ 1901 .. 5,569 .. „ 5,569
The co. has a nominal reserve fund of £7500. Against
that there is a partly paper item, “ Goodwill, plant, office
furniture, &c., £25,000.” How much of this is goodwill
doesn’t appear, but the amount, whatever it is, can t
fairly be considered as an asset, and badly needs writing
off. Also there is an item, “ Sundry debtors, which
stood at £97,757 -in 1899, but which has been
reduced to £70,765, and it is apparently the
loss (whatever it is) under this heading that is being
written off at present. The loss still unprovided for,
whatever it may be, and the depreciation of freehold
property (set down at £10,718), if any, and the goodwill
(whatever that may be), when added together make an
unspecified amount, and it, less the £7500 reserve, lias to
be written off before the co. has any right to be in a
dividend-paying state again. The amount being a mys-
tery, it is impossible to guess how long it will take to
write it off. The most promising features are that the
earnings are much better than they were five years
and the huge overdraft, which is almost the only outside
liability, is being reduced. The total outside liabilities
for the last three years were : _ , ,

Information concerning the Sydney Meat Pre-
serving 00., brought up to date :
Less
Loss on Subsidy Total
Business. Received from Reserves.
N.S.W. Pastoralists.
Dec., 1898 .. £66,474
June, 1899 .. £2,136 .. £2,346 .. 66,684
Dec., 1899 .. 2,714 .. 3,726 .. 67,696
June, 1900 .. 5,526 .. 4,074 .. 66,244
Dec., 1900 .. 8,011 .. 6,514 .. 64,747
June, 1901 .. 7,660 .. 5,518 .. 62,605
The co. states in its report that “ our competition raised
the values of stock at Homebush to such high lev els ”
that the above loss was the natural consequence. Still
the revolt among the pastoralists against the continued
payment of the subsidy of 10s. per cent, on all stock sold
at Homebush continues. The points which the pastoral-
ists consider are mostly these : —(1) The Sydney Meat
00. is only one of a number of companies in the
same line of business, and the others get on solnehow
without a subsidy. (2) If the Sydney Meat 00. makes
the above losses through bad management, that doesn’t
help the pastoralists ; if it makes them by selling meat at
a needlessly low price that directly injures them as re-
ducing the market value of their product. (3) The sub-
sidy—which, for the last year, equalled nearly 60 per
cent, on the Sydney Meat Co.’s capital—gives it an ad-
vantage, which badly handicaps other establishments,
and helps to prevent them growing larger and more
numerous, and thereby creating a better demand for
stock. A circular has been sent out to the pastoralists
on the subject, and at latest advices 909 replies had been
received : 235 for continuing the subsidy, 81 for divid-
ing it among all the companies, 693 for its total abolition.

The dividend is intended to be a division of 5 per cent,

to make up, and reserves are still decreasing.

The reserves consist of Depreciation Fund, £35,69i, and

increased by more than £4OOO, and the position looks not
especially promising.
* m • *
Mick O’Brien : We aren’t in the North Cobar
Co.’s secrets, but we regard their scheme as a legitimate
mining enterprise and a rather alluring gamble.
* • * *
Poor little North Central Co., which for ten
years has been vainly trying to pick up a spur of the
Broken Hill lode, is apparently nearing its end. The
manager, in his half-yearly report, writes dolefully ; if,
he says, work is continued at all, it might as well be on
present lines as any other. The co., a purely Sydney
venture, has spent £21,335 altogether in a more deter-
mined fight than present writer ever saw any other
Sydney silver-mining co. responsible for ; and it has still
£1771 “deposit and accrued interest.” The co. used to
have a locked-up deposit; whether it is free now this
balance-sheet doesn’t say. Only once or twice during the
past six years have the prospects justified the co. in going
on ; and the present most certainly is not one of those
times.
* * • *
Overflow Mines (N.S.W.): If results only equal
preparations what a magnificent property this will
become? “ The erection of the water jacket plant lead
and copper refineries is making good progress. Every-
thing is in readiness to receive the jackets, and the fur-
nace stack is nearly completed, contracts have been let
for a supply of charcoal, and the supply of fluxes and coke is
also satisfactorily arranged, and the manager hopes to be
able to blow-in in three or four weeks’ time.” It is to be
hoped that another blow-out will not follow. A most pro-
mising mine, with plenty of money and good management,
has suffered from several serious mistakes,.and it is almost
too much to expect that this last little effort is going to
pull it out ot the mud.

BRANDY
The importation from England of a new
town clerk for Sydney looks like another attempt
on the part of the average alderman to convince
himself that the staggering debt, and the annual
deficiency, and the slums, and the dirty gutters,
and the great holes in George-st., and the impossi-
bility of making a decent city so long as 480,000
people are bossed by 43 separate sets of aldermen,
all exist through bad town-clerking. The debts
arose because the aldermen hadn't the courage to
raise enough money to pay expenses. The holes
in the street arose from the same cause. The
council has certainly very limited powers to im-
prove the city, but the powers would be of no use
to it without money, and it never asked very
seriously for such powers. The best town clerk on
earth can’t do much till all Sydney is consolidated,
until the council gets almost unlimited powers to
improve it, and until it gets the brains and energy
and a rate of at least 2s. 6d.—or 4s. would be
better—in the £to improve with. But the habit
of being shocked at the town clerk has become
chronic. Probably Premier Philp, of Queensland,
in like manner was shocked at his office-boy when
he found he had a £528,000 deficit, or declared that
a new and more competent hall-porter must be
imported from England at once.
* * * *
Sydney hospitals have been excessively
crowded during the past few weeks. A case in
point, within a Bulletin correspondent’s know-
ledge, is that of a young lady who went in one
afternoon to undergo an operation under chloro-
form. At 3 o’clock, next morning, she regained
consciousness, and at 7 she was wheeled out in a
chair to a cab, and sent home, as other patients
were waiting to get into the ward she had been in.
THE NEW SERVANT.
“ You must understand folds, you know.”
“ Oh, yes, mum! I was born in a fowl-house.”
Premier Holder’s release from prison of
the Russian Finn, Johann Andersen, owing to
soriie invisible pressure, has features in common

tremendous sensation when her sex was discovered some

politician).
* * * *
A funny scene occurred the other day in
Hobart Supreme Court. Chief Justice Dodds
has lately been made a K.C.M.G-. and Premier
Lewis, who is also Attorney-General, arose when
the three Judges came on the bench to open the
Full Court, and on behalf of the Bar congratulated
the C. J. on the “ honor conferred upon him by
His Majesty.” But poor Lewis had just then been
flung a C.M.G.-ship, much against his will, and
while Lewis congratulated the Chief, the latter,
who is a man with a sense of humor, dared not
in return congratulate the unhappy Lewis.
* * * *
A number of the brave soldier-boys who
sailed from Sydney per Britannic were so
hideously sea-sick all the way to Melbourne that
they stepped off there with the idea of returning
to mother. They asked Govt, to pay their pas-
sages back to Sydney, but the Govt, was not
taking any, and when last heard of the warriors
bold were besieging the guardians of the Patriotic
Fund for assistance. The Age, which lately
sooled the young men of Australia on to the horrid
Boer, heads its account of the above—“ Sea-sic-
kness Cures War Fever ” (we are supposed to
have forgotten by now how energetically David
worked to spread the germs of that fever.) A tired
janitor pointed out that they Were merely servants
engaged under contract to work for certain par-
ties in S’Africa, and that they had broken their
contract; also, that the Federal Defence Depart-
ment never agreed to act as dry nurse to deserters,
and further, that the road home lay N. by N.E.
i-E., and was good, easy walking this time of
year.
• * * *
Victoria sent 105 returned cohentingenters
back per Britannic to fight for Rhodes, Beit, and
Co. at Bull’s expense. They slipped away so
obscurely that apparently the only spectator of
their departure was a Herald reporter. He, pre-
sumably anxious about the slump in Jingoism,
interviewed most of the warriors. Nearly all
freely admitted that the inducements were the
high wages and the easily-got job. Said one ; “I
am going back because I have to. I gave up a
good position as clerk to fight for the Empire, and
when I came back my employer, a merchant who
had talked patriotism by the yard, told me he was
sorry, but could not sack my successor, and had
no room for me. An old uniform and a medal
will not keep me, so I am going back to earn a
living You can tell the people that. A little of
the plain truth may do them good, because
they very soon get over their wild shouting and

The Argus has taken quite a democratic
stand on the question of military uniforms, and,
in a recent article, advocates the sweeping away
of gold lace and flummery, the abolition of the
gew-gaws of the Scottish regiment, and the sub-
stitution of two suits of khaki for officers and men
alike. The cost of the different existing uniforms
is appended ; a lieutenant’s full outfit is shown as
varying from £lO2 in the artillery to £56 in the
infantry. Also, the Argus publishes an article
advocating the abolition of cavalry, urging it to
be obsolete as an arm of Australian defence.
And this in the face of the fact that 100 of Mel-
bourne’s very Naicest have just formed an alleged
cavalry regiment, with a gorgeous uniform like
unto that of a comic-opera general.

BLIND!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He turned his back on the sunset gold ;
’Twas other gold he sought ;
The western wizard, weaving bold
Cloud-fancies, touched him nought.
This man had sold his soul for gain,
And thought the selling good ;
For, captive in a golden chain,
He praised his servitude.
The sun, the moon, the rain, the wind,
The stars, the flowers that vein
The earth—as alien to his mind —
He gave no thought to them.
There was a watcher in the park,
And he was smitten sore ;
For fate had touched his eyes, and dark
They grew for evermore.
Yet turned he them upon the West,
Where glowed a royal rose,
And smiled, and felt his spirit blest,
And saw the light—who knows ?
Who knows—with fancy at the helm
And secret stars to light—
What fair, far off and spirit-realm
The soul at times may. sight ?
The two men went their homeward way,
And one deep peace befell ;
But one had starved his heart that day
And deemed it nourished well.
Each came, at last, to his own door,
And left the night behind,
Yet, who was rich? and who was poor?
And which of them was blind ?

* * * »
A Melbourne breach of promise case
between a spinster of “32 or 33, as near as she
could recollect,” and a widower of 53, revealed the
touching fact that they had “ generally called one
another ‘ Kiddy.’ ” Love is ever youthful, and in
this case there was seemingly a good deal of love.
Plaintiff said that the promiser, presuming on his
promise, had “betrayed” her, and a witness
stated that the lady one day came into the room
whieh he and defendant occupied and threw her
arms around 52-year-old “ Kiddy,” whereupon the
embraced one said “ For God’s sake, get out of
this ! ” She got out of the case with a verdict for
£5O damages and costs.
* * * *
That Melbourne girl who “ breach of-
P 'oniised ” her ex-boy after rebuking him with a
loaded, revolver-—and got £5O damages—is an in-
stance of woman’s constitutional inability to seize
the psychological moment, as it were. In her
evid nice she said the lover aforesaid one day took
her to a phrenologist who was also a matrimonial
agent, and, on learning this, wanted to marry her
there and then. The dallysome damsel pleaded
“it was so sudden,” and procrastinated—and the
laggard lover could never be brought to the scratch
again.
* * ■* *
Re the little girl and the shoe-lace (B.
17/8/’01). They manage things differently in
England. The Midland Railway has just prose-
cuted a woman for “interfering with the comfort
of a passenger.” She got into a carriage where a
man was alone, and, later on, demanded money,
threatening to make it hot for him. This is the
old story, but the up-to-date sequel is on quite
unaccountable lines. 1. The man took it fighting.
2. The Railway took up the case. 3. The magis-
trate believed him. And 4 (and most astonishing
of all) the woman was fined £2 !

N.S.W. Society for Prevention of Cruelty
to Animals summoned a certain citizen for ill-
treating rats. Citizen had a patent rat-trap
holding a number of the rodents, and he sprayed
them with kerosene and applied a lighted taper.
Defendant’s infant-assistant danced round the
cage to the music of the rats’ squeals of agony.
Wh en the case came before the police-court the
information was found faulty, and the infant-
assistant had to be summoned. Latter could not
he served, as he had been smuggled away, and the
ease fell tit rough—owing to the crass ignorance
and inability of the inspector.
A. F. Basset Hull writes :
You revive the old misstatement about the sale of the
Duke of York’s stamps. I happen to know that he still
possesses every British-Colonial stamp that was given to
or purchased by him. He disposed of a few insignificant
foreign things by way of exchange when he decided to
devote his attention exclusively to collecting British-
Oolonials, but the statement that he “ sold ” his stamps
to Rothschild or anyone else was a pure fabrication. In
1896 I contradicted the cablegrammer in a par. in Sydney
E. News, of which I enclose copy. I have since personally
discussed philatelic matters with the Duke, and can
assure you that he still possesses even the Colonial
stamps given him by Dan O’Connor 20 years ago.
This is a serious reflection on the Australian Tory
press cable agency. Where next will “ disloyalty”
rear her malignant head ?

“ C.R.” makes disrespectful remarks
about Furniture:—
It amuses me to hear people in the
bloom of health talking about the
provision they intend to make
s. for their old age. They see
too far ahead. The natural
sgSSJPhs, man sees as far as the
first meal—what Haz-
litt called the abori-
ginal meal—breakfast.
The hand-to-mouth ex-
istence is the only vivid
existence. The savor
of life is on your
. lips all the time.
I have seen men,
literary men who
called themselves
Bohemians, go
about the city
with furrowed
brows and eyes
like dying em-
bers. What- was
their trouble ?
Furniture !
They had
nice, neat
little homes
with car-
pets on
the floor—
a grotesque
absurdity
and a sani-
tary crime
in this
climate—
and a side-
board with
shelves,
and a
piano, and
a sofa that
groaned when
you sat upon
it, and chairs
that groaned for
the arrears of
time-payment, and
an extension dining-
table, and a bedstead
or two with polished
brass-knobs, impudent
and exasperating to the
drowsy eye, and a mirror
over the mantelpiece—
where no mirror should
ever be—and stair-cloth
S and stair-rods and curtain
—another sanitary crime in
this climate—and pictures which
U should be hung, as consolations, on
’ 1 the walls of the Blind Asylum. And these
things tyrannise over them and crush them down
with the dead weight of sordid and insolent matter.
They worked for them and kept awake at night for them,
and were in a state of constant terror lest they (the furni-
ture fetishes) should be taken from them, after all. Men
of this slavish type form the great majority of the com-
munity. They are the persons who fear a bailiff more
than they fear Satan. And yet they talk with compas-
sionate contempt about the poor benighted heathen who
worships stocks and stones. There is no superstition
known to history that has done so much to spoil the Joy
of Existence as the Cult of Respectability. Better the
old wooden gods than the new time-payment ones.

Queensland Government persists in im-
porting adult laborers. Meanwhile, in one day’s
“ wanted ” advt. column in a Brisbane daily, out
of 66 advts. 29 called for boys, girls, youths,
improvers, apprentices, &c. Which suggests that
if Queensland really wants working population to
develope its industries it wants it under 17 years of
age. So let the Pliilpites get in touch with all
orphanages, foundling-asylums and reformatories
in thq dear old Mother Country, and give local
employers the girl and boy labor they solicit.
* • * *
Four cases against the N.S.W. Railway
Commissioners for damages arising from tram-
accidents were heard during the last week in
August, and (like most similar actions brought
this year) all of them resulted in favor of the
plaintiffs, one injured soldier recovering £B5O
damages. The fact that the Commissioners
amicably settled out of Court all the Sydenham
accident cases, about 50 in number, is a severe dis-
appointment to the Devil’s Brigade. The terms
of settlement have not been made public, but they
are supposed to involve payment of some £lO,OOO
in all.
* * ♦ *
The Brisbane public’s sympathy (in the
recent libel case) was undoubtedly with the
Observer, not on the merits of this particular
case, but on account of Col. Ricardo’s personal
unpopularity, and also on account of the scandal-
ously partial summing up of Judge Cooper. This
gentleman was so conscious of the bias displayed
in his own speech that he spoke in this wise : “ If
you imagine you know my opinion you may do
so.” A little later he added that if the jury did
not agree with his opinion (an admission tiiat they
knew it), they could reject it, but if they agreed
with it he would ask them to sustain it. Impar-
tiality !
* * * *
Brisbane Newspaper Co., which gob struck
for £5OO and costs (meaning in all, perhaps,
£4000), over the Ricardo-OBSERVER libel suit, has,
so far, tacitly agreed to accept a public subscrip-
tion to defray its loss. The plan is a shilling sub-
scription-fund, but, as the collection racket is
moribund in Queensland, the chances of gathering
in 40,000 or lings look small.
* * * V
“ Yeteran ” : Re the recent Ricardo libel-
case in Brisbane. The men who made those
statements about Colonel Ricardo’s conduct had
not his responsibility, and could not know what
was passing through his mind. Charges of
cowardice are like charges of falsehood, easily
made, but hard to prove. Nothing is easier than
to call coward to a man who refuses to take any
unnecessary chances —though it is absolutely a
soldier’s duty to avoid such. Moreover, in re-
maining, as alleged, under cover while his men
were skirmishing in front, and in staying behind
with the led-horses, Colonel Ricardo simply acted
as directed in the drill-book. Also said that when
a staff-officer gave Ricardo an unspecified order,
he said the Brigadier would be responsible for the
consequences if he obeyed it. No attempt was
made to show that any ill consequences resulted
from any of these things, and, in fact, there is
nothing in the Observer’s charges inconsistent
with perfectly cool, calculating courage.
* * * «
Somebody in Sydney Catholic Press has
just become very angry about the woman suffrage
movement, and writes a half-column to that paper
to the effect that if our mothers get the vote they
will cease to kill the bugs ! As a rule, the average
suffrage-opponent says nice, refined things about
woman shedding a light round the domestic
hearth, and the necessity that she should be pre-
served as a household angel to do sweet and
gracious actions. The Press correspondent, how-
ever, lets the cat out of the bag by saying quite
openly that, by the time his mother has scrubbed
his floors, made the beds, and chased and caught
the offending insect aforesaid, she won’t have
any time to vote. The gentleman’s final word is
that “men will become drunkards, women will-
become degraded, homes neglected, and this will
be the outcome of the womanhood suffrage.”
Which is very sad. Woman, having a vote, will
fall from her high estate as a person who stays at
home and kills bugs.

Private letters from S’Africa explain the
absence of at least three Australian swaddies
unaccounted for among returns, invalids or
deaths. These three are now doing “time”—
one for striking a British officer, one for looting,
and the other for stealing horses and accoutre-
ments from an English johnnie corps. None of
them lias more than a year to do, though it was
only recently that a private who struck his
superior was shot ihstanter.

Hordern’s are experiencing much difficulty
in getting suitable accommodation for their
gigantic business. Why not give some of the
churches a chance to pay off their big debts?
They are lying idle during the daytime, and if on
Sundays the goods were left ticketed, lydies, while
improving their chances of salvation, might have
the opportunity of choosing something to buy
next day.
* * * *
Apropos wife-kissing in the street. Last
week’s Sydney prosecution was not unique. A
few years ago Mr. Charles Moore, curator of the
local Botanic Gardens, prosecuted a young man
for kissing his wife (or sitting with his arm round
her waist) on a seat in the gardens. Present
writer is not quite sure how that case ended.
* * * *
N.S.W. bakers, employees’ new rules
controlling wages and hours came into force
last Sunday night; their fight, started barely six
weeks ago, has been a bloodless battle. Wages
now are £2 10s., with week of 54 hours in the
bakehouse, and overtime at Is. Gd. an hour, as
against 255. weekly, for 12 to 14 hours a night and
overtime only a mirage. The masters have
cleverly accepted the inevitable, at the one time
adopting the men’s ultimatum with certain modi-
fications agreed to by a conference, and manipu-
lating the position to purge their trade of the keen
competitor who was selling to the retail grocer at
one penny a loaf. The men intend helping their
much-sweated fellows in the country and the
formation of a Federal Bakers’ Union is
mooted.
* * * *
Sydney is to be experimented on with an
all-night service of trams, at long intervals, with
a minimum fare of 3d. To the long-suffering
cabby this innovation will come as the very last
straw; and to the dweller on the tram-routes
it will add another blight to existence; but to the
rest of the population the commonsense under-
taking will be a boon. -
THE RIGHT COLOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ I am not pretty,” said the maid,
“ Nor clever, like my cousin, Gwenny ;
The compliments that you have paid
Are wasted. I’m ‘not taking any.’
You always say sweet things to me ;
To other girls your manner’s slighting.
Now, what can the attraction be ? ”
The man replied, quite candidly—
“ Your hair’s inviting.”
The maiden frowned and shook her head,
Then bit her lip in sore confusion :
“ My hair, I know, is flaming red ;
You might have spared me that allusion.
Please hold a match up to the blaze
Next time your nasty pipe needs lighting.”
Undaunted by her angry gaze,
He spake again that simple phrase—
“ Your hair’s inviting.
I would not have you dark or blonde
(Don’t toss your chin and look so spiteful).
I seek a sweetheart fiercely fond ;
And you, I’m sure, would prove delightful.
The blue-eyed dolls, with looks of gold,
Are mostly dull and unrequiting ;
The average brunette is cold.
Bright-red is Love’s true tint, I’m told—
Your hair’s inviting.”
MELB.
