A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, September 3, 1901.
My Dear Arini,—
If you’ve got a miniature of your grandmother,
or a curious buckle, or even a button of a size and
style which no other woman in your circle is likely
to possess a duplicate of, you may hang it on a
loop of ribbon velvet round your neck, let it dangle
to your knees, and know that you are doing some-
thing after the manner of smart London. So
you’ll not have lived in vain. I’ve seen three
women in Sydney wearing dangles. One of them
(the ornaments) was an oxidised-silver mirror of
the artistic Renb Lalique design, with a hinged
cover and about three miles in circumference.
Englishwomen, say the fashion-writers, are out-
Parising the Parisians with toilettes that are light
or “ loud.” They go shopping in costumes that a
season ago would have been ball-dresses, with the
addition of transparent lace covering the neck and
shoulders, and a big hat. Gloves you may carry in
your hand as a guarantee that you are not too poor
to buy them, but you need not wear them. I sup-
pose, now that Alexandra is Queen, attenuation
in women will be fashionable. Also, light-brown
hair and cigarette-smoking, for the Queen is an
inveterate cigarette-smoker. I don’t quite know
if you must smoke 15 cigarettes or 50 a day to be
“inveterate,” but a friend of mine, who was
lounging about Copenhagen a few years ago, while
the then Princess was there, says that she and the
Danish royalties made the cigarette no secret.
Every afternoon they sat in the palace grounds,
in sight of the passing townspeople, who were too
used to the show to stare, puffing, puffing all the
time. Some Britishers snap-shotted their Queen
and one of her daughters with their weeds in
active service. So newspaper stories of Edward
VTl’s freezing attitude towards women who smoke
must be inventions of men who don’t want help
from their wives in spending tobacco-money.
The Court for hearing claims in connection with
the Coronation met recently in London, and the
Registrar, having called for silence, under pain of
imprisonment, read some of the petitions
received. A gentleman of the name of Southeron
Estcourt had sent in his claim to be “ Chief Lar-
derer as owner of the Manor of Shipton.” The
Duke of Norfolk claimed “to act as Chief
Butler of England,” the Duke of Newcastle “to
provide a glove and support the King’s arm,”
“ The Bishops of Durham and Bath and Wells to
support their Majesties at the Coronation.” Sir
William Carmichael Anstruther (C. J. Darley’s son-
in -law) wanted “to exercise the office of Hereditary
Grand Carver for Scotland.” One lord claims to
carry the golden spurs, another to bear the cap of
maintenance. The Big Show of next June ought
to be very good as a mediaeval procession, but
there’ll be much left out —for instance, the asses’
heads, worn (superfluously) by capering jesters.
A disagreeable little London paper is telling that
the King has been very cool to a certain actress well
known in Australia ever since Frank Gardiner, the
mining-millionaire husband of Carrie Swain,
motored H.M. round Paris in his car. Gardiner
had snapshotters stationed at certain corners to
snap him and his Royal pal. The King was then
P. of W., but that was quite good*enough for the
pallid Frank. The actress had introduced the
mining man to the future monarch. An introduc-
tion like that ought to be well-paid for.
A circular, gold and silver lettered, informs me
that on July 12, on board the Konigin Luise, a son
was born to Madame Ella Russell, wife of “Cesare
de Righini, Esq.” And the circular is stamped
with the arms of the liner, and the name of the
captain, and attested by two doctors. A touch of
Royalty !
Wedding-portraits of Dion Boucicault show him
to be the same young, unlined, keen-eyed Dot
that Australia knew some ten years agone. In
fact they surely must be of that time, for a letter
from a London friend says : —“ Boucicault and his
bride are a curious-looking pair. She, so tall and
gaunt; he, so small, crushed-looking, grey-haired,
and wrinkled. The wheels of London grind
quickly. Another man who has shrivelled and
parchmented is Haddon Chambers. While I’m
writing about stars —or only star-dust—let me say
that my ink is not blacker than the outlook before
Australians who come over here with their sing-
ing-voices, playing-fingers, or writing-brains, but
minus private means. One of Marchesi’s present
pupils (you know her well) tells me that she often
goes hungry to her gilt-edged lesson. None of her
Australian friends dream that she and others
with voices as good or better than the girls they
left behind them live in garrets and have not
sufficient food. These plucky creatures still keep
up the fiction of prosperity by getting the cable-
man to send along cheerful lies. One dramatic
artist confessed to me (I don’t say how long ago)
that she got the ‘ fox ’ of an engagement at £3O
per week cabled out when the figure was really
only 305.”
From Brisbane I hear that Carrie Moore wears
the Tyson diamonds a good deal, and very hand-
some ones they are—the big sun-ray star and a
dazzling swallow in full flight. Carrie is a great
favorite with the Brisbane public, but rigidly
sticks to her rule of “no suppers”—to the keen
disappointment of many gilded youths. When
last heard of privately, Palotta had been giving a
drag-picnic to the chorus-girls, whom she took
out, a hen-convention of them, amongst the
“wattle-blooms.” The latest Brisbane woman-
club is the Nymphea Rowing Club. The dames
and demoiselles attached thereto gave a concert
recently, and—well, if they can’t row better than
they can concertise, says my correspondent, they
will never set the Brisbane River afire. However,
they are all nice girls, so much can be excused
them.
Palotta and the rest passed through Sydney to
Adelaide last week. Grace had five fresh scalps
in the shape of Brisbane offers of marriage.
A lesson in toleration and good feeling was
given lately at Strahan, on the Tasmanian West
coast. The Anglican Bishop, Dr. Montgomery,
who was visiting the town, was entertained at a
social gathering ; he was met at the train and wel-
comed to Strahan by J. J. Gaffney, the R.C.
member for the place, and the local Catholic choir
gave a concert at the social in honor of the Bishop !
A year ago, when the new R.C. church was being
opened, the Anglican rector lent the benches from
his church, and went to hear the Catholic Bishop
preach. This is something like getting down to
original Christianity. By the way, sheep of the
R.C. fold are usually very kindly treated by
Anglican and other sheep when they want new
pens or when their stock-route is a bit dry and
grassless, and it is well that Holy Church should
show signs of reciprocity. A priest in a Vic.
township lately helped to fence a heretic church
and to give it a bell by taking a large party of his
parishioners to a bazaar got up for the purpose,
where he and they went in right heartily for bran-
pies, cosies, fancy aprons, and home-made mar-
malade.
Donald Macdonald and manager Geach passed
through Sydney to Melbourne last week. D.M.
has lost his besieged look, also the illusion (he
never had) about London keeping golden niches in
the temple of fame for Australian geniuses. London
he loathed, rural England he loved. The book-
market for the new writer is as dead as the share-
market, but with less hope of resurrection, for the
sixpenny edition of works of fore-front writers lies
a heavy boulder on its sepulchre. Audiences,
chiefly Australian, rolled up to hear D.M. warring
(Englishmen hate the war now ; they say, we are
paying the bill); but his expenses were fearsome,
and he had had enough of the pointer, the pictures,
and the charge of the Devons. So back he has
come to the bosom of his family, and the service
of the Granny in whose cause he has shed much
flesh.
“Rolfe Boldrewood ” Browne has been telling a
Melb. interviewer that he started “on his own”
at 18 years of age with a few hundred head of
cattle,_ a dray, and one pound in his purse. He
and his animals browsed their way from North
Melbourne to the Port Fairy district, where the
lad took up a “run.” The squatter-novelist’s
family have done themselves rather well in the
way of marriages. His sisters are, respectively,
Lady Darley and Lady Scratchley. One of his
sons married a much-moneyed Chirnside lady,
another wedded a daughter of Gerard Smith, a
Westralian Governor. Young Captain Victor
Scratchley, his nephew, who lately got the D. 5.0.,
is announced as engaged to Miss Anna Harvey of
Singapore.
The venerable Mrs. Gother Mann, who died
last week at the age of 95, of pneumonia, was one
of the most loveable and loved of women. Except
her last illness, she never had an ailment of any
seriousness, and her mental faculties were alert to
the last. During Commonwealth carnival she
went about the city brisk as a girl, viewing the
sights. At the Admiral’s garden-party for the
Cornwalls the Royalties were genuinely interested
in the presentation of Mrs. Mann, and kept her
seated between them, talking for half-an-hour.
Admiral Beaumont was a constant visitor at her
home at Greenwich. He used to call her the
“Good Angel of the Navy,” and his affection for
her dated from the time he came here as a young
lieutenant in the Blanche. Mrs. Mann’s husband,
who died in ’99 soon after the 60th celebration of
the anniversary of his marriage (he was at one
time Governor of Cockatoo Island), was an Indian,
Australian, and Imperial pensioner. One of his
brothers is the last-surviving member of the
Leichhardt expedition. Mrs. Mann’s family of
12 children are all living and vigorous.
The Australian acquaintances of Lord Francis
Hope and his wife, May Yohe, will be somewhat
shocked to read four or five columns which appear
in San Francisco Chronicle of July 18.
N.S.W. Art Society’s exhibition was opened
without any fanfare of trumpets by the Admiral,
attended by a flag-lieutenant. The National
Gallery has bought three pictures, first and fore-
most being a big canvas, by Frank Mahony, full
of delightfully-painted cows, and entitled “A
Spring Morning.” Mr. Mahony’s enemies are
saying they expected something better from him,
whereas his friends aver that on this occasion he
has absolutely surpassed himself. Anyhow, the
trustees of the N.G. liked his cows in their setting
of hazy blue and bright green well enough to pay
£2OO for the “ Spring Morning ” and add it to the
three other Mahonys in the Gallery. In this mat-
ter the same weary old rival Art cliques’ vendetta
crops up once more, the Art Society being now
represented on the Gallery trust by two practising
artists, whereas the Society of Artists is
not directly represented at all. The Gallery’s
second purchase is an English subject by Hanson,
a water-color, “ The Weary Horses Seek the
Stream.” A languid, mother-of-roses sunset
throws up a darkly grey and violet foreground,
with horses, farm buildings and broken fences.
The Gallery’s third investment, “The Millstream,”
is a black-and-white oil by Piguenit. Mr.
Lister-Lister’s big canvas holds a fine ex-
panse of coast-line, sea and sky, and Mr. Nelson
Illingworth’s bust of Primate Smith is the best
thing of its kind in the rooms and pleases even
the prelate’s greatest friends.
Next week will be a crowded one—the Aus-
tralian and Union Club balls, St. Vincent’s ball,
a lot of private dances, the Darleys’ garden-party
and the races. Items of interest since I last wrote
are “Lucia” at Her Majesty’s, and the opening
of N.S.W. Art Society’s Annual Exhibition.
Madame Sapio goes so beautifully, musically mad
that you long for her to bite all soprani of your
acquaintance and hope that the virus will “ take.”
She invests the distraught bride with so much
charm, and manages so artistically her cas-
cade of hair and the lawn and lace of her
dressing - gown, and raves in suoh wonder-
ful mad trills and roulades, that I shouldn’t
wonder if she sets the fashion of tem-
porary insanity for brides. The flute obbligato
must have been played on a magic flute, and the
audience leant forward eagerly trying to spot the
wizard flautist, Signor Amadio, in the crowded
orchestra. As for the harp, with its golden
chords, I’ll willingly be a party to kidnapping the
harpist, Signorina Sivocci, when she attempts to
return to her native Italy.
Signorina Marchi was a revelation as regards
appearance. Hitherto her roles have disguised
her height, beautiful figure, and face. As Alice,
her black hair, ivory face and long close dress of
white and crimson made her an eye-satisfying
portrait.
The colour scheme and the grouping of the
wedding guests is beautifully balanced and shown
up by Dani in his inky clothes taking the centre
of the stage. How well Dani and his countrymen
manage their swords and scabbards !
In a certain shop in George-street there’s a seal-
skin cloak, which a devoted hubby brought from
London for his wife. She wore it twice, and
growled all the time —sealskin clothes have a trick
of making most persons look a bit dropsical—so he
took it and hied him off with it to a fashionable
shop, where it is now for sale.
I saw a picture the other day of Edward VII.
playing his pianola. He has considerable musical
taste, and is said to play well, but can’t do any
with the piano itself. Lord Hopetoun also has
a pianola, and nearly everyone of note in Syd-
ney has or is going to get one. The pianola is not
merely an automatic piano-player. You put it to
the front of your piano, where its 65 fingers do
the playing. It is operated on by means of pedals,
stops, the tempo-regulator and perforated roll of
music. You sit at it, and play according to your
musicianly instinct; you supply the expression,
but anyone who does not know one note from
another can play with technical correctness any
piece written for the piano. Paderewski himself
—so goes the legend—does not disdain the use of
the pianola. You may see and hear it at Henry
Birge and Co.’s recital-rooms in the iEolian
Building, 323 George-street, where afternoon
recitals of pianola and seolian music are given.
I was much misled by the correspondent who
contributed the “ information ” that last year’s
St. Vincent’s Ball would have been a financial
failure were it not for the late Mr. Dalton’s aid.
The Committee did not need any after-aid because
instead of a deficit there was a surplus of
£221 2s. 7d, The art union tickets for a Singer’s
sewing machine are included in the gallery tickets,
2s. each.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ A matter of State rights which may yet
involve Australia in civil war is now raging between
the members of the Federal and State Houses.
This all-important question concerns the plucking
of the flowers in the gardens of Melbourne Parlia-
ment House. The Federal members, being on
the spot, scoop the beds about 9 a.m., and stroll
down Collins-street decked out like participants
in a Greek vintage-festival. Later on, the mere
commonplace local M’s.P. come along and find
nothing but buds which will not be out until the
morrow—whereupon they complain bitterly,
arguing that though Parliament House has been
lent to the Commonwealth, the gardens do not go
with it. The Commonwealth House, however,
maintains that the grounds are included, and the
matter stands there. The other day one exas-
perated local legislator, finding all the flowers
gone, set to work on the buds. He had just
finished when a Commonwealth member came on
the scene and threatened to give him in charge as
a trespasser. Then there were ‘a few words,’
and promises of violence, and eventually the State
man was warned to keep off the Commonwealth
grass in future. He went off threatening to test
the matter in the Supreme Court.
“Kingston, the Emperor of Silence in the
House of Reps., has one or two soft corners in his
heart for the beautiful in art and nature, and says
something more than ‘ Humph ! ’ to his beard
when taking the air of the country in its spring
bloom, or the tour of the Art Galleries in a stolen
half-hour. Women do not find him grumpy, and
some artists pin much faith to his sympathies.
Only in the House does he sit silent, unmoved,
impassive, stubborn, impervious to anything but
the sharp-shooting of Reid’s retorts from the
Opposition benches. Then Kingston levels his
sly eyes at the figure opposite, smiles, and watches
the round man’s antics covertly.
“At the Arnold matinee, Edith Crane, whose
fine figure showed up well in brown, sported pink
hydrangeas in her hat, and kept attentive hubby
Tyrone Power busy donning and doffing her
wrap 3. Mrs. Norman Menzies (Dorothy Vane),
in pale pink strapped with black, had white vest
and under-sleeves, and one of her favorite black-
feathered hats. Several Arnold mummers not in
the cast came and went. Edyth Latimer, with a
garland of spring flowers round her flat straw hat,
was with Miss Barrett. The latter young lady is
pale, thin, and academic-looking, thanks to her
pince-nez, I suppose. She doesn’t act, but has
come along to keep pa company.
‘ ‘ Matinees are regarded as deliciously thrilling
occasions by girls who have acquaintances in
‘ pro.’ circles. See an actor step forward to greet
Miss What’s-Her-Name; she reverentially rises
to her feet, and worships with her eyes. Soon as
he’s gone, her girl-chums close round her, and the
group excitedly discusses the hero’s lightest word.
Every woman has her beat. One is interested in
the arrival of war-ships, another woman lives for
Christian Endeavour conferences. It’s an ill-wind
that doesn’t blow excitement to some petticoat-
wearer or other.
“Churchy fogies, with their wives and daughters,
rushed Barrett’s ‘ Sign of the Cross ’ just as they
did formerly when the Knight-Ferrar co. served
up the same mixed brew. It’s evidently not the
play’s moral lesson our good friends are after.
They never even dream of sampling a nice, clean,
really ‘ moral ’ little play like ‘ A Message from
Mars,’ with its teaching of unselfishness, or ‘The
Professor’s Love Story,’ which gently decries
cynicism and black views of the motives of others.
Neither can the ‘unco guid’ be drawn to ‘The
Sign ’ by its sermons, hymns and prayers ; such
as chese are always on tap elsewhere, Sundays and
week-nights, at the cost of an offertory threepence.
Take away from ‘ The Cross ’ its ‘ moral teaching,’
plus scripture readings and scriptural songs—
what’s left ? Just a bit of color not exactly pink.
So one can’t help suspecting that local saints taka
less interest in ‘ early Christians ’ than they do in
late Pagans—midnight Marcus and his would-be
deeds of darkness.
“In the ‘ Message from Mars,’ dark, handsome
ituby Ray looks tall and slim in dainty white
dance-frock, one pink rose nestling in her hair,
knotted low on her neck, and another in her cor-
sage, cut well off wearer’s shoulders. Then she
throws on a pink evening-wrap while ‘ Auntie ’
(Nellie Mortyne) veils her sequined black with a
cerise cap. I like the prevalent theatre cloak—•
shawl-shaped, i.e., pointed at the back and slop-
ing up towards the shoulders. The most graceful
specimens are pretty long, knee-deep, say ; and
edged with white flounce of pleated silk-muslin.
“The Daffodil Show of the Horticultural
Society is to be opened at the Town Hall on
Thursday (sth). Ceremonial rites are fixed for 2
p.m., and the show, which has been put back a
week from the date first decided upon, will last
until Saturday evening.
“ The Dante Society, after a long period of
depression and sundry differences of opinion, had
its first flutter for the year on Friday. The
toiling committee, wearing a large and handsome
smile, breathed sighs of pardonable satisfaction as
the valuable social fish so painstakingly ground-
baited rose to the surface in twos, threes and half-
dozens. A juvenile detachment from Cliveden,
half the Consuls in Melbourne, and every Federal
big-wig from Forrest downward who hadn’t been
already entrapped to orate at the Salvarmy’s
big Town Hall spree, came, saw, and were con-
quered.
“ A Melbourne curiosity is a female crank who
spends all her time wandering from one ser-
vants’ registry to another, looking for a billet.
She is known to all the keepers of such institu-
tions, arid is constantly dropping in at each to see
if they have secured the desired place. She is
very well off, and is cute enough to invest her
money well; but in the matter of her quest for a
position as housemaid in a quiet Christian family
where she can wear a nice white cap and apron
she is as mad as a March hare. The crank is
known to all the servant girls of Melbourne, and
her eccentricities are considered a welcome break
to the tedium of long waits in stuffy offices.
A new kind of terror to domestic peace is
amongst us. A certain tall, tawny-headed
charmer, whose home is up South, is proving her
perverted sense of humor. She will telephone
some shining light of Church or State and inquire
“ Is Mr. Blank in?” “Yes, but he’s at dinner,”
comes the reply. “Well, please give him this
message : ‘Miss Fewclothes received his note, and
is sorry she can’t have supper with him to-night,
as she’s not very well.’ ” To another goes the
message : “ Miss Fewclothes did not receive that
promised packet from the jeweller’s. Will Mr.
Dash please see about it ?” These intimations are
often delivered to, or in the presence of, Dash or
Blank’s wife. Should one of the innocent injured
husbands (a well-known P.M.) get the damsel
before him
“A large showy matron and her black Pomera-
nian poodle gave a one-act-comedy performance
in Collins-st. on a recent afternoon. The
heavy dame stepped down from a high dog-cart,
at the Vienna, and set her pet on his paws, ex-
pecting the dutiful to walk in her train. But the
scamp saw some other gay dogs, perhaps, and
made off to join them. The matron angrily
stamped her foot, and enjoined master Pom. to
follow her; but he took no notioe. Then his
mistress indulged in a common dog whistle, at
which all the other pups (various breeds) along
Collins-st. _ stopped and looked. But this didn’t
allure the inky pet; so at last, in high dudgeon,
she strode down street, snatched up her rebellious
poodle from the backyard breed, and carried him
in disgrace into the Vienna. Probably his after-
noon rations were cut off.
“ ‘Mutual Improvement Classes ’ are the latest
emanation from the brains of the daughters of
Minerva. A well-known Melbourne woman is
striving to gather together some of her worthy
sisters to pitch an Improvement tent on one of the
flats of the city, and set an example to the Social
Prodigals in the old publican spirit. The idea
was hatched some time ago, but the work of turn-
ing out substantial results is yet in an early and
very tender age. To join such a class is to make,
as it were, a public repentance in a hair shirt, and
women, as a rule, consider hair-shirts very unbe-
coming.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
Art is a sensitive plant, and only flourishes
in a congenial climate. In the large cities of
Europe, where beautiful women adopt the flexible
P.D. figure and shed its influence upon the recep-
tive soul of man, musician, painter, and poet
thrill the world with divine harmonies in sound,
sentiment, and color. Such an inspirational influ-
ence has the P.D. that the King of the Belgians,
the keenest judge of figures in all the world, gave
the order of the Legion of Honor to the artistic
designer of that popular corset.*
An Australian military officer, referring
to the Hawthorn (Vic.) stieking-up case:—“ They
ought to look among our returned men. I don’t
say any of them did it, but there are dozens who
would take the job on if it were suggested to
them. Country fellows, tame and quiet enough
when they left here, have become really dangerous
men through their year or so at the war. A cam-
paign is not calculated to increase one’s respect for
life or property, and a lot of our men proved them-
selves only too able learners.” On this theory,
hey for the noble art of Bushranging in Australia
during the next few years.

The See Govt, selected M. Clarke (IVL.ij. A..
for Bega and the “Father of the House’’) for a
seat on the Public Works Committee. This was
intended to make up for ignoring bis claims to a
portfolio when the Ministry was formed. But, on

upside-down with his proposed tramway grab, is
a local patent-agent. His principals are the
London Westinghouse Co., which has already

Ex-Premier Parkes’ favorite monologue
for years was, “If I were offered my life over
again I would not take it.” Ex-Premier George
Reid said the same thing of Tiis life the other day.
To steal Parkes’ politics was bad enough, but to
steal his introspective vanity is barefaced robbery.
Abraham Cohen, of Tam worth (N.S.W.),
who died lately in England, cut up £110,624
among his family, leaving not one cent directly
to charities or public benevolences. He evidently
heard these crying, for he tells his children that
he trusts them to do the fair thing in those
respects.

every Dunstan assumes his own fitness to run a
daily paper amuses people “in the know.
.Reasonably-successful parsons grow on every

pearls beyond price.

Federal Defence Minister Forrest is com-
ing out in style since taking up his residence in
Melbourne. He may now be seen driving up
Collins-st. with a coat-of-arms on his gorgeous
carriage. His coachman and footman wear a
hybrid kind of kharki uniform with facings the

bigger and fatter every day.
The son of a long-departed Australian
statesman used often to be annoyed when intro-
duced to strangers by—“ Glad to meet you, but
you’ll never be the man your father was.” On
one occasion to this familiar formula he retorted
with much energy, “I know that —because my
father was chief among a million ; but anyhow
I’m a d d sight better man than you ! ” Then
ensued a painful silence.

him, but to show how hard the stone is !

“ Seppy ” Stephen, who lately died in
England, was one of the late ex-Chief-Justice
Alfred Stephen’s Sydney-born sons. Tbe Chief-
Justice was father of 18 children —nine boys and
nine girls, five of the boys being born in Hobart
and four in Sydney. It is peculiar enough to re-
call, as the old man often did, that of the nine
girls five were born in Sydney and four in Hobart.
“ Seppy ” followed law, but as a solicitor. He
sat in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly for a fevv years, and
afterwards in the Leg. Council, but did little to
perpetuate his memory. Of late years he resided
in London, where he posed as a light of
the Colonial Institute. The deceased was one of
a class who, though of Australian birth, care
little for Australia, but affect to be more Eng-
lish than the English themselves.

soribed, and certainly had he been

peerage and its Lord Mayor’s show
business. He, a peer, has laughed at
and satirised the airs assumed by

how the 8.8.8.’s hated him for it!
So they smote him withe (new double
word) last Trial by Peers and three
months in the debtors’ gaol. For
making Lady Scott’s daughter his wife
years ago it has cost R. £30,000 (he
says) in litigation alone, and his name
has (unfairly) been dragged through
the lowest bed of scandal’s sewers.

The Tasmanian Govt, is so pleased with
its Agent-General, Alfred Dobson, that it has re-
newed his appointment for another year. Mr.
Dobson has had a succession of the sweets of office
—Attorney-General, Speaker, Solicitor-General,
Crown Solicitor, and now A.-G. A sound lawyer,
and popular at the Bar, he would long since have
been on the bench, but for the feeling that there
might be too much Dobson in power at the
capital.
The N.S.W. Government gold medal with
which Manager Lamport, who defended the
bank at Henty (N.S.W.), is to be pre-
sented, is a real distinction. It has never been
bestowed upon anyone for an act which has not
stood the test of time. The medal was instituted
in the bad old bushranging days and has, so far,
been conferred upon not more than (say) a dozen
men. Manager Lamport is entitled to rank with
any of these. The man who, while covered with a
revolver held by a masked man at two or three
paces, will advantage himself, as it were, of a
flicker of the highwayman’s eyelash, to open the
drawer in front of him, grasp his own revolver and
take his chance, has got real grit.
Joseph Cook* of Boston “ Monday Lee-
ture”fame, died last June, in America. He is
doubtless enjoying himself “over there” re-slaying
theinfidels, free-tliinkers, and loose thinkers, whom
he so repeatedly scalped (in their absence) on his
own platforms here. A born bounder, pounder,
expounder, and £4O-er (he often asked, and some-
times got, £4O a week when on a lecturing tour),
Cook used to bulk large between mankind and
real religion.

from Kew Asylum before Vic. Supreme Court a
couple of years ago, is no longer in custody. He
gave his parole to the late superintendent and was

an 1 being thus freed from responsibility the eon-
fi me took French leave—and nobody seems to

In the interests of the peace, order and
good government set forth in the Commonwealth
Act, it is necessary that the refreshment bars at
Melbourne Parliament House be immediately
abolished. Certain prominent members, especi-
ally some from adjoining States, spend more
of their time in the bars than they do in the
chamber, and the results are very obvious. The
Vic. members would doubtless be in the same
boat, but then they are not away from their wives.
Mr. David Mitchell, barrister, in handing
over to N.S.W. Govt, his magnificent library of
books concerning Australia, made certain reason-
able conditions as to proper accommodation for it,
with which the authorities, after taking delivery
of the library, are slow to comply. Mr. Mitchell
grows importunate and the matter is getting on to
the official nerves. The library donor is not the
first Mitchell to worry N.S.W. officialdom. Sur-
geon Mitchell, his father, connected with the
Macquarie-street hospital 70 years ago, kept up a
fight in the little community for years. He fought
with the Governors, acting-Governors, and other
medical men ; appealed from authority to author-
ity, and finally won damages to the extent of £lOO
from his arch-foe, Inspector-General Thompson.
Miss Rose Scott, well-known in Sydney woman-
suffrage and benevolent circles, is grand-niece of
the old warrior-surgeon.
When Sydney Mayor Graham and other
Caledonians enthuse about the “beautiful city
of Edinbro,” one is reminded of a yarn of
Henry Parkes’. Upon a political campaign once
the Hairy Bismarck occupied the same room with
another prominent N.S.W. (Scawteh) politician,
and during the night the Father 6f Federation
was awakened by agonised groans from his room-
mate. The latter explained that he had had a
most awful dream. Parkes asked if he had
dreamt that his wife and family were dead. “Oh,
much worse than that ! ” was the answer. “ Well
then, did you dream you were in hell?” “Oh,
worse than even that ! ” replied Scotty. “Well,
hang it all, man !—what.dwf you dream ? ” “ Oh,
I dreamt I was at home in Scotland again !” The
book that Parkes borrowed this yarn from can
still be met with occassionally on the second-hand
book-stalls. It dates from about the time when
Sam. Johnson remarked that the fairest prospect
that ever opened to a Scotchman was the road to
London.
John Fisko, of Harvard, the American
Spencerian, is dead, surviving by only a couple of
months Joe Cook, whom, 20 years ago, he so un-
mercifully flagellated for “ theological charla-
tanism.” When, in the early, 80’s, Joseph was
lecturing in Melbourne, a local agnostic reprinted
Fiske’s article on him from the North American
Review, and thousands of copies were distributed at
the doors. This was so pointed that Cook couldn’t
ignore it, but contented himself by saying that
the author had regretted its publication. As Dr.
Fiske included the article in the volume of his
collected essays, published last year, the pro-
fundity of his “regret ” and the true inwardness
of Cook’s veracity are made manifest.
Thinking of the late “Bill” Forrester’s
fine physique, one is bound to attribute his early
death, from paralysis, to the game

pluckily. As a peaceful agri-
culturist, or an orthodox
commercial person, astute
William might have lived
20 years longer without
getting half as much en-
joyment out of his
innings. Or if he had
been a closer and more

sity for bringing off coups
would not have been so

With all his apparent suc-

his outlook probably
being even more blue
than it was prior to
V his first Melbourne
Cup success in ’97.
Everyone has heard the
story of how Forrester,
when he saw Gaulus and The Grafter
racing down the straight together,
lengths ahead of the field, rushed
down to the telegraph office in the
Flemington paddock and sent a wire
to his wife in Sydney, saying merely,
“Am out of my trouble.” Which is
surely one of the prettiest racing stories ever
told. The lucky man’s first thought was to let
his best friend and dependant know a little
earlier than she could otherwise get the news
that the sun was shining on them again.

’Tis a far cry to the days of Richard
Cromwell, the spineless son of old Ironside
Oliver, but in Auckland (M.L.) Library is the
document, witnessed by many nobles, by which
that quiet, cow-like young man heaved the crown
of England out of his family for ever. It was
presented to Auckland Library by Hori Grey,
with an understanding that the history of how it
came into his possession was not to be divulged
until after the Great Proconsul’s death. That
history would make interesting reading, and the
Auckland Library Trust might turn an honest
penny by publishing it.

Recently deceased, Thus. Slierwin, of
Whittlesea (Vic.), aged 82. He was born at
Parramatta in 1819, and arrived in Victoria in ’39.
With other civilians he took part in the capture
of the first bushrangers in Victoria, one of whom,
Williams, was shot dead at Whittlesea in ’42, the
remainder of the gang being hanged.
An elderly man, not long arrived from
England, was admitted to Hamilton (Vic.) hos-
pital, and, in reply to questions, stated that he had
no relatives in Australia, his elder brother, who
came out many years before, being dead, he
believed, as he had not been heard from since
1860. A few days later the Englishman, now
convalescent, met in the grounds another patient
in whose countenance he recognised something
familiar. Questions and answers were rapidly ex-
changed, the result being that the men found
that they were the two brothers who had not
heard of one another for over 40 years.
Bishop Colenso writing from S’Afnca in
February, 1881:
Here are the English papers reaching us full of ravings
about the treachery, cruelty and bloodthirstiness of the
Boers, of which, when the facts are thoroughly known
and fairly considered, hardly a trace remains. Also, the
accusation against the Boers of ill-treating the natives
under their rule must be greatly exaggerated. To all
appearance they even preferßoer rule to our own.
Even if some of the Boers are brutes, there
can’t be the least doubt about the savagery of
some of the British troops. Richard Harding
Davis, the American, who was there, described in
Scribner’s how some Lancers at Elandslaagte
killed men who had thrown down their arms and
held up their hands. One, a giant farrier, ran
two men through and had them on his lance at
the same time ; he was killed himself a few days
later. Davis says Joubert excused this savagery
on the ground that the English were not used to
fighting civilised people, but he had the sworn
testimony of several burghers taken for all that.

A noted cattle-duffer has just been laid
by the heels in a Far-Western town, the luck
that has kept him out of gaol for a lifetime
having deserted him at a critical moment. One
of his smartest deals was when he met with a tra-
velling mob of fat cattle, in which pleuro had
broken out, half-a-score being in a dying state.
The drover said our friend could have the hides
and horns on condition of his destroying the car-

Groan from a "Victorian country paper :
We must apologise to our readers for the late appear-
ance of the Bunyip this week, which is due to the fact of
our being left in the lurch by one of our employees, who
failed to turn up to work on Monday morning, and who
has not been seen or heard of hereabouts since. He was
never over-industrious, and left us in a similarly sudden
manner just before last Xmas. His name is Smith, and
he wrote to us about a couple of months ago expressing„
contrition for what he had previously done, and stating
that he was once more hard up after putting all his cloth-
ing into “ soak ” with his favorite “ uncle.” As we had
in the interim several worthless fellows, we decided to
give Smith another chance, and for our kindness towards
him we have been rewarded by having our work thrown
back several days, and it is highly probable that the
Bunyjp will be almost as late next week also. We have
issued a warrant for Smith’s arrest, and next week he will
probably have to explain his conduct to a local bench of
magistrates.
“ Westral ” : Re the lydies who “ travel ”
their charms (8., 25/5/’01) almost the only
“business” done, outside of the larger
towns on the Westrdliun goldfields, is—
barring Japs—by women who “go on
circuit.” The miner on the monthly pay-
day, after settling his store and pub.
accounts, starts in to get through the
balance, and consequently has very little
money until next pay-day. (I’m referring
to towns out-back from Menzies.) There-
fore, there is not sufficient inducement
•for business lydies to settle down in any
one place. So they get the dates of the
different “pays,” and arrive just when
everybody is temporarily wealthy. I
have got to know them well from their
frequent appearance on the roads ; the faces
are as familiar as those of the “commer-
cials.” One girl, in particular, goes as far
as Lawler’s, starting from Menzies; and what
amazes me is the fresh, healthy, English
look she always wears, notwithstanding the
hard travelling and the hard life.

“Vril” : Witnessed, on Peak
Downs (Q.), what I take to be an uncom-
mon sight, viz., a full-grown wild emu
pursuing a forlorn young calf. Emu, with
a good bit in hand, was about half a length
behind, while the calf was “all out ” and bel-
lowing dismally.
“C.F.”: Train-travelling, the other day,
from Geraldton to Perth, I had pointed out to me
on Minginew platform the nigger whom the
recently-murdered Durack, it is alleged, compelled
to shoot some other blacks, For this the native
got 15 years; but, in consideration of good
conduct in gaol, he is now on duty as black-
tracker under a sort of ticket-of-leave.

“Trotter”: In Mackay and Bundaberg
districts (Q.) the white man has, in the Hindoo, a
worse enemy than the Kanaka. Last year Tommy
Tanna was getting 15s. a week and rations ; now
he is being superseded by Ram Chundur, who is
fencing, ringbarking, corn-pulling, cane-cutting,
stumping, &e., at 7s. to 10s. a week, and “find
himself.” The white man is simply out of the
question in these districts, all the work going to
Hindoos, Kanakas, Japs, Chows, and Malays.

“Tom Colliiis ” on an appar-
ently interminable question :
He “Xerxes” (8., G/7/’l) : When a
small boy I had an ancient fliut-lock horse-
pistol, with which I missed many magpies
and ’possums. The mainspring and pan-
cover spring of this weapon had botli become so relaxed by
half-a-century’s service thtA their aetion was insufficient
to produce fire by the operation of thermo-dynamics. Con-
sequently, I fixed in the flint-holder a bit of wood, per-
forated to receive a stump off the business-end of a Bell
and Black ; and this served the original purpose. Here
was introduced the principle of chemical combination.
If I had employed one of my mates to start her off with a
burning-glass while I took aim, that would have meant
the enlistment of optics in the production of the vital
spark. Three distinct branches of physical science, you
see; and the first is the only one we have to deal with,
friction being an exercise of dynamics, not of chemistry
or optics. But all are fish to “ X.” Hear ye him:
“ ‘ T.C.’ might as well argue that a cold lens could not
generate heat enough to produce fire, or that spontaneous
combustion is impossible.” Well, despite the fact that
Faraday ignited gunpowder by means of a lens
composed of solid ice, “T.C. ” does argue that
it is not the function of a lens to “generate”
heat. The heat refracted by a lens has already
been generated in the sun’s mass. And “ T.C.’s” way of
rejecting spontaneous combustion would be to mix, for
“X.’s” information a pyroligneous compound, which
could not be preserved except in a closed vessel, as it
would ignite on exposure to the atmosphere. But these
facts don’t reach even the dignity of side-issues, nor do
they make the task of producing fire by friction of two
sticks any less “ arduous and momentous.” Gov. Phillip
writes of the blacks of Port Jackson “. . . they are
seldom seen without a fire, or a piece of wood on fire,
which they carry with them from place to place, and in
their canoes, so that I apprehend they find some difficulty
in producing fire by any other means with which they are
acquainted.” To which remark, Becke and Jeffery ap-
pend this foot-note “ Phillip was wrong in this conclu-
sion. The Australian blacks are adepts in producing fire
by the friction of two sticks.” (“ Admiral Phillip,” p. 62).
Here P. writes from personal observation ; B. and J. from
the hearsay testimony of that large class which loves the
maivellous for its own sake ; and this seems to sum up
the present situation. A saddening disclosure, however,
is that hazy perception which takes me as pronouncing
the friction method “ impossible” ; whereas my first par.
described the process as performed by the blacks of the
Upper Yarra. I protested—and still protest—only against
a slovenliness of skite which cannot but make the judicious
grieve.
The Bulletin is beginning to realise that fire can
be produced by rubbing two indignant contribu-
tors together, whatever else may fail.

“ Tonbur ” : Recently camped with a
Russian coffee-cockie on the Daintree River
(N.Q.). He could hardly sp yak a word of Eng-
lish. Fed me on stewed mussels, maize porridge
and potatoes. Housed me in a room with six
bunks, as in a forecastle, occupied by the family,
whilst stray dogs and pigs camped underneath.
Twelve Russian and Scandinavian settlers reside
there, seven married and five hatters. There is
no school, no church, no police protection. A
small “ half decker ” calls once a fortnight
with the mails from Port Douglas, and takes
away a few bags of coffee or corn. A tribe of
half-wild naked niggers dwell there; big, ferocious-
looking brutes, the terror of the settlers. The
land is magnificent, coffee, maize, hemp, spices,
sugar, tobacco, bananas, paw-paws, grenadillas,
&c., flourish there, and there’s an abundance of
timber —box, mahogany, cedar. The curse of the
absentee-landlord comes in here. Nearly 12,000
acres (cut up in blocks of 80 to 040) are lying idle
—the pick of the river. But in N.Q. the good
land, when not occupied by Chows, is mostly idle.

There is a remote chance of the pink and
white terraces atßotomahana(M.L.) being restored
in the distant future. A geyser in the lake is play-
ing up to a stupendous height, and the steam from
another at the top of a hill ascends from 800 to
1000 ft., and spreads out like a cloud, the expelled
water sweeping down the mud which accumulated
at the time of the Tarawera eruption. There is a
thin coating of pink silica on the mud, and it
is believed that the terraces were not destroyed,
but merely covered. The geyser now showing
activity is the cauldron which existed on top of
the terraces, and if the same amount of debri
continues to disappear daily these lost attractio
may in time be revealed to view.
• * . ,
At Reclfem station (Sydney), t’other day,
writes a correspondent, some of O’Sullivan’s pets,
day-laborers under the minimum wage, were
shifting old railway-sleepers a short distance —six
men to a sleeper ; two at each end with a cross-
stick, and two crossing hands in the middle.
Now, in loading up their waggon in the bush,
when the sleepers (with the sap in them) are much
heavier), the haulers-in almost invariably do all
the work single-handed, and shifting never takes
more than two men.
* * * m
A late visitor to Chatham Islands describes
it as a model place. A magistrate (who rarely has
anything to do) and three J’s.P. act as school and
licensing committee year after year. There are
no road boards, no voting, no electoral roll, no
rates or taxes (the dog-tax is evaded with great
unanimity, and the land-tax is never collected);
there’s no poverty, rescue homes, refugees,
neglected children, or criminals. There are two
drink-stores, but little drunkenness. Population,
400, and everyone on the island owns a horse.

“ YV.B.” : Met a man t’other day 100
miles from Cloncurry (Q.) who was walking to
Brisbane to get the old-age pension. Had been 40
years out-back and was 76 years old. I told him
the Bill hadn’t passed yet. “ Well,” he replied,
“ it’ll surely have passed by the time I get there.”

Dear B.—Am not making “J.W.C.”
(8., 17/8/’01) any offer, suitable or otherwise. Let
him go to the Hon. Crick and blossom into a
national benefactor with many beans, or own to
being an irresponsible idiot. Provided a bag of
pollard were sufficient to dose rabbits on a given
square mile, the thing might be done with a
poison so slow in its effects that the last bunny
would have time to stow his dose before the first
began to feel sick ; otherwise there are always
some with sufficient sense to leave the stuff alone
as soon as it begins to disagree with their pals,
and this evqn when they are starving. After suc-
cessful poison-operations, rabbits escaping will lie
close, often for days, badly scared by the squeal-
ing of the poisoned, and thus the novice will think
he’s got the lot. Perhaps that’s what’s up with
“J.W.C.” If not, Crick and fortune protrude,
and up goes the price of pollard. — Perdu.
* * * •
“Rockhampton”: Thus an enthusiastic
scribe who travelled with the Yorks : “ Wonder-
ful country, happy people, no poor, no beggars.”
Well, Queensland is at present on the verge of
bankruptcy. The West is filled with workless
men and half-starved, careworn women. Glad-
stone’s railway has put some 500 men. out of work ;
Lake’s Creek meat-works are practically closed ;
Mt. Morgan is, or has been, on the verge of
shutting-down ; Clermont district is deserted ;
Chillagoe is a frost; and the boodler is fighting
for Cloncurry. Out-back, we have towns containing
many grass-widows, who do strange things for a
few shillings, to obtain food for their children.
Immorality is rampant, and the Chow is the
white-haired boy ; while, on the coast, the yellow-
cum-black-cum brown agony supplants the white,
and drives him to the wilderness. “Yes, it is a
happy land!”

Copy of resolution posted in the Joss
House, Cairns —a township of a tricolored popu-
lation, which claims such men as Bamford and
Givens as representatives in the State Parliament
and House of Representatives :
To Messrs. R. Leon, Tan Bung Chong, Trustees Cairns
Joss House.
At the last meeting of my Council the matter of rating
portion 1 of allotment 18 of section 27, town of Cairns,
was considered, when it was unanimously resolved,
that, as the property is used exclusively as a place of
worship, no rates will in future be charged thereon, and
the rates now due will be cancelled.—l am, yours &c.,
, Mayor. October 24, 1892.
As man makes God in His own image, query:
What breed of god will the Cairns god be?

DARKY.
MY PUB. IS LIKE A GARDEN FAIR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My pub. is like a garden fair
With fragrant girls aglow,
Where malten flowers make bright the air,
And cooling streamlets flow.
Where jokes and jests flit to and fro,
Displaying plumage rare—
My pub. is like a garden fair
With fragrant girls aglow.
The golden pewters circle there,
The Hebes whisper low,
And on their perfumed pinions bear
This song, that all may know.
My pub. is like a garden fair,
With fragrant girls aglow.

Gallant Soldier’s Danger.
A gallant soldier of the Empire, Sergeant.
McNamara, late of His Majesty’s 82nd Regi-
ment, has given to a reporter upon request,
some particulars of his life. -
“You were present at the Siege and Fall of
Sebastopol, were you not?” said the reporter.
“Yes,” said Sergeant McNamara, “ I was then
in the 82nd Regiment. For that campaign I
received the British Medal and Clasp, and the
Turkish Medal. During the Indian Mutiny, I
took part in the relief of Lucknow under Sir
Colin Campbell for which I received the Lucknow
Medal and Clasp.”
“I would like you to tell me Sergeant, what
you consider the most trying experience you
have gone through.” .
“Well,” was the reply, “that occurred m
Australia in this wise. At the time—several
winters ago—l was confined to bed through a
severe cold, which developed into influenza. I
suffered from pains in shoulders and limbs,
heavy headaches, loss of appetite, sickness, and
a heavy, drowsy feeling, although I was quite
unable to sleep properly. The pains in my
head were so bad, that I could not lay the back
of it upon my pillow. Soup and tea were the
only nourishment I could take. Later on I
had another attack of influenza, and after that
I had two every winter. I was so weak and
ill with it that considering my advanced age
(sixty six) I should not have been surprised if
it had terminated fatally. To add to all my
troubles rheumatism afflicted me in the left
shoulder and in the foot, causing gnawing,
aching pains. I had medical treatment and
tried many advertised remedies as well, but they
proved useless.”
“You no doubt took the one remedy which
invariably wards off and cures influenza,” re-
marked the reporter.
“Yes, I did,” said Sergeant McNamara. “I
took Dr. Williams’ pink pills for pale people.
After using them for two weeks I felt consid-
erably benefited. I continued the treatment,
and now, notwithstanding my age and the late
inclement weather, I am hale and hearty and
have been free all this winter from influenza and
rheumatism. I have a good appetite, sleep well,
and am stronger and brighter than I have been
for years. I strongly recommend Dr. Williams’
pink pills to all those suffering from influenza
and rheumatism.”
Influenza may be warded off by taking Dr.
Williams’ pink pills, which cure its after effects
by enriching the blood, toning up the nerves and
strengthening the constitution. In this way they
also cure rheumatism, neuralgia, sciatica,
bronchitis, consumption, weak heart, diarrhoea,
consumption of the bowels and lungs, fits,
scrofula, paralysis, etc. Sold by chemists and
storekeepers and by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine
Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney, two and nine ; six
boxes fifteen and three, post free. Sergt.
McNamara resides at George-street, Bur wood,
Sydney.*

At the recent Melbourne
Denton Hat Mills’ feast chairman
Bruce stated that the Australian
rabbit was deteriorating—it now
needed two more rabbits to make a
hat than it did five years ago.
Housekeepers also notice that the
animals are much smaller than here-
tofore. The reason may be that for
years past the slaughter of rabbits
lias been so great that the animals
have not had time to reach maturity,
and breed before they are fit to pro-
duce good progeny, being shortly also
thereafter killed off themselves.
Climate can have little to do with it,
for the pet bunny of this land is
nearly double the size of his wild
brother.

Rabbit Bill touches the spot; it provides for local
control. There are nine-and-sixty ways ot killing
off rabbits, and every single one of them is tne
right way—and generally the only right way m
some particular district. It is foolishness for the
man in one part of the country to lay down the
law of rabbit-slaying to him m another, and that
has been at the root of most failures hitherto—that
and the want of provision to make destruction
general. If there is a doubt at all about Crick s
proposals it is in re compulsory rabbit-netting:
the local rabbit-governing body might be per-
mitted to also decide whether netting is r'ece.s-
sary, with right of appeal on the part either ot the
Minister or of any land-holder affected to the

* * *
The Kanaka, it has always been main-
tained, is imported by Queensland solely to foster
tropical agriculture. Yet a return shows that 707
of these aliens are specially exempted from the
provisions of the Pacific Islanders Acts and are
allowed to follow any occupations they choose !
As gardeners, grooms, etc they are becoming
quite common in the piebald State, and look like
settling down and becoming a recognised strain in
the local breed.
“ Will J.” : Wyche-
proof (Vic.) main thorough-
fare is a fine instance of
how far Departmental
audacity will go. The
railway-line not only runs
up the centre of the three-
chain street, but is without
any inclosure whatever.
Dogs, vehicles, drunks,
cows and babies loaf on
the rails impartially, and
strangers with frisky
horses, entering that town
when the train swerves
suddenly into the street,
are lucky if they don’t
meet with sudden death
by concussion with metal.
Runaways don’t count with
Wycheproof citizens now.
They only go to the door
when their verandah is
raked away by an escaped
vehicle, and then, as a
rule, they threaten the
utmost terrors of the law
if the stolen premises
aren’t instantly returned.

From letter recently received from Mt.
William (Vic.) goldfield
We can’t do much gold-digging just now. Everything
is under snow, so we go firewood-digging out of the snow.
The birds are very tame. As I write a robin is sharing
my fire with me, and sitting only 9in. off my foot. Ano-
ther small bird was on the table just now, picking the
fat out of my slush-lamp.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.

WHAT MAY HAPPEN TO A
FOREIGNER IN SYDNEY.
Scene. —A police-court.
Seak : “ Prisoner, you are charged with riotous
behaviour on the streets. What do you
say 1 ”
Prisoner : “ I don’t know what to say. I kiss
my wife and say ‘ Good-bye,’ and a police-
man come and take me ! ”
Beak : “ Kiss your wife ! Good Heavens ! I
1 fine you five shillings. If you had merely
blacked her eye I would have let you off!
'Patient: ft Then you think, doctor, that epidemics are governed
by the pseudo law of cycles 1”
Doctor : “ Pseudo ! —there’s nothing pseudo about it. Noth-
ing is more clearly established. Take the last two years :
.First we had the Jingo fever, then came thev plague, then
influenza, and now we are threatened with a
visitation of cricket! ”

Mr. Crick proposes to found a village for working people on the sand-dunes of
La Perouse. In moving the 2nd reading, of the Block-holders Bill, he said he pro-
posed to prohibit the issue of any licenses within this area. He also proposed to
call it Cnckville—(laughter)—and he hoped to the end of his days that he would
be able to represent it as its member in Parliament.
In the picture above is a view of a typical street in Crickville, while in the fore*
ground is seen Mr. Crick, of Crickville, “scouting” for a constituent at the Tillage
goose-pood.

yet more ignored by the medical profession than URIC ACID.
Nitrogenous waste creates a condition incompatible with health.
Remaining in the blood, it exerts an influence upon the nervous
system which we call auto-infection, causing neuralgia, impaired
vision, gout, rheumatism, eczema, constipation, dyspepsia,
bronchorrhoea, diabetes —mellitus and insipidus, chronic lithi-
asis, &c. When the uric acid is properly neutralised and
eliminated through the urinary and alimentary tract, all these
ailments produced by faulty metabolism disappear. Therefore
KUTNOW’S Improved Effervescent Carlsbad POWDER has
gained a pre-eminent favourite position with the most prominent
practitioners throughout the world. For it is composed of the
health-spending medicinal salts obtained direct from the waters

efficacious. It will be found an unexcelled alkaline and saline,

LIVER LOGIC
The Liver is the mainspring of the system.
When active it is the great purifier.
When sluggish it easily develops a poisonous
tendency.
The Liver both secretes and excretes.
It secretes about two or three pounds of bile daily.
It excretes impurities and waste materials.
The Liver is the sewage farm of the body.
If the Liver is sluggish it cannot exnel its refuse,
and as it must go somewhere it is absorbed back into
the blood, causing biliousness and often jaundice.
The Liver also makes from the blood a kind of
sugar, which, if healthy, is used up as nourishment.
If the sugar is excessive or inferior it cannot be so
used, it then escapes into the urine, causing diabetes.
A Torpid Liver develops gout and rheumatism in
predisposed subjects.
The absolute, incontestable remedy for all this,
and the one which never fails is Nature’s Great
Restorative, Kutnow’s Improved Effervescent Carls-
bad Powder.
This Powder is made by an ingenious expedient
from the health-giving medicinal springs of the
Continental Spas.
It is improved by removing all drastic or lowering
properties.
It is gentle and pleasant, but the greatest of all
Stimulators of the Liver.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Jingoism now slumps heavily. Almost
the whole Australian press, city and provincial, is
condemning “indulgence” passages to S’Africa.
Though S. Australia has adult suffrage,
the census shows that there are still 15 per cent,
of the adult males, exclusive of unenfranchised
Chinese, whose names are not on the rolls. The
81 census showed 20 per cent, of absentees.
The Afghan, who used to be only a camel-
driver, has now spread into the tailoring and
other trades in Westralia. The cholera and the
cheap nigger and a keg of butter in Gehenna are
three things whose spreading-power is unrivalled.
An analysis of the total paid in Royal
Commission fees (about £10,000) during the cur-
rency of Q. Parliament shows that the Labor
party’s adherents received about £350 of the
swag. The rest went to Ministerialists and Inde-
pendents.
From an English correspondent :
The treatment meted out by Vic.' Leg. Assembly to
Findley has been passed over in silence by most of the
London newspapers.
Jingo servility has its limits—even in the English
press.
J. H. Carrutliers’ opinion that, under the
Compulsory Arbitration Act, trade-unionism
should be made compulsory, contains a pregnant
idea. Sooner or later, and on terms reasonable to
present non-unionists, that question must become
a live topic. The gipsies of labor must be taken
into camp.
John Haynes, M.L.A., has a morion before
N.S.W. Assembly that fares and freights on the
Cornstalk railways—already run at a constant
loss—“ should be reduced one-half as a State
policy favouring inland development.” As
present charges don’t cover expenses this means
that one half the freight should be paid by
taxation on the community at large, by way of a
subsidy or bonus to the wool-growers and agri-
culturists. Freetrader John wants to artificially
coddle these classes, in fact, at the public expense,
and go in for all the horrors of Protection.
WATCHING THE PROGRESS OF REFORM.
Seeing tnat The Bulletin with “ Tatter-
sall’s” advertisement in it is strictly shut out
of Queensland, and it is necessary to print a
special edition minus that sinful notice—how
comes it that Sydney Telegraph, having “ Tat-
tersall’s” announcement conspicuously displayed,
circulates there quite freely ? Is it because that
paper doesn’t make unpleasant comments on
Philp and co.—or what ?
Perth (W.A.) Morning Herald puts the
old argument to prove that there is no real diffi-
culty in raising all Australian loans locally :
The loan expenditure of the various States totals some
£5,000,000 per annum, while their revenue is £35,000,000.
If the Australian Governments can raise £35,000,000 per
annum revenue in Australia, is it not possible for them to
raise one-seventh of that amount in loans ?
Quite true ! and there is also another question : if
the Australian Governments can raise £35,000,000
revenue and £5,060,000 loans in Australia, why
can’t they raise the whole £40,000,000 as revenue
in Australia and have no loans ?
Said that the Barton Cabinet is going to
try and put back the “seditiousand blasphemous”
clause into the Postal Bill—making postal officials
judges of what even a Supreme Court Judge finds
it difficult to decide ! Barton has already alien-
ated many supporters ; and if he goes on this way
he’ll lose them all.
Voting by post is sure to form a part of
the Electoral Act under which the next Common-
wealth Parliament will be elected. The recalci-
trant States will follow. Woman franchise will
be a mockery without it, as in the great bulk of
families in country districts wives cannot go to the
poll the same day as their husbands.
See’s amended N.S.W. Old Age Pensions
Bill will provide, among other items, the repeal
of the clause fining publicans for selling a pen-
sioner a glass of beer. That clause was wedged
in by the Leg. Council, and to do Lyne justice it
made him so mad that only fear of losing the
Bill for a session impelled him to accept it.
Apropos the facts that Adelaide has two
daily and six weekly papers and that only one, a
labor weekly, opposes the tramway job. Brisbane
has three dailies and about eight weekly papers
and only one—also a Labor paper —plays con-
sistently the role of jobbery-exposer. Brisbane
can “lose” Adelaide where Boodle is in question.
Almost the only proposal in Premier
Peacock’s budget which was not retrogressive was
that Victoria should open State coal-mines for the
supply of the State railways. Coal is the great
essential to keep a railway going, and when
private employers, by starting some purely private
squabble with their men, are able to cripple a
great State industry, the situation is wholly
ridiculous.
Writes “ Shaker ” :
Curiously coincident with the announcement that the
Australian Premiers are to be invited to the coronation
comes a rumor that an influential English syndicate is
preparing to submit fresh proposals to the Govt, of New
Guinea, with the object of acquiring a large tract of land
in the Possession to be developed by Indian coolies. If
the Premiers do go to London again, it might be as well
(in view of the fact that on the former occasion another
l E. s. nearly had the contributing colonies on toast over
the same matter, per medium of, then, Premier Nelson
and Co.) to have a clear understanding before they start
as to how far they are empowered to commit the country
to any similar schemes which Joberlain or his friends
may have up their sleeve.
Sydney newspaper item :
At Bathurst local police-court yesterday the glories of
a Freetrade policy as a wage-producer were exemplified in
a startling manner. Sergt. Morris, inspector under the
Public Instruction Act, proceeded against W. Cowall, a
farm-laborer, for neglecting to send his two children to
school. Defendant pleaded guilty, and stated that he
was a farm-laborer, “ with nine children, earning only
15s. a week and his ‘ grub.’ ” The “ grub,” he said, was
worth ss. a week. Mr. O’Neill commented on the shock-
ing state of affairs, and stated that the excuse advanced
that he couldn’t provide his children with decent clothes
was a fair one. The summons was withdrawn by the
police.
Go on the land, young man !
Tasmania's Government has
decided not to buy that most un-
profitable private spec., the Emu
Bay railway. On the face of
things, it is right, for the Emu
Bay line hardly pays for axle-
grease. But if it bought the line
at its present scrap-iron value,
and then resumed the huge
estate of that •foolish and dis-
graceful English monopoly, the
Van Diemen’s Land Co., and cut
it up on the easiest possible terms,
and made roads through it to
bring produce to the railway, it
would be one of the best things
any Tas. Government ever did.
The land isn’t good, yet it isn’t
by any means hopeless. But the
V.D.L. Co. is asleep and three-
parts dead. There are no roads
through its territory—even the
important seaport of Burnie and
the big mining town of Zeehan
are not connected by road—not
even by goat-track. There are
no branch roads to bring traffic
to the railway. There is practic-
ally nobody to live on a branch
road even if there was one, for
the V.D.L. Co. lies like a night-
mare on the stomach of the sleep-
ing country, and neither sells,
nor uses, nor opens,up the land to
any extent worth mentioning. If
it was shifted the West might
wake up, but there is no hope
otherwise.
Why should a legislator’s
salary in a Freetrade .State like
N.S.W. be artificially fixed at
£3OO a year ? Why not let candidates tell the
electors what salary they will accept, and let the
electors take the cheapest man they think will fill
the billet to their satisfaction ? Why have an
artificial minimum wage for M’s.L.A., thus
bursting up the allegedly unalterable Law of
Supply and Demand ?
Still another deputation of Victorian
manufacturers called on Premier Peacock, the
other day, to protest against the Minimum Wage.
A sample
Mr. T. N. Harkness pointed to the deletion of industries
from Victoria which was now going on. . . He told the
Premier that the number of employees in factories had,
in consequence of the Act, decreased.
The Act dates from about 1896, and these are the
number of employees in Victorian factories since
then, according to the official returns: —lB96,
50,448; 1897, 52,701; 1898, 54,778; 1899, 60,070;
1900, 64,210. Where does that deputation expect
to go to when it dies ?
HE WASN'T OMNIPOTENT.
MICK (the well-lamed one): “Did ever yez read 'Looking Backward,'
Pat?”
PAT (indignantly): “How the divil could I?"
S.A. Leg. Council, now ruled by a Tory
majority, passed the unspeakable Snow Tramway
grab Bill in one day. Except two Labor-members,
the only speakers were Snow barrackers. At the
last moment Adelaide Corporation comes out as
an opponent of the measure, and a majority of
suburban councils also oppose, but they are also
up in arms against Govt, conversion, and S.A.
Assembly is not unlikely to make this an excuse
for supporting Snow’s London company. At
latest, the plastic Premier Jenkins had not
definitely announced the Govt, policy on the
subject.
N.S.W. Lands-Secretary Crick has intro-
duced a Bill to enable workmen to take up half-
an-acre to an acre-and-a-half at La Perouse, near
Sydney, on easy terms. The measure also pro-
vides for a loan of £5O, to enable each holder to
build a house. Altogether, 3000 families are to
be accommodated. “ I propose,” said the Minis-
ter, “ to call it Crickville, and hope, to the end of
my days, that I shall be able to represent it as its
member in Parliament. ” The Crick ville electorate
will take in Band wick, and if its vote makes an
end of hatter Storey, M.L.A., Crick will not have
lived in vain.
Melbourne Argus has been saying for
years that the lowness of wages in Victoria as
compared with N.S.W. is a Shocking Example of
the results of Protection. It is still saying that.
At the same time, seeing no other way to get rid
of the hated Minimum Wage law, it has begun to
say that that Act must be suspended because,
when the border duties are abolished, Vic.
manufacturers won’t be able to compete with the
lower wages in N.S.W. And it has just begun
to dimly realise that the two statements don’t
hang well together, and don’t even look well
when hung separately.
S A. Assembly has before it three different
schemes of Parliamentary reform. The Jenkins
Govt, proposes reduction of Assembly members
from 54 to 36, with single-member districts. The
country party, which is in a majority, has elected
a committee to devise its own scheme ; the Labor
crowd favors leaving the Assembly alone and
abolishing the Council, which is the best idea of
nil; and Homburg and a few other Tory Opposi-
tionists propose nothing and oppose everything.
After three years of wrangling Parliament has
done nothing except reject Holder’s sensible pro-
posal of last year, viz., to leave the whole ques-
to an independent convention elected by the
people.
S. Australia is making a desperate plunge
to get a batch of far-Northern squattages off its
hands. The misguided province, some years ago,
decided to pay squatters cash for the improve-
ments they had made if they didn’t want to renew
their leases. Plenty of them jumped at the
chance, with the result that,, for years past, the
Govt, has had to pay caretakers to more-or-less
look after these improvements. Of course, the
Govt, got no return; instead, despite the outlay,
the improvements began to fall to bits. So the
squatters are getting the country back—country
that has been nicely rested —for from 3s. fid. to 4s.
a square mile, with the improvements at just
about a fourth the price the Govt, paid for them.
And, in a year or two, you will hear the N.S.W.
squatter quoting what S.A. has done, as an
example for N.S.W. ; what he will not refer to is
the tight corner S.A. got itself into before offer-
ing these terms.
When Seddon, of Maoriland, was alleged
to have remarked that arbitration was being
ridden to death, and unless moderation was
observed there would be a reaction against the
principle, the Tory Press of Australia rose unani-
mously to heap discredit on the same class of
legislation in this country. Now it turns out that
Seddon’s remarks were not directed against
arbitration at all, but against the way in which
the courts of conciliation were being conducted.
In one dispute, at Auckland, no less than 400
witnesses were listed to testify, and on account
of the voluminous nature of the evidence taken
at these inquiries the work has fallen into arrear.
Nobody proposes to abolish the ordinary civil law
because cases are long-dragged-out. And the
longest arbitration case that has been heard so
far didn’t last one-tenth as long as the longest
strike, nor cost nearly so much.
“ Protection,”
said Senator Josh
Symon, indignantly,
the other day, “is a
selfish policy.” Why
doesn’t Symon make
the equally brilliant
remark that a cow
has four legs ? Of
course, Protection is
selfish. So is Free-
trade. Every Free-
trader in a Free-
trade country strug-
gles to prove that his
policy has benefited
his own country \
and not one of them
says: “We recom-
mend Freetrade be-
cause, though it has
done us a great deal
of damage, that is
more than counter-
balanced by the
good it has done to
other countries.”
The Freetrader who
recommends Free-
trade solely on the
ground that it will
selfishly benefit
“ us,” and who com-
plains when t he Pro-
tectionist recommends Protection for exactly the
same reason, is indeed a nice object to preach the
doctrine of disinterestedness.
About half the time of tli e Viccorian
Legislature is taken up with the grievances of
public servants. Out of 14 questions in the
Assembly on a recent night, Tapeanwax was res-
ponsible for no fewer than eight. When Brom-
ley’s Bill came on, the Service wire-pullers were
there in force long past midnight, openly beckon-
ing members out and sending them back again to
talk. This persistent undergrounding by the
public servant will end in a movement to deprive
him of voting privileges. Bromley’s Bill went for
a revival of seniority as an absolute standard.
Seniority is the zareba of the incompetent. Tom
Bent’s seniority would make him Premier over
Turner.
Premier Barton (in reply to King O’Malley’s
proposal that the Federal capital territory should
include at least 1000 sq. miles, and the amendment
by Chapman, of N.S.W., that it should include
5000 sq. miles):
The Constitution does not contemplate anv verv large
area for the capital.
The Bulletin desires to deny this flatly. The
Constitution says that the Federal capital area
shall be not less than 100 sq. miles. It may be
any amount more. But, apart from its words, it
doesn’t contemplate anything. It isn’t a live
thing, so it. can’t contemplate. And if the half-
dozen men who put in the allusion to the 100 sq.
miles (it was riot done by the people’s elected
representatives in the Convention, but by the
after-Conference of Premiers) contemplated any-
thing that isn’t definitely expressed, the people
have nothing at all to do with that. They can
go and contemplate by themselves.
According to the new Elections Bill just
tabled by the Q. Govt, the oldini quitous Property
vote is to reign undisturbed, but a franchise is to
be conferred on the women—also a fancy franchise
on the fathers of families. If a man (in the word 3
of the Bill—any male elector who is the father of
at least two children— male —mind you, not female)
be blessed with two or more legitimate children
he secures an extra vote. A woman similarly
blessed secures no additional voting privileges
—probably because she hasn’t done the
State any particular service compared with
the man. No woman is to be allowed to sit
or vote in the Assembly. The “aboriginal native
of Australasia, India, China” is to still be allowed
a vote for a freehold qualification, experience hav-
ing amply proved that the alien freehold vote
always goes for the Govt, that fights for the alien.
The Bill bids fair to be a laughing-stock, and
could only be introduced by such a Govt, as the
Continuous Ministry of Queensland.
J. T. Bell (Independent—otherwise
Philpite—candidate for the vacant Federal Dar-
ling Downs seat) has been trying to prejudice the
Danish and German farmers of the Downs (who
are rather numerous) by asserting that if Barton’s
Aliens’ Exclusion Bill had been in operation some
years ago, when the original Danish and 'German
immigrants came to the country, it would have
prevented their settling in Australia. No one
knows better than Bell that no immigrant of
European race will ever be subjected to the educa-
tional test demanded by Barton’s Bill; no one
knows better that it is aimed purely at Asiatic,
African, and Polynesian aliens ; and no one knows,
better that the aforesaid educational test was-
rendered necessary by the action of the Imperial
Govt, to which Bell and his fellow-Philpites pro-
fess such a superabundant loyalty. The real
trouble with the “ culchawed ” Bell is that the
White-Australia party on the Downs are backing-
Littleton Groom, who is pledged to the teeth
against every variety of black and yellow alien,
and this hypocritical appeal to Danish and German
prejudice is merely an attempt to discount Groom,
in the eyes of a large number of Downs farmers.

Mr. S. A. PALMER,
Agent for WEBBER’S VITADATIO.
Sir,—lt is with feelings of deep and heartfelt gratitude
that I write to thank you for the benefit I have received
from VITADATIO. It has honestly made a grand change
in my life. For the past 15 years I have been a martyr, to
indigestion in its worst form. For over two years I
was practically helpless, being doubled up and quite un-
able to straighten myself. My stomach was so weak that
I was unable to take more than four ounces of very light
food per day, consequently I became weak generally, and
within a short time I fell away to a mere skeleton. I wa 3
attended by some of our leading doctors, and must have
drank gallons of various kinds of medicine, all to no
purpose. Whilst in this miserable condition I read of
several cures of indigestion by VITADATIO, and almost
as a last resource I decided to give it a trial, and right
glad lam that I did so. After a few large bottles I felt
great relief. I continued with the medicine, and all along
could feel the “slow but sure” return of health, until
here I am to-day, perfectly restored and without an ache
or pain in the stomach after food. I can now eat three
good meals per day and enjoy them. I am at loss for
words wherewith to express my gratitude, but you may
be sure I shall spread the good news that Webber’s VITA-
DATIO can, and has, cured indigestion in its worst form.
I send you this testimonial perfectly voluntarily, and
you may have my permission to use it as you deem advis-
able. I shall be pleased to interview and give details of
my case to anyone.
Yours gratefully,
ISAIAH GLOVER,
Of the firm of Glover and Sons

The Age writes about the accused in a
charge arising out of the fact of a Vic. girl, not yet
12 years of age, being found to be enciente :
His arrest on such a grave offence has caused great
surprise, as he was always regarded as steady ana
industrious, and being a capable musician he was a
familiar figure at nearly all the dances held throughout
the shire.
Which is quite a charming bit of newspaper logic
Earl Russell, in asserting his preposterous
right to be tried by his peers on a charge of cheap
American bigamy, has probably driven the last
nail into the coffin of the coroneted-jury system.
Even the peeresses present laughed at the expen-
sive farce. When an ancient lordly institution
excites the ridicule of women who have been bom
and bred in an atmosphere of class privilege it
seems to cry aloud for its own death-warrant. t

“ H.” : There is a remarkable engineering
project, in France, for a railway across the Sahara,
from Algiers, and right to that point in Africa
where the continent is nearest South America and
begins to tuck in for the Bight. Such a railroad,
in conjunction with steamboat services, would
shorten the time on the journey from Paris to
Buenos Ayres by about one-half.
“F.”: The Abbotsford Convent figures
in a recent “Plain English” letter are under-
stated. The number of inmates of the Magdalen
Asylum on June 30th last was 338. During the
previous 12 months the admissions to the home
were 98, whilst 85 women were “restored to
friends, or otherwise provided for.” Some of the
aged inmates, of course, are practically fixtures.
They have been there 20 or 30 years, or more, and
in such cases the Government saves the ten-
shillings-a-week pension, for which they would be
eligible if they went forth into the world again.
Which is possibly a factor in Premier Peacock’s
ardent admiration for the Abbotsford Convent
work, though that smileful personage says it was
the spectacle of all the human belongings of
the convent, gathered together at the laying, of
the foundation-stone of the new wing, which
“knocked” him. The most impressive thing he
ever saw in his life, declares the man with the
terrible guffaw.
