“Piles and Their Cure.”—The pamphlet
by Albert Sharp, entitled “ Piles and the Wav to
Cure Them,” without operation, sent free, on
application to the Sydney Depot, 257 George-
street.*
The recent increase of violent crimes both
in Melbourne and Sydney is too marked to be
ignored. The forces of the law, instead of being
marshalled against the street vendor of pen’orths
of violets, the shilling punter, and the seller of
the Sabbath banana, should realise that the
average citizen has a body to be kicked as well as
a soul to be damned; and that the former is just
now in much the more dire danger.
Beale and Co. are building another large
factory to their already extensive establishment at
Annandale, Sydney. This firm has devoted a
life-time of thoroughness and experience to piano
and sewing-machine manufacture, and should hold
their own more than formerly with the splendid
means now at their disposal.*
“ Quad ” : Woollahra House (Mr. W.
Cooper’s old home), now the property of “ Tom ”
Longworth, of Cobar copper fame, is in the
running for the N.S.W. State Governor’s official
residence. The house has one of the finest ball-
rooms in N.S.W., but when the estate was sub-
divided no provision was made for a roadway to
said ball-room ; guests must land at the main
entrance and walk through the building to reach
it.
“ I have found,” says Mr. Geo. Jenkins,
C.M.G., the high priest of Victorian official
ceremony, “ that in a pure democracy there is
always the greatest anxiety on the subject of
precedence. Every day I found 40,000 people
waiting to see me. Half of them wanted invita-
tions, and the other half wanted to sit on the dais
with the Duke and Duchess. Some of the
bitterest enemies that I have made during the
whole of my long official life were made because I
could not, and would not, put them on the dais.
First of all, everybody came for their invitation
cards. Afterwards three-quarters of the number
came back and said : ‘ Here, you can take back
these entree cards, Mr. Jenkins. We could not
possibly accept them. Mr. Smith is in a row in
front of us, and Mr. Brown, to whom we do not
speak, and who is much lower in the social scale
than we are, is two rows in front of us.’ It was
not much wonder that Mr. Gale found it difficult
to see me.”
There is quite a boom in organising
sweated (N.S.W.) workers. The Cold-Stor-
age employees met the other day to form a
union. They now work for 9d. an hour in stores
at from lOdeg. above to 15deg. below zero ; their
organised brethren, the wharf-laborers, are
(thanks chiefly toW. M. Hughes) receiving Is. 6d.
for the same time, but with the cold blast off.
Then the cold-storage workers object to being
now and then worked from 100 to 110 hours at a
stretch, and often being called to a job for a
stated hour and being forced to wait several hours
before commencing—pay only beginning when
they start. The meat-canning employees are also
moving ; they claim that they work 62 hours .a
week for 20s. to 35s ; an exception is one foreman,
who has been in the trade for 11 years, and he
gets only £2 2s. The same strong desire for better
terms has spread to the wool and hide workers at
Botany. The Newcastle tailors have struck for a
15 per cent, increase in wages; and a section of
the Sydney boot-trade has just formed a society
of clickers. The president of the Bootmakers’
Union, who presided at a meeting of the sweated
clickers t’other night, detailed the condition of
workers: 12s. 6d. to £2 (average 275. 6d. weekly)
for all-round expert workman. Maoriland and
Victoria’s lowest rate, he said, is £2 2s. Simul-
taneously the Brisbane men held a meeting, and a
conference with the employers was decided upon.
In Brisbane, it seems during 1899, out of 887
males in the trade there were 387 boys, of whom
186 were above 18 years. Average wage for men,
£ll7s. Bd.; for boys, 13s. 6d. The 138 women
employees averaged 19s. weekly; the 220 girls,
7s. 9Jd.
According to a cable—which reads like a
particular bit of “nark”—the behaviour of the
undergraduates at Gape Town University Com-
mencement, in the presence of the Dukeandutch,
was most decorous, and formed a striking con-
trast to the uproarious behaviour of the Mel-
bourne students.” This is accounted for in Mel-
bourne, where the undergrads, got on to a few
personal and sartorial peculiarities of the Juke’s
literary mouthpiece, and made a song of them.
The potentate in question smiled and smiled
under the infliction, like a nervous man with a
steam-roller on his corned toe, but he “ had it in
for ’em ” all the time, and this message is evi-
dently an attempt to “get back.” But the idiocy
of the Melb. students certainly pulled the Duke
off his pedestal, and made him look throughout
the function like an anxious small boy “up for
cuts.”
The cabled comments on the marked
difference between the conduct of the Cape Town
students when the Duke visited the local uni-
versity, and that of the Melbourne and Sydney
university students has aroused indignation in
Melbourne. The “ Commencement ” there passed
off well and little comment was made at the time ;
but the ’Varsity dons have met in solemn protest
at the slight on Melbourne University. The
Sydney professors, however, have not met in any
protesting spirit. Not much ! They held one
meeting—five minutes after the rout of the Duke
and suite. It was then carried unanimously that the
professors should not grace with their presence the
social event held that afternoon; and at next Senate
meeting recommended that in future the students
should be kept out of the University’s annual
festival. As a matter of policy the Sydney papers
were prevented from saying much about the row
but its consequences threaten to shake Sydney
University to its foundations.

Severe recent depredations upon the
poultry of a Sydney suburb were discovered to be
the work of a peculiar-looking dog, which, upon
being shot, turned out to be an escaped dingo from
the Zoo.
The luck of some people. The captain of
a China boat calmly sails into Rockingham Bay,
near Cardwell, N.Q., over the Barrier Reef, having
mistaken his port. He wanted Rockingham Bay,
W.A., about 4000 knots away. Being advised of
his error he coolly sailed out again. Mark the
spot on the map and ask any coastal skipper how
he would like to chance it.
A Critical Case.
A Senior-Inspector of Police Submits
his Evidence.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills add one more
name to the long list of their wonderful cures.
It is for those once placed on the verge of the
grave, and who have been given a longer term
of life by the timely use of this wonderful
remedy, to give the Pills their due of praise.
All who use them give them a good name, but it
is only those who, after a long and dangerous
sickness, feel through their use the rich blood
of perfect health in their veins, that can bring
warmth and conviction and the clear ring of
manly and vigorous truth to their words. Such
a man is Senior-Inspector W. T. Langworthy, of
Yass, New South Wales, who writes as fol-
lows : “Dear Sirs, —For many years I have
been a sufferer from rheumatism, constipation,
giddiness, violent pains in the head, and a
‘liver,’ colds, chills, and, worse than all,
lumbago; of course, I got temporary relief from
doctors and chemists. At last I read your pamph-
let on Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, and I con-
sidered the vegetable ingredients would not
hurt, and took four—then three, and no pain.
The change was wonderful. Instead of leaning
against the wall while putting on my trousers,
as usual, through giddiness, I was abie to stand
upright, and I felt a desire to turn a somersault
upon the bed as a boy would. You would laugh.
Well, sirs, it is a fact. I have used them now
occasionally for three years, and carry the little
bottle with me on the road when travelling. I
recommend them to everybody, with good results.
Whenever I look at the little bottle, I feel that
I owe one debt to you, and now I will repay it
by expressing my gratitude.” (I have no objec-
tion to my testimonial being used relative
to the benefit I derived from Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills.—W. T. Lang worthy.)
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a perfect blood
purifier and a positive cure for biliousness, indiges-
tion, constipation, headaches, sallow complexion,
liver and kidney troubles, piles, pimples, boils,
blotches, and female ailments. Sold by chemists
and storekeepers, Is ljd per bottle, or six bottles
6s. 6d. Sole proprietors, The W. H. Comstock
Co., Ltd. (Australasian Depot), 58 Pitt-street,
Sydney. Packed in amber bottles, and the full
name blown thereon.*

Nothing stands out over-much for A.J.O. Derby

meetmg Sir Leonard would look promising but for the

while it may take out of Sir Leonard the dash which he
showed with only 7.8 to hump. But Sir Leonard at
Randwick was close on 2st. better than at Rosehill, and if
there s anything in that form he holds Huasca, Grasspan,

nag finished up the Autumn something better than Ibex
or Grasspan. '

To-day si(Sat.) A.J.C. Epsom is the most open
for years. There are a dozen likely winners, and the best
way to get a good reliable tip is to put 10 names in a bag,
shake em up, and plunge on the first that comes out.
Anyhow, it would be no worse than other tips.

inllers Caravel, Rock Gun, Prince Butler, Nymagee, and

Two M.L. racing clubs now pay-out on first and
second horses with striking success, and the practice may
prove general. A few pay “ odd money ”up to sixpence,
but others grip it tight-—a species of thievery strongly
condemned Thus, if the “cut-out” is lid., club and
tote-proprietor whack it. In Adelaide, writer believes, it
is distributed amongst charities.

.. The dual-divided system is calculated to knock
the pestilential M.L. tote-bettor right out. Some of him
have already retired ; others, instead of paying two divs
pay out a third more to the winner, and as the public has
been picking ’em with sickening regularity lately, Ikev is
loudly denouncing clubs for “ taking the bread out of his
mout/ii.

M.L. Police Commissioner Tunbridge com-
plams, re the inability of the “ foorce ” to deal with the
tote-bettor who swarms all over the footpath in every big
town in the province, that magistrates hold that tote-
betting must be absolutely proved before a conviotion
can follow, and condemn the police if they seek to make
bets or procure others to do so in order to secure the
necesssrjr evidence. WeU, “straight-out” wagering is
lawful ana tote-betting isn't, and beaks are naturally
careful when confronted with contradictory testimony.
Ihe Commission w asks for power to arrest without war-
rant betting-men doing business in the streets.

The Oxenham establishment scooped Sydney’s
two big winter billiard-tournies. “Oxy” himself won

consequently scored a skinner.

A very handsome match-box is being
issued as an advertisement by the Atlas Pipe
Wrench Co., through N. Guthridge, Ltd., 71
Clarence-street, Sydney.*
Cabled that the Boers in the concentra-
tion-camps are showing themselves most ungrate-
ful, and construing the kindness extended to them
as sympathy with their cause. Then, later on,
comes a cable that of 1067 deaths in the concentra-
tion-camps during July, 860 of the victims were
children! With so much to be thankful for, the
Boer’s ingratitude is indeed a marvellous thing.
Melbourne’s “ wretched climate ” has
created a panic among Federal legislators, several
of whom have fled in dismay, pursued by influenza,
pneumonia and similar pleasantries. Wise mem-
bers, however, speedily discovered that the only
cure for “the wretched climate” lay in D.C.L.
Whisky, and so obviated the necessity for flight.
That so few legislators fled is a testimony to the
combined wisdom of the House, and to the
extraordinary efficacy of the medical comfort in
question—D.C.L.—The Whisky of The Wise.
Touching that Heaven-born refreshment, we
would say with the late Tom Hood,
Think of it, drink of it
Dissolute man !•
Melbourne’s latest sensation puts up a
record for utter unaccountableness. A Carlton
constable, hearing screams at 1 a.m., investigated,
and discovered a woman with a revolver bullet in
her head lying on the floor of her own bedroom.
He rushed her off to hospital, and on the way
thither she told a circumstantial story of a masked
man, who entered with a skeleton key, demanded
and got her purse, tore off her rings, fired three
shots into her and decamped. Further investiga-
tion at the house revealed, in the next room, a
small daughter of 11 and a baby-cousin, aged 4.
The former said she went to bed at 10,
leaving her mother talking to some lady
friends and a certain “Jim.” Later, she
was awakened by someone striking a match,
and saw her mother and a man in her room talk-
ing. The man asked for the loan of some money,
and the mother said “ All right.” Almost imme-
diately after there ensued three shots, many
screams and general consternation. The daughter
said that the man’s voice sounded like —r -’s,
but she was quite certain it couldn’t be, “as he
was always so fond of mother.” A side-issue in
the matter is that the parties were consuming
oysters some time before the shooting episode.
There are very often a lot of oysters in such cases.

South Grafton (N.S.W.) people are happy.
They are to have a clock-tower to the local post-
office, despite the decision of Federal P.M.G.
Drake that no more shall be erected. South
Grafton is in the electorate of Premier See.

It is a curious fact that in Great Britain
the existence of an army is prohibited by law.
Every year the law is suspended for another 12
months by special Act. Some da-y there will be
a hitch and the special Act will get through a day
too late, and then for 24 hours the humblest
private will be able to kick the Commander-in-
Chief with no worse prospect than being fined,
as an ordinary citizen, for assaulting another
ordinary citizen.

At Poverty Point.
The last three operas produced during the Italian
season in Melbourne were “ La Boheme,” “ Un Ballo ana
“ Rigoletto.” In Sydney they took their turns among
the first four, as the result of managerial experience
down South,’though the present co. certainly doesn’t shine
in the Verdi operas by comparison with the best perform-
ances of yore. Signora Guttica, and Melossi as the
Jester, scored a memorable success in “ Rigoletto ” some
years back. All the same, the present crowd of Italians,
taking one performance with another, are probably the
strongest company that Australia has sampled.
“ Esmi ” : Oomic stage-business is apt to divert atten-
tion too much from the music, even if the audience
refrains from laughter. At “La Boheme,” I closed
my eyes, to rest them a moment from the glare, during
Musetta’s coquettish fooling in the second act, and im-
mediately became aware of the great beauty of the music
then being played. Finding it more enjoyable to listen
than to watch the performance, I kept my eyes closed.
“Melb.” : Atholwood is the only performer who will
figure in the Wilson Barrett cast of “ The Sign of the
Cross,” after being included in that with Julius Knight.
People are so accustomed to see Jimmy Atholwood in old
man parts (bulbous-nosed as a rule) that they were quite
confounded with the sight of him as the yellow-wigged
young clerk in “ Man and his Makers.” They mistook
him for an imported boy comedian. “ Atholwood ? Im-
possible 1”
The music hall profession will soon have to be brought
under the Shops and Factories Act. A Brisbane show
works its artists seven nights a week and gives a Saturday
matinee.
Announced that Wilson Barrett has written a new his-
torical play with Alfred the Great as the central character.
Two things to be sincerely hoped for are that it won’t
bear so striking a resemblance to anything else as “ The
Sign of the Cross” does to “ Quo Vadis,” and that Alfred
will wear more clothes than Claudian does.
A bright and sprightly little actress, on leaving Mel-
bourne by train recently, was attended to the station by a
crowd of admirers, mainly masculine, and the demon-
stration on the platform quite upset the railway regu-
lations and arrangements for the time being. Parting
was such sweet sorrow that one impressionable johnnie
blubbered publicly, and the bright, particular star was so
moved at this display of feeling that she gave_ way too,
and kissed all-comers with generous fervor. Blinded with
tears, she failed to discriminate between friends and
strangers, and two or three luggage-porters, a good grey
guard, and two enterprising young cabmen joined the
throng, and were rewarded with a pillowy hug and a large
feeling kiss apiece.
Lalla Miranda, at latest, was captivating them at Vichy
(France), as the name character in “ Lakma.” One critic
enthuses over her “crystalline voice and faultless acting.”
Another says “ The actress, as well as the slender singer,
has triumphed. With fine eyes, an expressive face, a
series of very enveloping attitudes (‘ enveloping ’ is good !)
Miranda gives to the beautiful Indian an attraction full of
flavor by reason of her charm and poetry. The fine voice
of the singer, attaining wi‘. bout effort the highest note in
the vocal scale, gives marvellously, in a clear and caressing
sonorousness, a melancholic color of the songs of Lackmb.”
And anotherprostrate worshipper of “Mdlle. LallaMiranda,
who was so much applauded at Brussels at the Thbatre La
Monnaie,” exclaims “What a sweet and charming
voice she has. She was encored more than twenty times,
and I take this opportunity of addressing to her my
most sincere congratulations.” The news that the
Spanish-Australian warbler is a delightful Indian girl
comes as a matter of course to her admirers hereabouts.
She is still quite young, and has preserved all the distinc-
tive charm of the personality that made her a favorite
concert singer before she was out of her teens. An artis-
tic portrait post-card issued this year from the Brussels
Theatre Royal de la Monnaie shows Lalla Miranda looking
out from a frame, and the up-to-date face might have
been taken from an old Melbourne photo, of the adorable
brunette.
Australia is dropping behind the times in its theatrical
productions. “ San Toy ” drew a record first-night house
at Capetown (of all places!) early in June.
Melba, as the consumptive Mimi in “ La Boheme,” is
evidently regarded as a physical joke. London Referee
critic says: “Mine. Melba, I am told, thinks she is an
ideal Mimi. There is much comfort in self-faith, and
Melba certainly sings the music charmingly.”

At Poverty Point they are speculating as to how a cer-
tain human hair-pin manages to get into those tight mili-
tary breeches. Does he grease his soles and use a shoe-
horn, bai Jove ?
The Westralian goldfields are a hard road to travel just
now with any sort of theatrical show. Business-manager
Lohr said of Arnold’s recent visit to Kalgoorlie, Cooi-
gardie, &c. : “ We did very well indeed, but two years ago
we’d have done 25 per cent, better.”
One of the youngest of Williamson’s Italian Opera co.
(born in 1872) is Signora Benedetto, who has been trying
hard to say “the ” and learn the “ th ” sound generally
in the intervals of being a brown Ethiopian maiden m Che
days of the unbuilt Pyramids. The signora is a nice-
looking girl, though that is not apparent under the brpwn
paint of Alda, which necessarily thickens the contour of
the face and adds heaviness to the features. When that
coating is off, she possesses a complexion that it is posi-
tively sinful to bury in the manner of Aida. The signora,
according to a Melbourne interviewer, has a strong
admiration for Wagner ; also, though this statement has
no connection with the other, a vivid faith in the musical
culture of the Melbournites. As a matter of course, she
was also reported as remarking that she would like to
reside in Melbourne permanently ; it is one of the trials
of the mummer’s lot that she has to have a consuming
desire to live everywhere all the rest of her days. Doubt-
less Miss Benedetto wants to live permanently in Sydney,
too. She also discovered that the Melbournites have ihe
“ spark of intuition” ; and if that same spark isn’t found
in the harbor city, there will be trouble in store for the
unfortunate Italian.


Wellington (N.S.W.) is on the road to
Sheol if the local churches, are to be credited. C.
of E., Wesleyan, Presbyterian and Salvationist
clergy unitedly condemned the proposal to raise
hospital subscriptions on a recent Sunday. Dese-
cration of the Sabbath, yell the clerics. But
Wellington persists.
“ Melb.’ 7 : A thing which has got to come
is a central news agency. Four separate little
concerns are at work on the Commonwealth Par-
liament reports. They ought to combine and
send a man to London, New York, Washington,
and Paris, in order to get pon.jd up on news-
collecting bureaux. •

work. But to have the privilege of being shown thiougn
the building by the Specialists themselves, and to see am
learn something of the real character of their wide pi ao-
tice, is a treat that only comes the way of a fortunate in-
dividual like the writer. ~ . „
The instrument-room was, as usual, one of particular
curiosity. Laymen always have a penchant for eyeing
and examining the polished steel articles witn which so.
many extraordinary acts are performed upon the human!
body. The collection in this room is in every way up-to-
date. Whenever a new instrument comes to the front,,
and its worth is proved beyond question, it is added to.
this rare assortment. Another room full of absorbing in--
terest is that devote'd to the galvanic batteries, of which
there are several magnificent kinds. The noted Lame-
batteries, the finest French makes, are here in perfection,,
irrespective of their high cost. If any article or mechani-
cal and electric contrivance is found necessary in main-
taining the fame of this institution, the price is never
allowed to stand in the way.
The correspondence department is a veritable eye-
opener. Its sacredness is a bar to a stranger’s admission,
but a full view of the viork going on is had through the
glass doors. Half-a-dozen typists are seen driving their
machine-writers at electric speed. To the principals only
are known the names of the persons throughout Australia
who are being treated as skilfully in their homes about
the metropolis and away in the interior as if they were
present in personal consultation at the institution.
The packing-rooms are likewise shut off from curious
and prying glances, and the work of one department is a
mystery to the others. No better system for holding
secret the nature of sufferers’ troubles, for safe-guarding
their interests, and giving due heed to the feelings and
sensitiveness of patients, could be imagined. Packages
are not plastered with advertising labels, they are put up
in such a way that only the names and addresses are m
view.
What a practice is here revealed and what a consola-
tion must it be to these eminent specialists to know that
they are permanently curing and restoring to health and
happiness thousands of people whose lives were a burden
to them. The young, the middle-aged, and the healthy
are treated with the same amount of success. The pro-
digious nature of the work accomj li hed by these
specialists necessitates —not the stereotyped morning and
evening two hours’" consultation ticket, but their constant,
attention at the institution from early morning till late
into the night. “ I can assure you,” said Dr. Wallace,.
“ we don’t put in these long hours because we are fonder-
SB
Premises = Oor. Elizabeth and Bathurst Sts. (Opp. Hyde Park, Sydney
(All Trams Stop at the Door).
of electricity, and only a fringe of imagination of the un-
told stores of strength and wealth wrapt up in this
phenomenal power.
Therefore it requires no argument to convince the
most pronounced of optimists that the field in any one of
the great and honored professions of the day is far too
vast for the thorough comprehension of the most per-
fectly brain-endowed man, or body of naturally-gifted
men. Yet in respect of the most delicate and difficult of
all professions—that of prescribing for the ills and
diseases of mankind—we find doctors, scattered over the
globe, off-handedly and without careful, mini' te, and
searching examination and diagnosis—without a jy know-
ledge of the past lives of patients—flippantly dashing off
prescriptions for every class of complaint, as if a cure was
as simple as falling over the proverbial log.
Thanks to the march of civilisation, culture, and edu-
cation, people are getting to know that modern medical
men who are worth consulting confine their practice to a
certain class of cases. Realising the utter hopelessness of
mastering and curing everything under the sun, and find-
ing that “gullibles” are not so plentiful as they used to
be, the wise and progressive men of medicine devote
themselves to one or other class of cases, and work out
the remedies for them to the best of their skill and
ability.
What better and more striking illustration of the suc-
cess of Specialists in Skin Diseases, Heart, Kidney, Liver
Affections, Rheumatics, Sciatica, Nervous Diseases, could
the people of this magnificent continent, Australia, Rave
than the enormous practice of the fathojus practitioners of
the Electro-Medical Institute. Their skill, attainments,
position, and renowned reputation stamp them as the un-
doubted masters of the classes of diseases and complaints
which have been the study of their professional lives.
When we speak of electricity we think of Edison. Why ?
Because he dwarfs other men in this branch of science.
In the same way, when we talk of specialists, we at once
associate the names of the Freeman and Wallace Insti-
tute. So it is in every branch of scientific medicine;
some one or two men stand head and shoulders above
their fellows.
It is the aim, object, and devotion of certain doctors to
he pre-eminent in some defined class of disease rather
than to tinker with a host of complaints about which
they can have but a hazy notion, and superficial know-
ledge. To use a hackneyed but expressive phrase, they
are “Jacks of all trades and masters of none.” Never
since the time of Ricord, the noted French Specialist,
have any men conducted so gigantic and successful a
practice as that conducted by the Freeman-Wallace
I Electro Medical and Surgical Institute.
of hard work than any other medical men ; we do it in
the best interests of sufferers. We don’t keep them
waiting longer than we can help, and we suffer many in-
conveniences for our patients’ sakes.”
Noble sentiments, thought the writer, as the time came
for his departure, and the Specialists returning to their

Too Feeble to Smile
AN ADELAIDE RECORD.
A Complete Restoration.
Th« Oate of
Mrs. S. J. TURNER,
(BY AN ADELAIDE REPORTER.)
Widespread astonishment was expressed
in the town when it became known that
Mrs. Susan Jane Turner, of Jerningham-
street, North Adelaide, was apparently
making rapid strides towards a complete re-
storation to health.
“ This happened some time ago,” ex-
plained Mrs. Turner to our reporter, “and
although I was so feeble that I could
scarcely raise a smile, I could not help
laughing, in spite of it hurting me to do so,
at the look of wonderment on the faces of
those who called to satisfy themselves that
I was really getting better.”
“Your friends were greatly surprised,
I believe?”
“ How could they be otherwise, consider-
ing that I had been suffering for five years,
and only those who knew me intimately had
the least conception of the extent of my
i stress. I had long since forgotten what
it was to enjoy a meal, as anything in the
ihape of eatables was taken against my
will through an entire absence of appetite,
and also on account of the disagreeable ex-
periences which followed my meals so per-
sistently that I got to dread the thought of
trying to eat A quantity of gas used to
form inside and distend my stomach to an
extent which made the touch of my gar-
ments extremely painful. The wind also
roamed about my heart, causing that organ
to flutter in a way which indicated that 1
was suffering from an affection which
might at any time entail the most dreaded
consequences. There were times when I had
to struggle to get my breath, through a
feeling of heaviness in the chest, as if a great
load had been placed upon me. 1 had ter-
rible pains in the leftside, which were made
all the more unbearable by violent fits of
retching. *vhe internal strain thus involved
iffecting me so greatly that I was left
horoughly exhausted and helpless after
each atta k What made me still more
wretched was to be unable to do my work.
If I had been allowed the privilege of busy-
ing myself about the house the time would
nofrhave dragged so drearily; but, you see,
I was absolutely incapableof exerting my
sell in any shape or form. When I stooped
to put on my shoes I couid not raise myself
up again for a long time, owing to the ex-
cruciating pains in my back, and I was also
subject to continuous twitchings between
my shoulders. As each year rolled on so
did my sufferings increase. It became more
difficult to obtain any sleep, as my brain
was so unsettled with misgivings about the
future that I could not reconcile myself
sufficiently to fall into that peaceful state
of mind which brings rest to the weary.
There was nothing unusual in lying awake
all night, and each morning I was in the
same pitiful state, with horrible sick head-
aches and a taste in my mouth that was
most revolting.”
“ Were you able to take any exercise ? ’’
“I was too weak to think of such a
thing, for when I moved in my chair the
effort made me quite giddy ; besides, my
limbs ached with such awful violence that I
could not trust myself to stand. Several
times a day I was in the habit of trying to
wash the fur.off my tongue, but still it re-
mained. I tried to assist my digestive ap-
paratus by taking the lightest of foods, but
my stomach could scarcely retain anything,
and I was for ever vomiting a nasty green-
coloured bile. I could get nothing to re-
gulate me, and the medicines I was taking
were of no benefit to my nervous system,
for I suffered acutely from neuralgia, and
my nerves were never still. The sharp
neuralgic agony which darted from the side
of my face up to the crown of my head
nearly sent me mad, and I often thought
that death was my only means of escape
from all my afflictions. There was not an
ounce of flesh on my body, my face being
drawn and pinched, and the colour of my
skin was like that of a corpse. I was
labouring under a burden of five years’ grief
and pain, and as there was every likelihood
of being an invalid for life, my only wish
was to die and end my misery.”
** Where did you get the medicine you
spoke of ? ”
“ From my doctors. I had two of the
most skilful medical men in the State at-
tending me, besides spending a lot of money
on pills and physics from chemists. The
term of my illness is enough in itself to prove
the inefficacy of the treatment I received,
especially as I had nearly abandoned all
hope when I started taking Clements Tonic,
and that was the only medicine which did
me any good. A visitor to my house urged
me to try Clements Tonic, but I had so com-
pletely lost faith in all medicines that it
took a lot of persuasion to induce me to
yield to my friend’s advice. When I had
taken a couple of bottles of Clements Tonic
a simmer of surprise prevailed all round me,
my neighbours looking upon me as the sub-
ject of a miracle. The neuralgic troubles
were removed with the greatest ease by
Clements Tonic, and my nervous system was
welded together to a degree of perfection
that in a short space of time there was not
the least sign of a quiver in any of my
nerves. 1 waß no longer kept awake by
terrifying thoughts at night, but I enjoyed
instead a proper amount of refreshing
sleep, and in the mornings there was an ab-
sence of that bitter taste in the mouth, and
the retching fits were completely stopped."
‘Did your doctors express surprise at
such a great change taking place ? ”
“ Well, they must have been surprised,
especially as I had been taking medicine
from one of them for fully three years, and
had not made any progress at all. Both of
my physicians said that I was suffering from
a severe form of dyspepsia, so it was not be-
cause they were treating me for the wrong
complaint that their medicine failed to cure
me. With Clementß Touic as my friend 1
soon experienced a return of appetite, and 1
was not at all fastidious about my food, for
I felt so hungry that 1 could tat anything.
The suffocating feelings left my chest, and
the medicinal properties of Clementr Tonic
acted so successfully on my digestive organs
that I did not feel the least discomfort after
eating. Inoticerlthat when I became free from
the windy spasm troubles there was simul-
taneous relief from my heart affections, and
thus I went on from day to day until my
health was regained. Clements Tonic had
all my tortures under control, and my
strength was restored by the same remedy
in a way that enabled me to go through my
household duties as though nothing had
been wrong with me. By using Clements
Tonic I acquired a happy release from a five
years’ martyrdom, and I feel overwhelmed
with gratitude because of the fact that my
health has been perfect ever since."
“If you have no objection I will bring
your case before the public."
“ If I withheld from you that privilege I
would be doing a great wrong to those who
suffer, like I did once, in ignorance of the
reliability of Clements Tonic, so you may
publish my statement in any way."

I, Soban Jans Tubmsr, of Jerningham street,
North Adelaide, in the State of South Australia, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully
read the annexed dooument, consisting of four folios
and consecutively numbered from one to four,
and that it contains and is a true and faithful
account of my illness and oure by Clements Tonio;
and also oontains my full permission to publish in any
manner my statements —whioh I give voluntarily,
and without receiving any payment; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to bo true, and by virtue of the provisions of
an act made and passed in the sixth yeai of the reign
of hie late Majesty King William the Fourth, intituled
“ an aot to repeal an act of this present Session of
Parliament, intituled ‘an act for the more effeotual
abolition of oaths and affirmations taken and made in
various departments of the State, and to substitute
declarations in lieu thereof, and for the more entire
suppression of voluntary and extra-judioial oaths and
affidavits, and to make other provisions for the aboli-
tion of unnecessary oaths.
Declared and subscribed at Adelaide, in the State of

“ Waddy ”: Re smoke-prevention. The
black smoke from a coal-fire is due to incomplete
combustion, and may be considerably reduced
remembering to “feed light and often.” Note
how little smoke issues from the of big
ocean steamers —all due to skilful firing. Air or
steam-jets are sometimes used to diminish the
smoke, but steam-jets are apt to damage the flues
where the jet impinges. In places where coal is
more expensive than Australia (’Frisco, for in-
stance) coal is pulverised and fed automatically to
the nose of an air-blast pipe, and so blown into
the furnace, in the heat of which, owing to its finely
divided state, it is instantaneously converted into
gas. Result, great economy of fuel and no
smoke. I cannot see why, in large establish-
ments,' producer-gas is not made, and the boiler-
gas fired. It is more economical, and there is
absolutely no smoke.
The Bulletin receives No. 1 of N.S.W.
Aborigines Advocate— organ of N.S.W. Abo-
rigines Mission —which “has for its supreme
object the carrying of the Gospel of the
Lord Jesus Christ to the aborigines scat-
tered throughout N.S. Wales.” What futility !
What' the N.S.W. aboriginal wants is a
special reserve, with whites kept off it
under pain of gaol—a place for the race to live
and die in peaceably, such as the U.S. has pro-
vided for the Red Indian. “Believing, as we do,
that Christ died for all men, we believe that He
died for the Australian aboriginal.” What
satire! The aboriginal is killed off on earth,
but heaven is thoughtfully reserved for him—
if only a white missionary can get him to patter
acceptance of a creed which even its own votaries
cannot comprehend, and dare not follow. Then
comes the usual appeal for cash to pay a mis-
sionary’s salary—as if all the cash wasn’t wanted
to give the missionary's human excuse-for-exist-
ence blankets and tucker and a bit of tobacco.
Why will our religionists insist on sending the
victims of their race to a mythical Heaven, instead
of trying to ease the torments of their hell-upon-
earth ?

412 GEORGE STREET and STRAND , SYDNEY.
M.L. dredging-market, contrary to the fervent
hopes of the brokers, has failed to improve with the
increased returns of working dredges. When the floods
subsided and the dredges got fairly on the bottom of the
Molyneux, last month, the returns shot up to three and
four times the ordinary. For the three weeks up to Aug.
3, over half-a-dozen dredges have regularly provided from
200 oz. to lOOoz., while about 25 others range from lOOoz.
down to 30oz. Fact is, that the public is about bled
white with call-paying to “cats.” Now they have the
mortification of seeing real good stock buyable at almost
par, and yet, in many cases, they have nothing left to
speculate with. Those who have the needful are so im-
pressed with the sorry straits of the luckless contributing
shareholders that they are not taking any dredging scrip,
and, between them, the brokers of three Dunedin ex-
changes look vainly for clients and speak slightingly of
the prevailing lack of financial pluck.
* • * *
Writer knows a man who, for the past two
years, has carefully taken home every dredging prospectus
he could get. And after perusal did he fill up the appli-
cation-form for 100 shares and then enclose a cheque ?
No, he put ’em all into a big box, and is now busily
engaged reckoning up how much he has saved by not
investing.
* * * •
The biggest screen yet made in M.L. for gold-
dredging purposes is owned by Earnscleugh No. 3
Dredging 00. It is 7£ft. in dia. and weighs eight or nine
tons.
• * * *
Paringa Copper Mine (S.A.): The British
Australasian takes a remarkable interest in booming
these shares, and never concludes a par. about them with-
out something of this kind: —‘ 1 We have every confidence in
recommending the shares to the notice of investors either
in copper or other mining securities at anything like their
present quotation of £1 55.” Of course attention i 3
drawn to the magnificent prospects and intrinsic merits
of the mine, and the ore will regularly be available for
smelting, and a steady and increasing weekly profit will
result. “ This can readily be understood, as already
parcels of ore aggregating 69 tons have yielded the large
return of 19 per cent, of copper; and we are informed
that £3 per ton will cover the costs of smelting, mining,
and carriage.” Stress is laid on the fact that the mine is
on a railway-siding 36 miles from Adelaide, and that it
possesses such advantages for economical working that a
profit from £6 to £7 per ton of ore may be looked for.
There are 150,000 shares in the co., and at £1 ss. each the
writer does not hesitate to declare that the thing is a
rank cat. Passengers by train from Melbourne to
Adelaide have often noticed a few men gauging small
copper-veins near Oarlingford, once the site of an old
copper-smelting works. From the description given there
is no doubt that this is the locality of the Paringa copper-
mine.
• • * «
Following upon a recent clean-up at. a certain
Vic. gold-mine, two of the directors, with a mining-expert-,
adjourned to the local pub. for a refresher. After the
first wet, one director suddenly exclaimed : “Oh ! I
must have some hot water with this whisky ! ” and rushed
off to the kitchen. The expert followed and found the
gentleman arranging with the hotel-keeper to despatch
his groom on horseback to , five miles off, to send a
private wire. The expert went back to the bar and whis-
pered the other director. “ The old fool! ” said the
latter —“ why, I sent my wire to half-an-hour before
we left the mine 1 ”
* • * •
Westralian mine-workers are having a harder
struggle than most to knock on the head the mine-owners’
levy of a shilling a pay “for the doctor.” The Crown
Law officers say that the deduction is illegal, but that “ it
is no part of the duty of Grown Law officers to take action
where private individuals are concerned ” ; and no friendly-
society feels like incurring the expense of a fight. But a
bill is drafted to compel mining co.’s making this deduc-
tion to register as friendly-societies, which seems a round-
about way of biffing an ancient abuse.
The Seddon Govt, proposes that the mining
and companies laws require amending in the direction of
ensuring that directors of companies shall be liable to be
proceeded against for payment of calls, and that the calls
of other shareholders shall be invalid unless the calls of
directors have been paid, or steps taken for the recovery
thereof. Apropos, the condition of several N.S.W. gold-
mining co.’s with respect to the unpaid calls of directors
is most amusing.
* * • *
“Westral”: Kalgoorlie mine-managers are
having a tough battle with specimen-thieves. One man
told me he was spending over £loooayearon detectives ;
“ but still they get away with it,” he said. He further
uttered a sweeping condemnation of advertised busi-
nesses in the towns which, he stated, were simply living
by buying tellurides, zinc-slimes, amalgam, &c. Occa-
sionally one' is dropped on, but not often. Most of- the
mines have changing-houses, hut the plunder is spirited
off somehow, the nightman being a good medium for
getting it outside the lease, occasionally in the pans, into
which it is dropped in instalments. A bank clerk also
told me that the people now who buy drafts for a yearly
trip to the East are the miners. They get £3 or £3 10s. a
week, and live for £l, so that, if they don’t drink it, there
is a good accumulation at the end of the year when an
occasional cheque from the specimen-buyer is added.

During the past five years no fewer than 116 men
have been killed in Barrier (N.S.W.), mines. This is
quoted as a set-off to the beloved Tory shibboleth “ risks
of capital ” allegedly taken along the same line of lode.

Mounts Jukes and Darwin copper-shows (Tas.),
are at last giving signs of “treasure unbeholden.”
Deeper sinking (650 ft., or thereabouts), has disclosed
stone indicative of something better than calls.
* * * *
The Raub Mines : Curie, the Economist’s
mining writer, has examined these. The directors were
told of 300,000 tons of good ore in sight, and that the
mines in depth continued to fulfil all expectations. Ourle
says there are barely 40,000 tons of pay ore visible, and
the appearance of most of the mines at lowest depth is
very disappointing. He advises the stopping of explora-
tory work on some of the mines, the taking out of all pay
ore in sight and driving the tunnels into the surrounding
hills.

Frank Gardner ia reported as having said in
London lately that he had made a more important dis-
covery than that of the Rontgen Rays, wireless tele-
graphy or liquefied air—viz., the patience of the British
mining investor, the stuff he believes, his want of dis-
ciimination, and the nonsense he listens to. “ They are
simply like a flock of sheep. I have been working hard
17 years trying to teach these people. My mines and
management are right, and I am a dividend-payer.
London Critic gives a full list of Gardner’s flotations.
They are indeed an awful lot. Some of them have passed
into'oblivion, others are windingup, others shouldbe wound
up. The list is too long for publication here. The only
div -payer appears to be the Wilfley Ore Concentrator syn-
dicate. His N.S.W. flotations are all in a bad way. The
Brown’s Creek Co.—a splendid plant lying idle on a mine
which has not worked for four years, and shows no sign
of re-starting. The D’Arcy Estates Co. and D’Arcy Went-
worth Gold Mine are amalgamated with the others
of the camp, and are passing through a period
of great tribulation. The Lagoon Creek Gold Mine,
in liquidation, proved a duffer from the start; Tam-
baroora Creek gold-mines also in liquidation, and another
duffer. Frank was a clog-dancer years ago 111 America,
and came to Australia as manager for his wife, Carrie
Swain. Made his first mining hit at Broken Hill by
buying a parcel of Central shares at a few shillings each
and selling them at as many pounds. In this he acted on
the advice of Thomas, the manager, and not on his own
judgment. Subsequently took a hand in the flotation of
the Wentworth mine, made a good deal of money out of
it and then lost it in the same property. Made a heap of
money in Rand stocks, and subsequently associated him-
self with Zeb. Lane in the flotation of the Boulder
Perseverance and other mines. His faith in Perseverance
shares has much exceeded his discretion, he having, by
his own admission, been potted with 17,000 of them at an
average of £ll each. He is now winding-up the British-
Westralia Syndicate. It is really hard to think that the
pallid Mr. Gardner can still be the holder of a lot of
money made out of that list. His excuse for winding-up
the B.W. Syndicate is that his health will not permit his
attention to it; at the same time he admits holding about
nine-tenths of the shares.
My First Love.
A BUSH ROMANCE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At that time I had heard muoh about love, but
had never felt it. I had some idea that, when
that sublime feeling struck people, they had to go
up into a high mountain and write poetry till they
got used to it. But when I fell in love with Mary,
I didn’t think about mountains—no, I just felt as
if I would like to take her in my arms, and, and
—anyhow, I didn’t.
When I came to Wambo I had a little money ;
when I left I had none. Old Francis offered me
a job; so I went to work for old Francis. I had
heard rumors about cattle-duffing, wrong earmarks,
and so on; but when I saw Mary, I said they
were darned lies.
After I had been there three weeks I hoped
Mary was getting a bit fond of me, but I wasn’t
sure. And, this particular morning, Mary was
angry. The grindstone was kept in the barn, and,
when I used to grind my axe in the morning,
Mary somehow used to be there grinding corn for
the fowls. I forget what I did, but I remember
what she said. She said :
“ What do you mean, Tom ? How dare you put
your arm round my waist 1 You men are always
the same when you get a girl by herself.” I saw
the tears in her eyes; I had never seen a girl
crying before. It was awful. #
“Oh! Mary, forgive me!” I blurted out. “I
couldn’t help it, Mary. There is no foolery about
me ; I’m in earnest, and I am so fond of you.”
“Well, Tom, that makes a difference, but—Oh,
now ! There you are again. Let me go at onoe!
You mustn’t kiss me like that, or I’ll tell father
about you.”
I got wild, grabbed my axe, and walked off.
“ I won’t tell this time, Tom,” she sang out; but
I was deaf.
That was just like Mary, I reflected ; one time
my heart was in my boots, another time I would
be dreaming all about Don Juan in Byron’s
poetry. Then I met the old man. “ Hullo, Tom !
seen Mary about?” “Yes,” said I, “she’s in
the barn.” “Oh! it takes her a darned long time
to grind that corn. Come home early, Tom ! I
want a hand to kill a bullock.”
♦ » * •
We had just about finished skinning the
bullock ; and Mary was there helping. Suddenly
old Francis looked up and whistled. “By God,
trapped ! Here’s the trooper ! ”
He rode up on a bright bay horse, and grinned
at us. “Good day, Francis ! Nice bit of meat
you’ve got there. Mind letting us see the brand.”
“ It’s no use fooling round,” said the old man ;
“this beast is not one of mine. I shot him by
mistake. I’m going to send word to Laird and
pay him for it.”
“ Yes, that’s all right, Ned ! I’ll see that Laird
knows all about it. And just now you’d better
come along with me. You too, my girl! Get
ready for town ! Seems you’re in the swim—and
you?” He looked at me hard.
“Oh,” said I, “I’m working by the week
fencing ; I know nothing about this affair.”
“Yes,” said old Francis, “ that’s right; he only
did what I told him. There’s no use mixing him
up in it. He’s only a swaggie.”
“ H’m,” said the fellow; “ you had better hang
round a bit, though. You might be wanted.”
I took Mary aside. “ Mary, my darling, if I
get you out of this mess, will you marry me ? ”
“ No use, Tom ; it’s all up this trip.”
“But,” I said, “ will you promise ; that’s all.”
And she promised.
» * * *
It was four miles to town; I was there in half-
an-hour by the short cut, asking for Laird. Pre-
sently I found him.
“ Are you Mr. Laird ? ”
“ Yes, that’s me. What do you want ? ”
“ Well, we’ve been and shot one of your cattle
by mistake, and want to straighten things up.
You know the beast; he was by himself; white
face, white hind-feet.”
“ Yes ; I know him. Fat, too, isn’t he ? ”
“Yes, middlin’fat.”
“And who the blazes are you?” said Laird,
looking at me, suspiciously.
“I’m tank-sinking on Toolba. Your beast got
mixed with the killing-cattle, and was shot. How
much do you want for him ? ”
“ Well; ten pounds is what I want for that bit
of meat! ”
“ Can’t do it. Give you eight.”
“ Not a darned stiver less than ten quid,” said
Laird. And I all the time in a funk lest the
trooper should trot up. So I said, “Right you
are ! here’s the cash. Give me the brand and re-
ceipt.”
Mary was saved.
Laird was furious when he found-out, and the
trooper persisted in bringing the case before the
court. He got snubbed. Old Francis produced
my receipt, and the Bench said a man could kill
any beast he liked if he could prove it was his own
in a court of law.
The trooper was sold.
That afternoon I was outside the pub., a little
bit sprung. Up came the trooper. “Hullo, my
lad ! that was a smart trick of yours. I suppose
Mary will pay you for that.” I knew what he
meant. A vision of Mary’s beautiful auburn hair
rose before me. People said it was redbut it
wasn’t. I jumped three feet in the air, and
grabbed that trap by the leg. He was off his
horse in a jiffy. I had things pretty well my own
way for a while. He thought I was in fun.
But he got tired after a bit, grabbed me by the
back of the neck, and marched me off to the lock-
up. I felt I was a hero. I had fought for Mary ;
bled for her; and got jugged for her. Next day
it was “ Two pounds or fourteen days.”
I paid the fine. That was twelve pounds for
Mary. _
“Been having a lively time?” said the pub-
lican, when I came out. “Yes, I have!”
“Stranger hereabouts?” “Yes.” “Thought
so. Say ! here now, best let that girl slip ! Now,
don’t flare up ! wait till I tell you. ”
“ What do you mean ? ” I said, getting hot.
“ I suppose you haven’t heard about the—er—
kiddie, have you? No? I thought not. Oh,
yes ; its daddie lives in town here. You see, it’s
this way ; you never know what happens to them
quiet sort of girls.”
I left Womba with a blighted heart, and an
empty pocket. I also took away a broken nose I
got from the bobby. I hate red-headed girls;
though I thought all the time there was some-
thing wrong about that love, because I didn’t have
the proper feeling for writing poetry or anything
like that. lam still single.

The Bald Brigade.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now this is a song of the Bald Brigade
That carries a gold-knobbed stick,
And gathers at halls of the leg-show grade
Where Totties spring forth and kick.
Their smooth pates shine like globes of glass;
They 101 l in a paunchy row,
Whilst the gods in the gallery watch things pass
In the glistening skulls below !
The old boys snigger in glumptious glee
When the frisky girls appear;
At the gladsome glimpse of a shapely knee
They snigger from ear to ear.
When the floating billows of muslin rise
They cackle Ha ! ha ! Ho ! ho !
And the gods in the gallery fix their eyes
On the glistening skulls below !
Warmed by the vision of wanton feet
Defying Dame Grundy’s laws,
The old hearts pulse with a quicker beat ;
But e’en as they clap their paws
Those veterans sigh for departed days—
They had wild oats then to sow,
And they sowed them thick, that the gods might
gaze
On the glistening skulls below !
The bats they strewed in their lines of life
Brought plentiful crops of care,
Worry and trouble ’twixt man and wife—
But never brought crops of hair.
They sowed more oats on the grave of youth,
They gathered dyspeptic woe ;
And their sins lie bare in the naked truth
Of their glistening skulls below.
The old eyes ogle each charming girl
When her slipper in mid-air floats,
And her shielding draperies swish and swirl
To the music’s maddening notes ;
But old eyes, dim with the film of time,
See naught save a pink leg-show:
’Tis the gods ! the gods get a thrill sublime,
By the grace of the skulls below !
High-kickers are not in the least concerned
By the leers of the Bald-heads “ blue,”
But little they dream of their charms up-turned
To the gallery’s bird’s-eye view.
From the red lips, curved in a smile serene,
Hot, sulphurous words would flow,
Did the dancers guess what the gods have seen
In the glistening skulls below !
Ah ! sad, sad day for the gods ’twill be
When the bright reflectors fade,
When the “ five-bob ” seats at the front are free
From the bucks of the Bald Brigade ;
When the faithful mirrors shall hide their lights
In the land where the dead men go,
And the gods of the gallery see no sights
In the glistening skulls below !
M.L.
THE RIP.
Paderewski.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What is a Great Player, a Great Pianist ? Every-
one in the world will give you a different descrip-
tion. Each to his own needs first, and, in propor-
tion as these are satisfied, so is the music-maker
great.
To me, the great one is the one who takes me
away from petty thoughts. The greatest is the
one who absolutely forbids or prevents me from
thinking at all.
Why not judge of him that way as well as any
other, since music for ever leads Nowhere ? It
opens all gates, unlocks all doors, gives a wild,
fleeting revelation of Everything, then turns you
out and leaves you there, alone in darkness, look-
ing towards all things, unable to reach any.
Here he comes, the yellow-haired, the sleek-
eyed man with the vague face, that looks as if
carven carefully out of a dream.
He seats himself, and his yellow head stands
out against the black gown of a woman in the row
behind the piano. His eye and nose are sil-
houetted too, the full-drooped lid of the artist,
■"6 nose that is at once sensitive and generous
And the yellowy moustache and chin-tuft hiding
a mouth that is compressed a little, and is graven
about with fine pain—this, then, is Paderewski.
So we see him, looking at him for the first time,
a vague, yellow face in yellow hair; gentle, but
carrying somewhere between the narrowing lids a
hint of tiger-fire.
He plays. Hush ! hush ! He plays softly to
quieten the house of women. A chord is struck
in warning, then a run floats across the hall
hushing all away.
What is he playing? What does it matter?
Name it or not, it is you who give it its meaning.
Well, then, since you d-mand, I will tell you he
is playing Etudes Symphoniques—Schumann’s.
Paderewski and Schumann, they chase the great
idea up and down, in and out of Erard, and a
faint voice from within whispers that he lacks in
some subtle shade of fineness, making his tran-
sitions too marked for such a poet.
For such a poet! Ay, there’s the rub. For
the poet that he is, is he quite poet enough ?
And then he touches the piano with two “ Songs
Without Words,” slowly,- very, very slowly
lagging, dreaming. ’
Will Beethoven wake him ?
But that yellow vagueness is not for Beethoven.
Music breathing and thundering out the highest
metaphysics, the essence of spiritual fervours, the
message of a giant brain, these are not for that
faced carved out of a dream.
Give him Schubert, Liszt—give him Chopin—
and the king* comes into his own.
He takes the piano in his hand like a brush and
paints on the flexible molten brain of his audience
a storm, a torture, the “Hebe’s kind,” “The
Erl Konig.” He dashes it in with a passionless
force ill the house flees with him in the wild
flight—but leaving its body here behind it in St.
James s Hall, with the rain beating on the roof—
yes, always leaving its body here behind it. He
takes the soul, but leaves the body.
And then Chopin—a Ballade, two Preludes,
etudes, a mazurka, a polonaise. None of them of
the too-hackneyed kind that every school-girl
murders. They float, they float, they caress the
The yellow hair gets in its work, and the tiger-
fire flashes, with the strange, indifferent passion
of a poet. His runs are like streaks of silver
flung across the spirit.
Runs ! Oh, hideous word ! The crudeness and
clumsiness of the English language can show no
uglier example than that word Runs. Runs!-
*° n £« e of flame leaping across the piano !
T?TTTV«7 f 0 / t *£ t^ ash °, f . s P ra y in the sunlight !
RUNS. for that long, thin streak of lightning that
flashes across the heart, firing the inner eye with
silver, stealing the breath, leaving one dizzy
dazed, and thrilled ! J
. In Paderewski’s hands a run is like an “Oh ' ”
m some moment of sudden spiritual exaltation.
Just as the poet cries “Oh, God!” “Oh, my
Rove ! J aderewski sends up these silver cries.
It is hours since I heard him, but I see the color
of his runs still. Silver—always silver.
Until I heard him play, I never heard the
message of a cascade of notes. I saw it as an
ornament, and turned aside a little. With the
unnecessary cruelty of the ignorant I called it
vulgar, and drew away, admiring, but never
knowing, never taking it to myself.
Till Paderewski played those Preludes. Then
1 was punished, and all my days I’ll go the
sottiier, sadlier, for my crime’s sake.
The girl next to me said : “ I don’t like the way
he lifts his hands. It’s so affected /”
But fought of the runs, and I said to myself:
Tor this vague man with the mouth of pain
there is some hidden meaning even to those hands
thrown back, held back, posed back, theatrically.”
And I found the meaning after a time. His
pose of hand is the expression of his soul, his
brain, his condition of emotion. Sometimes his
hand flies up, and remains, dead still, the fingers
bent. It stays like that for a full moment. My
girl says “Affectation.” It seems along, long
time before it comes down again. If you don’t
know what it means you grow a little uneasy,
overstrained. But if you understand, if you watch
ms eyes, his lips, listen to his music, and get into
his region, you will find he is venting himself in
those raised hands, just as you bite your lip in
sudden danger* just as you open your eye in great
surprise, just as you lift your hands at some dread
message. Those taut raised hands are the ex-
pression of his soul.
But great— is Paderewski great ?
Not to a craving, exacting imagination that
demands fire and dew together, that cries for the
Heavens to be opened and solid earth to be swept
from beneath its feet. He plays for ever in a
dream. He translates Chopin inimitably, with
perfect grace, with despair, with a lovely charm
and a veiled passion, but the homely flower-feel-
in £i 4 the domestic pure love in Mendelssohn’s
music, was blurred with a sentimentality that had
551 pl ace there. He played those two “Songs
Without Words ” with a drawn-out tenderness
that grew thin from very slowness. And in a
Beethoven. Sonata” (op. Ill) the struggle between
Paderewski and the greatest musical genius of all
time was to the strong. Beethoven conquered:
the player was left behind. He has neither the
grandeur of style nor mind for this immortal.
Give him the songs he knows to sing and
Paderewski is superb. But call him Padereffski,
or you are not worthy to listen to his dreamings.
They say he plays to Women. Well, but what
then ! So do all artists, everyone who plays to an
audience—the poet, the actor, the artist, the
writer, the pianist, the singer—it’s to women he
is appealing, or to the woman in a man.
London, July.
LOUISE MACK.
At Whangarei (M.L.), recently, the skip-
per of a coastal steamer was prosecuted for allow-
ing drink to be sold aboard his boat. The beak
decided that, as the steward sold the drink, the
captain wasn’t liable. A week or two later, the
police found that drink was still being sold aboard.
A new P.M. had meanwhile come to the district,
and he decided that, as the skipper was in charge
of the boat, the steward wasn’t liable.
The second reading of the Victorian Bill
providing for the election of a Convention to draft
a new State Constitution was passed by only four
majority—47 for and 43 against. Four paired, so,
with the Speaker, every man in the Assembly was
accounted for. The Tories were against the Bill
because they hate the referendum principle in
every shape, and there is a suggestion of the
referendum in this idea ; also, because the measure
threatens their beloved House of 48 Rich Land
lords. And many who secretly approve the prin-
ciple voted against it because their seats are
insecure, and tliey are likely to be among the Lost
when the Convention cuts down the size of the
Assembly. Ex-Premier and one-time Democrat
Shiels was among the Noes—but then Shiels,
while a good theoretical Democrat, has always fled
in affright from his own ideas when he got close
to them.
Writes “ Fair Dinkum ” :
Barnum-Powell’s profusely-advertised Defence of Mafe-
king was a theatrical fraud, no doubt about that, but it
was a success in so far that it gulled more people than the
British Jingoes who waved toy flags and got drunk. Col.
Plumer took the imposture seriously, which fact is ad-
mitted by the loudest denunciators of Barnum’s bunkum
policy. And in deceiving Plumer, his nearest British
military neighbour, Barnum deceived the whole country.
The moral effect of the “gallant defence ” was the same
to the hesitating Cape Dutch as though it had been
genuinely heroic. It served a British purpose for months.
Finally it resulted in the capture of about 200 Boers,
under Eloff, the “ toffy” grandson of Kruger, who were
trying to capture the supposed starving garrison before
the arrival of a relief force. Not until after the “ relief”
of Mafeking did Barnum-Powell prove himself to be the
tinnest of tin gods. Had he shown only a little of the
energy and generalship that his own carefully-spread
fictions had credited him with he would have captured
De Wet and a large force of Boers whom the ever-vigilant
Methuen managed to drive into a very tight place. Ask
the returned Bushmen to name the most over-rated and
under-rated of the well-known British commanders in
S’Africa, and they will answer Baden-Powell and Cord
Methuen every time.
Writes “E.C.8.” from London :
Re English payments to Australian writers and your
par. about an experience with Boys of the Empire (9/3/’01).
The facts are these—The article in question contained,
not 2000 words, but 1273 words. The standard rate of
payment with such publications in London is £1 per
1000 words—in many cases it is less. Therefore the
writer got what he asked for—“ the standard rate
of payment." From this sovereign it appears that
he paid his typewriter a charge of 4s. That, of
course has nothing to do with B. of the E. ; but one
must pause to admire the munificence of an author who
pays his typewriter 4s. for 1273 words (the London price
is from 9d. to Is. per 1000). Then comes the justified
complaint that payment waajiiade by a cheque on which
heavy exchange was charged, and that the covering en-
velope was insufficiently stamped. That was grossly in-
considerate (by the way, is the exchange on London
cheques in Sydney as high as 2s. 6d. in the £7) Yet some
allowance may be made for a paper just started and un-
familiar with Australian business. As an Australian
battling in journalistic London I must speak as I find. I
have occasionally contributed to B. of the E., and re-
ceived fair and prompt payment; I could name a dozen
London papers of which I cannot say so much.
